The synthesis of *fa* (law), *shi* (power), and *shu* (technique) that Han Fei developed provided a comprehensive theory of governance that was both intellectually compelling and practically effective. It offered a vision of absolute rule in which the ruler was guided not by moral considerations but by rational calculation of what was necessary to maintain power and strengthen the state.

Han Fei famously traveled to Qin to present his ideas to the king of Qin—likely Ying Zheng himself—impressed by the *Han Feizi* text. However, according to traditional accounts, Han Fei's fellow student Li Si, who served as chancellor in Qin, became jealous of Han Fei's influence and convinced the king to have him executed. Whether or not this story is accurate, Han Fei's ideas had a profound impact on Qin governance and continued to influence Chinese political thought for centuries.

## The Theoretical Basis of Qin Rule

The Legalist philosophy that Shang Yang implemented and Han Fei theorized provided the theoretical foundation for the distinctive system of Qin governance. This system was characterized by several key principles that distinguished it from the governance traditions of other Warring States.

First and foremost was the principle of legal supremacy. In the Qin system, law was the supreme authority, superior to any individual—including the ruler himself in his personal capacity. The *Shiji* records that Shang Yang argued that "there is no one above the law" (Graham, *Disputers of the Tao*, 218). This principle was reinforced by the practice of posting the legal code publicly, so that all subjects could know what was expected of them. The transparency of law was meant to ensure that punishment was not arbitrary but followed from clear, publicly known rules.

Second was the principle of centralized authority. Unlike the feudal system that had characterized the Zhou dynasty, in which power was distributed among semi-autonomous nobles, the Qin system concentrated all authority in the hands of the ruler. Local officials were appointed by the central government and accountable to it; hereditary nobles were stripped of their independent power. This centralization allowed the ruler to mobilize resources and coordinate action on a scale impossible for more decentralized systems.

Third was the principle of merit-based advancement. In the Qin system, positions of authority were awarded on the basis of performance rather than birth. This principle, which was expressed in the practice of *anji* (按迹)—evaluating officials by their achievements—created a system of competitive advancement that attracted talented individuals from throughout the Chinese world. It also broke the power of the old aristocratic families, who had previously dominated government positions by virtue of their lineage.

Fourth was the principle of harsh punishment. The Qin legal system was famous for its severity, prescribing brutal punishments for a wide range of offenses. The *Shiji* records that the Qin code included punishments such as death by slicing, death by burning, death by drowning, and various forms of mutilation. Even minor offenses could result in severe penalties, including hard labor, military service on the frontier, or enslavement. This severity was justified on the grounds that it deterred crime and maintained order.

Fifth was the principle of state control over the economy. The Qin government maintained extensive control over economic activity, including the regulation of agriculture, commerce, and manufacturing. The goal was to ensure that all economic activity served the needs of the state, particularly its military capacity. This included the standardization of weights and measures, the regulation of trade, and the monopolization of certain key industries.

## The Implementation of Legalist Governance

The Legalist principles were not merely theoretical; they were implemented through a comprehensive system of administration that touched every aspect of Qin society. The state was divided into administrative units—commanderies, counties, and districts—each governed by appointed officials who were accountable to the central government. These officials were responsible for collecting taxes, maintaining order, recruiting soldiers, and implementing the ruler's policies.

The system of local administration was supported by an extensive network of spies and informants, who reported on the conduct of officials and subjects alike. This network, which was justified as necessary for maintaining law and order, created a climate of suspicion and surveillance that pervaded Qin society. The Legalists believed that this surveillance was necessary to prevent corruption and ensure compliance with the law.

The military organization of Qin reflected the same principles. Soldiers were organized into units commanded by appointed officers, subject to strict discipline and harsh punishments for failure. The system of rewards and punishments was applied rigorously: soldiers who performed well were rewarded with land, titles, and other benefits; those who failed or deserted were punished severely, often along with their families. This system created a highly motivated and disciplined fighting force.

The Legalist system also had significant implications for social organization. The collective responsibility system (*baozhi*) created intense pressure toward conformity, as individuals were held accountable for the actions of their neighbors. The restriction on movement made it difficult for people to escape the surveillance of local officials. The emphasis on agriculture over commerce and the professions created a relatively static social order in which most people were farmers subject to the state.

## The Reception and Legacy of Qin Legalism

The Legalist system that Qin implemented was controversial both in its own time and in subsequent history. Critics from other philosophical schools condemned the harshness of Qin governance and the rejection of moral considerations in favor of pure expediency. Mencius had warned that a ruler who governed through fear rather than virtue would eventually lose his throne, and many later commentators saw the Qin dynasty's rapid collapse as confirmation of this warning.

The Confucian historian Sima Qian, writing in the Han dynasty, presented a complex assessment of Shang Yang and the Legalist tradition. While he acknowledged the practical effectiveness of Legalist reforms, he also criticized their moral emptiness and the suffering they inflicted. The *Shiji* includes both praise for Shang Yang's achievements and sympathy for the victims of his harsh policies.

In the long term, the Legalist approach to governance was never entirely abandoned in Chinese history, even as Confucianism became the dominant ideology. Successive dynasties employed Legalist techniques of administration, even while officially endorsing Confucian values. The tension between Legalist efficiency and Confucian moralism became a recurring theme in Chinese political thought.

For Qin itself, the Legalist system provided the foundation for unprecedented military success. The discipline, organization, and mobilizing capacity that Legalism enabled allowed Qin to defeat the other Warring States and unify the Chinese world. Yet the same qualities also generated the resentment and resistance that contributed to the dynasty's rapid collapse. The Legalist system, while effective for conquest, proved less sustainable for long-term governance.

## Scholarly Debates on Legalism

Modern scholars have offered various interpretations of Legalism and its role in Qin's rise and fall. Some have emphasized the historical context of Legalism, arguing that it represented a pragmatic response to the conditions of the Warring States period—the need for strong states that could mobilize resources for warfare and maintain order in an era of chaos. In this view, Legalism was not a coherent ideology but a collection of practical measures that happened to be effective.

Others have emphasized the philosophical dimensions of Legalism, treating it as a serious intellectual tradition that offered a genuine alternative to Confucianism. This approach takes the Legalist texts at their word, examining their arguments on their own terms rather than dismissing them as mere propaganda or opportunism.

Still others have focused on the social and economic dimensions of Legalist reform, examining how the transformation of land tenure, taxation, and social organization affected different groups in Qin society. This approach tends to emphasize the costs of Legalist governance—the suffering inflicted on peasants, artisans, and other groups who bore the burden of state extraction.

The relationship between Legalism and the subsequent development of Chinese empire has also been debated. Some scholars argue that the Qin model established patterns that persisted throughout Chinese imperial history—the centralized state, the meritocratic bureaucracy, the emphasis on law and punishment. Others argue that the Han dynasty, which succeeded Qin, fundamentally transformed the Qin model by incorporating Confucian values and institutions.

What is clear is that Legalism played a crucial role in the emergence of the first Chinese empire. The philosophical framework that Shang Yang implemented and Han Fei articulated provided the intellectual foundations for the unprecedented concentration of power that Qin Shi Huang achieved. Whether one views this as a achievement or a tragedy—as the necessary consolidation of a fractious world or the birth of a totalitarian system—it fundamentally shaped the course of Chinese history.

### Chapter Notes

The traditional account of Shang Yang's reforms is derived primarily from the *Book of Lord Shang* (*Shangjun Shu*) and from Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian*. Modern scholarship has examined these accounts critically, noting that both sources were written after Shang Yang's death and reflect particular ideological perspectives. The *Book of Lord Shang* is generally considered to be a composite text, with some sections possibly written by Shang Yang himself and others added later.

The date of Shang Yang's arrival in Qin and the timing of his reforms have been debated by modern scholars. Some scholars, following the traditional chronology, place his major reforms in the 350s and 340s BCE; others have suggested that some reforms may have occurred earlier or later. The traditional account in the *Shiji* provides specific dates, but these may not be entirely reliable.

Han Fei's relationship with the court of Qin is also subject to scholarly debate. The traditional account, preserved in the *Shiji*, describes Han Fei's visit to Qin and his execution at the instigation of Li Si. Some scholars have questioned whether this account is accurate, suggesting that it may reflect later Han dynasty efforts to discredit the Legalist tradition.

The philosophical classification of Legalism as a distinct "school" of thought is a modern convention. In the Warring States period, the boundaries between philosophical schools were more fluid, and many thinkers drew on multiple traditions. Nevertheless, the Legalist emphasis on law, punishment, and centralized authority does represent a distinctive approach that merits separate treatment.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- *The Book of Lord Shang* (*Shangjun Shu*), trans. J.J.L. Duyvendak (London: Arthur Probsthain, 1928). The primary text attributed to Shang Yang.
- Han Fei, *Han Feizi*, trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1964). The collected works of Han Fei.
- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The standard historical source for Shang Yang and Qin history.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Graham, A.C., *Disputers of the Tao: Philosophical Argument in Ancient China* (La Salle: Open Court, 1989). Excellent overview of Warring States philosophy, including detailed treatment of Legalism.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Modern scholarly analysis of Qin's political system.
- Rickett, W. Allyn, *Guiguzi: China's First Political Psychologist* (Hong Kong: Chinese University Press, 2001). Analysis of early Legalist thought.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Contextual analysis of Warring States political developments.
- Goldin, Paul R., *The Culture of Sex in Ancient China* (Honolulu: University of Hawai'i Press, 2002). Discussion of Legalist views on human nature.
- Kern, Martin, "The Philosophy of Law," in *Birth in an Age of Sacrifice* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2009). Scholarly analysis of Legalist philosophical foundations.


---


# Chapter 8: The Great Powers of the Warring States

The period known as the Warring States (战国时代) represents the final and most intense phase of the political fragmentation that characterized the Eastern Zhou dynasty. Beginning around 475 BCE with the collapse of the Spring and Autumn order, this era witnessed the emergence of seven major powers that dominated the political landscape of the Chinese world until the unification under Qin in 221 BCE. Understanding these states—their distinct political cultures, military traditions, diplomatic strategies, and internal dynamics—is essential to comprehending how Qin was able to achieve supremacy and what obstacles it overcame in the process of unification.

The seven Warring States—Qi (齐), Chu (楚), Yan (燕), Wei (魏), Zhao (赵), Han (韩), and Qin (秦)—represented dramatically different approaches to governance, warfare, and diplomacy. Each had developed its own strengths and weaknesses, its own cultural identity and political traditions. The interactions among these states, shaped by the competing strategies of alliance and confrontation, defined the diplomatic landscape that Qin would navigate on its path to empire.

## The Evolution of the Warring States System

The Warring States period emerged from the collapse of the Spring and春秋 order, in which a weakened Zhou king was supported by a system of vassal states led by a hegemonic power. As the Zhou authority declined further, the vassal states that had formerly acknowledged the king's supremacy began to pursue their own interests with increasing independence. By the fifth century BCE, the political map of China had been redrawn around a handful of large states that competed for dominance without any effective central authority.

The early Warring States period was characterized by a process of consolidation and expansion, as larger states absorbed smaller ones and extended their control over new territories. This process reduced the number of independent states from dozens to seven major powers plus several smaller states that survived as satellites or buffer zones. Each of the seven major states had its own capital, its own administration, its own army, and its own diplomatic relationships.

The period from roughly 350 BCE to 221 BCE saw the emergence of a distinctive balance of power, in which the seven states engaged in complex diplomatic maneuvers designed to prevent any single state from achieving overwhelming dominance. This balance was maintained through a combination of military deterrence, strategic alliances, and the careful manipulation of relationships among the major powers.

The geopolitical center of the Warring States world was the Central Plains (中原), the fertile river valleys of the Yellow River region that had been the heartland of Zhou civilization. The states that controlled this region—Wei, Zhao, Han, and later Qi—were often considered more culturally advanced than the peripheral states, though this assessment was contested. The peripheral states—Chu in the south, Yan in the north, and Qin in the west—were often dismissed as cultural inferiors by the Central Plains states, though they possessed significant military and economic strength.

## Qi: The Eastern Power

Qi (齐) occupied the Shandong Peninsula in eastern China, a region of fertile farmland, developed commerce, and access to the sea. The state had been a major power since the Spring and Autumn period, when it served as one of the hegemonic states that dominated the Zhou political system. By the Warring States period, Qi had developed a distinctive political culture that combined elements of Confucian tradition with practical governance.

The Qi rulers adopted the title of king (王) early, asserting their status as independent sovereigns rather than vassals of the Zhou. The Qi capital at Linzi (临淄) was one of the largest and most prosperous cities of the Warring States world, renowned for its markets and cultural life. The *Guoyu* and *Zuo Zhuan* records describe Qi as a center of philosophical debate and artistic refinement.

The military tradition of Qi emphasized the use of infantry and chariots, reflecting the terrain of the Central Plains where the state primarily operated. Qi generals were known for their tactical skill, and the Qi army was generally considered one of the strongest in the Warring States period. However, Qi was also vulnerable to naval attack from the states of the eastern coast and faced pressure from Qi on its western border.

Qi's diplomatic strategy was often characterized by a degree of caution and pragmatism. The state was slow to engage in the most intense phases of conflict and sometimes preferred to negotiate from a position of neutrality rather than commit to decisive action. This tendency toward caution, while it preserved Qi from the most destructive conflicts, also limited its ability to expand and eventually left it vulnerable to Qin's diplomatic manipulation.

A significant development in Qi's history was the reforms of King Wei of Qi (齐威王), who ruled from 356 to 320 BCE. King Wei implemented administrative reforms that strengthened central control and improved the efficiency of governance. He was also famous for his willingness to listen to honest counsel, including the famous episode in which he rewarded the minister Zou Ji (邹忌) for a candid assessment of the king's weaknesses. These reforms made Qi a more effective state, though they did not transform it as fundamentally as the Legalist reforms transformed Qin.

## Chu: The Southern Giant

Chu (楚) was the largest of the Warring States in terms of territory, controlling the vast region south of the Yangtze River. This territory included rich agricultural lands, extensive forests, and access to the mineral resources of the south. Chu's position on the southern periphery of the Chinese world gave it a distinctive cultural identity that set it apart from the Central Plains states.

The Chu state traced its origins to a confederation of tribal groups in the south, and its rulers claimed descent from the mythical Yan Emperor (炎帝) rather than the Yellow Emperor (黄帝) who was the ancestor of the Central Plains ruling houses. This different genealogical claim was one expression of Chu's distinct identity, which was also reflected in its artistic traditions, religious practices, and political institutions.

The political culture of Chu was characterized by a degree of pluralism that distinguished it from the more centralized states of the Central Plains. The Chu ruler governed through a network of local lords and tribal leaders who retained significant autonomy, and the central government was often weaker than in states like Qin. This decentralized structure gave Chu a degree of resilience—revolts or losses in one region did not immediately threaten the whole state—but it also limited the state's ability to mobilize resources for sustained military campaigns.

Chu's military forces reflected its geographic and cultural context. The armies of Chu included large numbers of infantry, and the state was known for its use of archers and its navy, which operated on the Yangtze River and its tributaries. The Chu military was generally capable but sometimes suffered from the same organizational weaknesses that affected the state's administration.

Chu was a persistent challenger for hegemony in the Warring States period, engaging in frequent conflicts with the Central Plains states. However, Chu also faced challenges from within, including the problem of integrating conquered populations into the Chu political system. The state's size and diversity, while a source of potential strength, also created management challenges that limited its effectiveness.

## Yan: The Northern Frontier

Yan (燕) occupied the northern region around modern-day Beijing and Hebei, controlling a territory that extended to the borders of the nomadic peoples of the steppes. This frontier position gave Yan a distinctive character and shaped its political priorities, which often focused on defense rather than expansion.

The Yan state traced its origins to the early Zhou period, when a member of the Zhou royal house was granted a fief in the north. However, Yan was long considered a marginal state, peripheral to the major centers of Chinese civilization. The *Zuo Zhuan* and other early sources describe Yan as a relatively weak state that was often threatened by more powerful neighbors.

The political culture of Yan reflected its frontier position. The state was known for its military preparedness and its vigilance against the nomadic peoples who threatened the northern border. Yan rulers invested heavily in fortifications and maintained a standing army that was prepared for both external attack and internal defense.

The relationship between Yan and the nomadic peoples of the northern frontier was complex and sometimes involved periods of conflict as well as cooperation. Some scholars have suggested that the Yan developed techniques of cavalry warfare that were influenced by contact with nomadic traditions, though the evidence for this is contested. Whatever the precise nature of this influence, Yan's frontier position clearly shaped its military practices.

Yan's diplomatic position in the Warring States system was often difficult. The state was surrounded by more powerful neighbors—Qi to the south, Zhao to the west, and various nomadic groups to the north—and had to navigate carefully to maintain its independence. Yan's typical strategy was to seek alliances with one powerful neighbor against another, playing off the competing interests of the major states.

One notable episode in Yan history was the alliance with Zhao against Qi, which resulted in the recovery of territory that Yan had lost. Another significant event was the failed attempt by the Yan general Qin Kai (秦开) to invade Qi in the early third century BCE, which ended in disaster and weakened Yan significantly. These episodes illustrate both Yan's ambitions and its limitations as a major power.

## Wei: The Central Power

Wei (魏) was one of the most important states of the Warring States period, occupying a central position in the fertile Central Plains. Wei controlled the region around the Yellow River, including the old Zhou capitals, and was considered one of the most culturally advanced states. Its capital at Daliang (大梁) was a major center of commerce and intellectual life.

The Wei state emerged from the division of the former Jin state in the fifth century BCE and quickly established itself as a major power. The early Wei rulers, particularly King Hui (惠王), who ruled from 370 to 319 BCE, pursued aggressive policies of expansion that brought Wei into conflict with its neighbors. At its height, Wei was arguably the most powerful of the Warring States.

The military tradition of Wei was sophisticated and innovative. The state was known for the quality of its infantry, organized in formations that emphasized discipline and coordination. Wei also developed the "flexible tactics" (太极拳) that would later be adopted by other states, reflecting a sophisticated approach to warfare that sought to combine offensive and defensive capabilities.

The political culture of Wei was characterized by a tradition of strong central government and effective administration. The state employed a system of merit-based advancement that attracted talented officials from throughout the Chinese world, and its bureaucracy was generally considered efficient. The *Wenzi* and other texts describe Wei as a model of good governance.

However, Wei's central position also made it vulnerable to attack from multiple directions. The state was surrounded by powerful neighbors—Qin to the west, Zhao to the north, Han to the south, and Qi to the east—and had to fight wars on multiple fronts. This strategic vulnerability was a persistent problem for Wei and ultimately contributed to its decline.

The decline of Wei began in the mid-fourth century BCE, when Qin defeated Wei in a series of battles that culminated in the Battle of Maling (马陵之战) in 341 BCE. This defeat, which involved the famous stratagem of feigning retreat, cost Wei heavily and marked the beginning of Qin's ascendancy. After this point, Wei was increasingly forced onto the defensive and eventually became a subordinate of Qin.

## Zhao: The Northern Warrior

Zhao (赵) emerged from the same division of Jin as Wei but developed a distinct character shaped by its northern position and its frequent conflicts with nomadic peoples. The Zhao state occupied the territory around modern Shanxi, extending to the borders of the steppes. This frontier position gave Zhao a martial character that distinguished it from the more culturally oriented Central Plains states.

The military forces of Zhao were among the most formidable in the Warring States period. The state was famous for its cavalry, which was developed through contact with the nomadic peoples of the northern frontier. The Zhao cavalry, organized and trained along lines influenced by steppe practices, gave Zhao a military capability that few other states could match. The legendary general Lian Po (廉颇) and the even more famous Zhao Mu (赵武) were products of this martial tradition.

The political culture of Zhao was characterized by a degree of pragmatism and adaptability. The Zhao ruler King Wuling (武灵王), who ruled from 326 to 295 BCE, implemented reforms that adopted nomadic clothing and cavalry tactics, setting aside the ceremonial traditions of the Central Plains in favor of practical military effectiveness. This reform, which was controversial at the time, demonstrated Zhao's willingness to learn from other traditions.

Despite its military strength, Zhao faced significant challenges in the Warring States system. The state's position on the northern frontier meant that it had to maintain expensive defenses against nomadic incursions while also engaging in competition with its human neighbors. The famous general Bai Qi (白起), who would later become one of Qin's greatest commanders, was originally from Zhao but served Qin instead—a reflection of the talent flows that shaped the period.

Zhao's most famous military episode was the defense against Qin in the late third century BCE, when the state resisted Qin's attempts at conquest with remarkable determination. The Battle of Changping (长平之战) in 260 BCE, which resulted in a catastrophic defeat for Zhao and the death of 400,000 Zhao soldiers, marked the effective end of Zhao as a major power. The aftermath of this battle, in which Qin executed the surrendered Zhao soldiers, became one of the most infamous episodes of the Warring States period.

## Han: The Smallest of the Great Powers

Han (韩) was the smallest of the seven Warring States, occupying a relatively small territory in the Central Plains around modern-day Henan. Despite its limited size, Han was a significant power in the Warring States period, in part because of its strategic position at the crossroads of the major states.

The Han state emerged from the division of Jin alongside Wei and Zhao. Its capital at Xianyang (新郑) was a prosperous city, though not as large or influential as the capitals of the major states. Han's territory was fertile and well-developed, but it lacked the resources to compete with the larger states on equal terms.

The military forces of Han were limited by the state's small size, but Han compensated for this weakness through technological innovation and diplomatic skill. Han was known for the production of high-quality weapons, and the state was a center of metallurgical expertise. Han's crossbowmakers were particularly famous, and the state's weapons were sought after by other powers.

The diplomatic strategy of Han was typically characterized by flexibility and adaptation. As the smallest of the major states, Han had to be careful not to provoke stronger neighbors and often had to choose between alignment with one power or another. Han's position at the center of the Warring States system made it a valuable ally and a tempting target.

Han was the first of the Warring States to fall to Qin, in 230 BCE. The state's small size and strategic vulnerability made it difficult to defend, and Qin was able to conquer it relatively quickly once the time was right. The fall of Han marked the beginning of the final phase of Qin's conquests.

## The Balance of Power: Vertical and Horizontal Alliances

The diplomatic system of the Warring States was characterized by the competing strategies known as "vertical alliance" (合纵) and "horizontal alliance" (连横). These terms, which derive from the directional orientation of the alliances on maps of the period, described the two main diplomatic approaches that shaped the interactions among the major states.

The vertical alliance (合纵) referred to the strategy of forming a coalition of states to resist the dominance of a single powerful state. Typically, this meant the alliance of the eastern states—Qi, Zhao, Yan, and sometimes Chu—against Qin, which was the most powerful state in the west. The goal of the vertical alliance was to balance the power of Qin and prevent any single state from achieving hegemony.

The horizontal alliance (连横), by contrast, referred to the strategy of aligning with Qin, typically in exchange for territorial gains or other benefits. States that adopted this strategy hoped to benefit from Qin's strength while avoiding its attacks. The horizontal strategy was often associated with the diplomacy of individual states seeking to improve their position at the expense of their rivals.

These two strategies were not mutually exclusive, and states shifted between them depending on changing circumstances. The same state might pursue vertical alliances at one moment and horizontal alignments at another, depending on the threat environment and the opportunities available. The fluidity of the diplomatic system meant that alliances were constantly being formed, broken, and reformed.

The most famous practitioners of these diplomatic strategies were the itinerant diplomats known as "strategists" (策士), who traveled from state to state offering advice and arranging alliances. The most famous of these was probably Su Qin (苏秦), who advocated the vertical alliance against Qin and succeeded for a time in forming a coalition that constrained Qin's expansion. His counterpart Zhang Yi (张仪) advocated the horizontal strategy and worked to break up coalitions against Qin.

The balance of power that these alliances produced was inherently unstable, and it shifted over time as the relative strength of the major states changed. In the early Warring States period, Qi and Chu were among the most powerful states; by the later period, Qin had emerged as the dominant power. The collapse of the balance of power as Qin grew stronger eventually led to the unification of China under Qin rule.

## The Competition Among the Powers

The Warring States period was characterized by a constant competition among the major powers, driven by the logic of survival in a system where only the strongest states could expect to endure. This competition took many forms—military, diplomatic, economic, and cultural—and shaped the development of each state.

Military competition was the most visible form of the rivalry, as the Warring States engaged in almost constant warfare. The scale of these wars was often enormous, involving tens or even hundreds of thousands of soldiers. Battles could decide the fate of states, as the defeat at Changping demonstrated. The military competition drove innovations in tactics, weapons, and organization, as each state sought to gain an advantage over its rivals.

Diplomatic competition was equally important. The formation and breaking of alliances, the negotiation of treaties, and the manipulation of relationships among the major powers were all crucial to survival. The great diplomats of the period, like Su Qin and Zhang Yi, were among the most influential figures of the age, and their successes and failures shaped the course of history.

Economic competition was a quieter but nonetheless important dimension of the rivalry. States sought to develop their agricultural production, promote commerce, and accumulate wealth that could be used to support military efforts. The Legalist reforms in Qin were partly motivated by the desire to create a more efficient economic system that could support sustained warfare.

Cultural competition was also significant, as states sought to attract talented officials and scholars who could contribute to their strength. The great philosophical schools of the period—Confucian, Mohist, Daoist, Legalist, and others—traveled from state to state, offering their services to rulers who would employ them. The competition for talent was intense, and states that could attract and retain skilled officials gained significant advantages.

## The Road to Unification

The Warring States system was ultimately unsustainable, and the competition among the major powers was resolved by the emergence of Qin as the dominant state. This outcome was not inevitable; it resulted from a combination of Qin's distinctive strengths and the particular circumstances of the late Warring States period.

Qin's Legalist reforms gave it a level of organizational capacity that other states could not match. The centralized administration, the meritocratic bureaucracy, and the discipline of the Qin army gave it advantages that accumulated over time. While other states also implemented reforms, none did so as thoroughly as Qin.

The geographic position of Qin also contributed to its success. The state was protected by natural barriers in the west, which allowed it to focus its military efforts on the east. The fertile Guanzhong region provided the economic base to support sustained warfare, and the state had access to the resources of the frontier.

The diplomatic skill of Qin also played a crucial role. The policy of "allying with distant states while attacking nearby ones" (远交近攻) allowed Qin to isolate its immediate neighbors while building coalitions with more distant powers. This strategy systematically undermined the balance of power and paved the way for conquest.

When the time came for final conquest, Qin was able to defeat each of its rivals in turn, culminating in the unification of China in 221 BCE. The Warring States system had produced one survivor, and the era of fragmentation gave way to the age of empire.

### Chapter Notes

The traditional account of the Warring States period is derived primarily from Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* and from earlier historical works like the *Zuo Zhuan* and the *Guoyu*. These sources provide detailed narratives of the major events and personalities of the period, though modern scholars have noted that they were written from particular perspectives and may not always be accurate.

The concept of "vertical" and "horizontal" alliances is central to the understanding of Warring States diplomacy, but scholars have debated the precise meaning of these terms. Some have argued that the terms referred primarily to the geographic orientation of alliances, while others have suggested that they had more complex meanings related to the goals and strategies of the states involved.

The exact chronology of the Warring States period is sometimes debated, particularly regarding the transition from the Spring and Autumn period to the Warring States. The traditional date of 475 BCE, marking the beginning of the Warring States, corresponds to the death of the Zhou king and the effective end of the Spring and Autumn order, but some scholars have suggested alternative dating systems.

The relative strength of the Warring States shifted over time, and the balance of power was constantly in flux. The traditional narrative often emphasizes the rise of Qin and the decline of the other states, but this perspective can obscure the complexity of the period and the genuine strengths of the other powers.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary historical source for the Warring States period.
- *Zuo Zhuan*, trans. Stephen Durrant (Seattle: University of Washington Press, 2016). Early historical narrative covering the transition from Spring and Autumn to Warring States.
- *Guoyu* (*Discussions of the States*), various translations. Early source for diplomatic history of the period.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive overview of the period.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of Warring States society and politics.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Analysis of the Warring States political system.
- Beck, Mansvelt, *The Fall of the Han* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986). Discussion of the late Warring States period.
- Gernet, Jacques, *A History of Chinese Civilization* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1982). General overview with useful contextual material.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, "Warring States: Political History," in *The Shape of the Turtle* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2006). Detailed analysis of Warring States politics.


---


# Chapter 9: The Strategy of Conquest

The unification of China under Qin Shi Huang was not merely the product of military superiority; it was the result of a sophisticated and sustained strategy that combined military force with diplomatic manipulation, economic pressure, and psychological warfare. Qin systematically exploited the divisions among its rivals, playing one state against another while building its own strength for the final conquest. Understanding this strategy is essential to comprehending how a state that had been considered a marginal power on the western frontier came to dominate the entire Chinese world.

The strategy that Qin employed in its conquest of the Warring States has been studied for over two millennia, and its principles have been applied in subsequent Chinese history and beyond. The key elements of this strategy—the prioritization of diplomacy over military force when possible, the exploitation of rivalries and suspicions, the patient accumulation of advantage, and the willingness to use both overt and covert methods—reflect a sophisticated understanding of power politics that continues to resonate.

## The Strategic Environment

Before examining Qin's specific tactics, it is important to understand the strategic environment in which the conquest took place. By the mid-third century BCE, the balance of power that had characterized the Warring States system had shifted decisively in Qin's favor. The Legalist reforms had created a state with unprecedented organizational capacity, and the Qin army had demonstrated its superiority in repeated engagements with rival states.

Yet Qin still faced significant challenges. The other major states, while individually weaker than Qin, retained substantial military capabilities. The formation of a successful coalition against Qin could still pose a serious threat, as the vertical alliances of the past had demonstrated. Moreover, the geographic position of Qin, while offering protection from the west, also meant that the state had to fight wars on multiple fronts when it moved against its neighbors.

The solution that Qin developed was a comprehensive strategy that combined military and diplomatic approaches in a systematic manner. This strategy was not the creation of a single brilliant mind but rather the accumulated wisdom of generations of Qin rulers and their advisors, refined and perfected over time. Its implementation required patience, consistency, and a willingness to accept short-term costs in exchange for long-term gains.

## The Principle of "Ally with Distant States, Attack Nearby Ones"

The foundational principle of Qin's diplomatic strategy was expressed in the formula "ally with distant states, attack nearby ones" (远交近攻). This principle recognized that the most immediate threats came from neighboring states, while more distant states could be cultivated as allies or at least neutralized through diplomatic means.

The logic of this principle was both strategic and practical. Distant states posed less immediate threat because they could not easily mobilize forces for sustained campaigns across long distances. They also offered opportunities for diplomatic engagement that could isolate nearby enemies. By cultivating relationships with distant states, Qin could prevent the formation of coalitions and focus its military efforts on one front at a time.

The implementation of this principle required Qin to maintain different relationships with different states. Distant states like Qi, in the far east, and Chu, in the far south, were offered friendly relations, trade agreements, and diplomatic overtures designed to keep them neutral or supportive. Nearby states like Wei, Zhao, and Han, which bordered Qin directly, were targeted for military attack and territorial conquest.

This principle was not merely a guideline for diplomacy; it also shaped military strategy. Rather than dispersing forces across multiple fronts, Qin concentrated its attacks on specific targets while maintaining defensive positions elsewhere. This approach allowed the state to achieve local superiority and defeat its enemies in detail.

## The Policy of "Divide and Negotiate"

While "ally with distant states, attack nearby ones" described the overall framework of Qin's strategy, the more specific policy of "divide and negotiate" (连横) referred to the systematic effort to exploit divisions among the rival states. The term "divide and negotiate" originally described alliances between Qin and individual states, but it came to encompass a broader approach that included bribery, deception, and the manipulation of rivalries.

The fundamental insight behind this policy was that the other Warring States were unlikely to form lasting coalitions against Qin because of their own competing interests. Each state had its own ambitions, its own grievances, and its own relationships with the other states. Qin could exploit these divisions by offering particular states incentives to remain neutral or to collaborate with Qin at the expense of their rivals.

The tools of divide and negotiate included territorial concessions, trade benefits, diplomatic honors, and outright bribery. Qin diplomats were authorized to offer significant inducements to states that would cooperate, while also threatening those that opposed Qin's interests. The goal was to create a situation in which each state calculated that cooperation with Qin was more beneficial than opposition.

Historical records describe numerous examples of this strategy in action. In some cases, Qin offered to return territory that it had captured in exchange for neutrality or alliance. In other cases, Qin provided economic benefits or diplomatic support to states that were willing to align with Qin. The specific terms varied according to circumstances, but the underlying logic was consistent: divide the opposition and isolate specific targets for conquest.

## Bribery and Diplomatic Manipulation

A particularly controversial element of Qin's strategy was the extensive use of bribery to influence officials and decision-makers in rival states. Qin maintained a sophisticated program of bribery that targeted ministers, generals, and even rulers in the other Warring States, offering them wealth and honors in exchange for policies favorable to Qin.

The *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), a collection of diplomatic documents from the period, contains numerous examples of this bribery. In one famous episode, Qin is said to have sent a large amount of gold to a Zhao minister, which he used to persuade his ruler to reject a proposed alliance with other states against Qin. In another example, Qin diplomats are said to have distributed bribes among the ministers of Chu, creating divisions that prevented the formation of a coalition.

The effectiveness of this bribery was based on the political conditions of the Warring States. The Warring States were not modern nation-states with strong institutions; they were personalist polities in which individual ministers and generals wielded significant influence. By corrupting key individuals, Qin could affect the decision-making of entire states without engaging in direct military confrontation.

The use of bribery has been criticized by some historians as unethical or dishonorable, but it was entirely consistent with the realist logic of Warring States politics. In an era of existential competition, states employed whatever means were available to gain advantage over their rivals. The acceptance of bribes by officials in the rival states was equally a product of this environment.

## Intelligence and Espionage

Complementing the bribery program was a sophisticated system of intelligence gathering that allowed Qin to understand the capabilities, intentions, and vulnerabilities of its rivals. The Qin state maintained networks of spies and informants that penetrated the administration and military of rival states, providing information that was crucial for planning military campaigns and diplomatic initiatives.

The *Strategies of the Warring States* contains references to the activities of Qin spies, and later sources describe the organization of intelligence operations in some detail. According to these accounts, Qin employed a variety of methods, including the insertion of agents into rival courts, the cultivation of informants among merchants and travelers, and the analysis of public information to infer developments in rival states.

One particularly important form of intelligence was the understanding of internal politics in rival states. By monitoring the relationships among ministers, the succession struggles within ruling families, and the factions within administrations, Qin could identify opportunities for diplomatic manipulation or military exploitation. The death of a ruler, the rise of a new minister, or a change in factional alliances could all create vulnerabilities that Qin could exploit.

The integration of intelligence with strategy was a hallmark of Qin's approach. Military campaigns were planned on the basis of detailed information about enemy capabilities and dispositions, and diplomatic initiatives were timed to take advantage of internal divisions in rival states. This integration of information and action gave Qin a significant advantage over rivals that lacked comparable intelligence capabilities.

## The Systematic Elimination of Rivals

With this comprehensive strategy in place, Qin proceeded to systematically eliminate its rivals one by one. The conquest proceeded in stages, with Qin targeting the weakest states first while maintaining diplomatic relationships with more distant powers. This methodical approach allowed Qin to build momentum while avoiding the formation of effective opposition.

The conquest began with Han, the smallest of the Warring States, which was annexed in 230 BCE. Han's small territory and vulnerable position made it an easy target, and its fall opened the door for further expansion. Following the conquest of Han, Qin turned against Wei, which had been weakened by earlier defeats and was unable to resist effectively. Wei was annexed in 225 BCE.

The conquest of Zhao proved more difficult, as Zhao was a larger and more powerful state with a formidable military. The campaigns against Zhao culminated in the Battle of Changping in 260 BCE, one of the bloodiest battles of the Warring States period. The Zhao forces were decisively defeated, and the subsequent execution of 400,000 surrendered soldiers marked a brutal turning point in the conquest. Zhao was annexed in 222 BCE.

Chu, the largest of the Warring States in territory, posed a significant challenge. Qin launched a major campaign against Chu in 223 BCE, but the initial effort failed. A second campaign, launched in 224 BCE under the command of the veteran general Wang Jian (王翦), succeeded in overcoming Chu's defenses, and the state was annexed in 223 BCE.

The conquest of Yan followed quickly, with the state falling in 222 BCE. Yan had been weakened by earlier conflicts and was unable to mount effective resistance. The final conquest was of Qi, which had attempted to remain neutral throughout the later conflicts but was ultimately forced to surrender in 221 BCE.

## The Role of Key Figures

The execution of Qin's strategy depended on the abilities of several key figures who served the state as diplomats, generals, and advisors. Among the most important were the ministers who formulated and implemented the diplomatic strategy, as well as the generals who led the military campaigns.

The diplomatic strategy was associated particularly with the minister Zhang Yi (张仪), who served as chancellor under King Huiwen (惠文王) in the early third century BCE. Zhang Yi was the principal advocate of the "divide and negotiate" policy and is credited with developing the diplomatic approach that isolated Qin's rivals. His skill in argumentation and persuasion was legendary, and he was able to extract significant concessions from rival states through a combination of threats and inducements.

However, the foundational principle "ally with distant states, attack nearby ones" (远交近攻) was formulated by Fan Ju (范雎) during King Zhaoxiang's reign (c. 270 BCE). Fan Ju's strategy appears in *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), chapter 3. While Zhang Yi championed *horizontal alliances (连横)*, he did not originate this specific maxim.

Other diplomats continued this approach in later periods, maintaining the relationships with distant states while targeting nearby enemies for conquest. The consistency of this strategy over generations indicates that it was not merely the preference of individual ministers but a systematic policy of the Qin state.

The military campaigns were led by generals like Wang Jian, who commanded the campaigns against Chu, and Lian Po (廉颇) and Bai Qi (白起), who led earlier campaigns against other states. These generals combined tactical skill with the organizational capacity of the Qin military system to achieve victories that might have seemed impossible given the scale of resistance.

The integration of diplomatic and military efforts was coordinated by the Qin rulers themselves, particularly King Zhaoxiang (昭襄王) and his successors. The long-term planning and patient execution of the strategy required consistent support from the highest levels of government, and the Qin rulers provided this support over generations.

## The Conquest and the Birth of Empire

The final conquest of Qi in 221 BCE marked the end of the Warring States period and the birth of the first unified Chinese empire. Ying Zheng, who now took the title Qin Shi Huang (First Emperor of Qin), became the ruler of a territory that encompassed the entire Chinese world.

The transition from conquest to governance presented new challenges that tested the limits of Qin's organizational capacity. The administration of a unified empire required different approaches than the conquest of individual states, and the strategies that had enabled unification were not necessarily suited to the maintenance of empire. The problems that Qin would face in the years after unification would ultimately lead to the collapse of the dynasty.

Yet the strategy of conquest had succeeded in its immediate objective. The fragmented political landscape of the Warring States had been replaced by a unified state of unprecedented scale. The methods that Qin employed—diplomatic manipulation, bribery, espionage, and military force—had proven effective against each of the major powers in turn.

## Legacy and Interpretation

The strategy that Qin employed in its conquest has been studied and debated for over two millennia. Subsequent Chinese dynasties drew lessons from Qin's experience, and the strategies of alliance, division, and manipulation became standard features of Chinese diplomatic practice. The principle of "ally with distant states, attack nearby ones" became a fundamental guideline for Chinese foreign policy.

Western observers have also found relevance in Qin's approach, drawing parallels to modern theories of international relations and strategy. The combination of military power with diplomatic manipulation, the exploitation of divisions among rivals, and the patient accumulation of advantage all resonate with familiar themes in strategic studies.

Yet the strategy also raises ethical questions that continue to be debated. The use of bribery, deception, and brutal military force was characteristic of the Warring States period, but it also established patterns that would persist in Chinese imperial politics. Whether these methods can be justified as pragmatic responses to the conditions of the era, or whether they represent morally problematic approaches to power, remains a matter of interpretation.

What is clear is that Qin's strategy was extraordinarily effective in achieving its objectives. The unification of China was a monumental achievement that transformed the political landscape of East Asia and established foundations for Chinese civilization that would endure for millennia. The strategy that made this achievement possible reflected both the particular conditions of the Warring States period and timeless principles of power politics.

### Chapter Notes

The primary sources for Qin's conquest strategy include the *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), a collection of diplomatic and military documents, and Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*). These sources provide detailed accounts of diplomatic negotiations, military campaigns, and the activities of key individuals.

The interpretation of Qin's strategy has been the subject of extensive scholarly debate. Some scholars emphasize the effectiveness of Qin's approach, arguing that it represented a sophisticated and rational strategy that exploited the weaknesses of rivals. Others have been more critical, focusing on the ethical problems posed by bribery, deception, and brutality.

The role of bribery in Qin's diplomacy has been particularly debated. Some scholars accept the traditional accounts at face value, while others have suggested that they may reflect later propaganda or the biases of Confucian historians who were hostile to Qin. The evidence is fragmentary, and definitive conclusions are difficult to reach.

The chronology of the conquest is generally well-established, with the major campaigns dated to specific years in the third century BCE. However, some details remain disputed, and alternative chronologies have been proposed by various scholars.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), trans. J.I. Crump (San Francisco: Chinese Materials Center, 1985). Collection of diplomatic documents from the period.
- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). Primary historical source for the conquest period.
- *Han Feizi*, trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1964). Legalist philosophical text with implications for strategy.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of the conquest and unification.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the Warring States period.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Discussion of the political transformations of the period.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Detailed study of Qin's unification and the role of key figures.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, "The Birth of the Empire," in *The Early Chinese Empires* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Analysis of the transition from Warring States to empire.
- Kern, Martin, "Qin Unification and the Making of the Empire," in *Birth in an Age of Sacrifice* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2009). Scholarly analysis of the conquest process.


---


# Chapter 10: The Transformation of War

The military conquest of the Warring States was not merely a political event—it was a revolution in the nature of warfare itself. The armies that fought under Qin Shi Huang (Ying Zheng) bore little resemblance to the aristocratic warbands of earlier centuries. They represented a new kind of military force: massive, bureaucratic, and technologically sophisticated. Understanding this transformation is essential to grasping how unification became possible, and why it was Qin, rather than any other state, that ultimately succeeded.

The Warring States period witnessed what scholars have termed a "military revolution"—a fundamental transformation in the scale, organization, and conduct of warfare that reshaped the Chinese political landscape. This revolution involved changes in recruitment and conscription, weapons technology, battlefield tactics, siege warfare, and logistics. Each of these developments contributed to the emergence of a new kind of warfare that made large-scale conquest feasible for the first time in Chinese history.

## From Aristocratic Warbands to Mass Armies

The warfare of the Western Zhou (c. 1046–771 BCE) and the early Spring and Autumn period (770–476 BCE) was fundamentally different from the conflicts of the Warring States. In earlier periods, warfare was largely the preserve of aristocratic warriors—nobles who fought from chariots or on horseback, accompanied by their personal retainers. Battles were often brief, formalized affairs decided by the clash of elite forces. The mass of the population played little direct role in military affairs.

This began to change during the Spring and Autumn period as the great powers expanded their territories and came into conflict with previously independent peoples. The state of Jin, for example, began incorporating large numbers of foot soldiers from the common population into its armies during the seventh century BCE. This process accelerated dramatically in the Warring States period, as the demands of total warfare compelled rulers to mobilize ever larger proportions of their populations.

By the mid-third century BCE, the major Warring States could field armies numbering in the hundreds of thousands. The state of Zhao, according to Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian*, mobilized 400,000 soldiers for the Battle of Changping in 260 BCE—a figure that, if accurate, would represent one of the largest armies the world had seen up to that time. The Qin army that eventually conquered the other states was similarly massive, numbering perhaps 600,000 men at its peak.

The shift to mass armies required fundamental changes in military organization and administration. The old system of aristocratic warbands, in which each noble led his own followers, gave way to a hierarchical command structure with professional officers overseeing standardized units. The Warring States developed sophisticated systems of military rank and file organization that allowed hundreds of thousands of soldiers to be coordinated effectively on the battlefield.

This transformation was closely linked to the broader political and social changes of the period. The Legalist reforms in Qin, which abolished the aristocratic privileges that had characterized earlier periods, created a society in which social advancement depended on military merit. The *Book of Lord Shang*, a Legalist text from the state of Qin, explicitly advocated for the recruitment of soldiers from the general population and the rewarding of valor on the battlefield. These policies helped create a military culture in which service was both a duty and an opportunity for advancement.

## Iron Weapons and the Arsenal Revolution

The shift to mass warfare was made possible by advances in weapons technology, particularly the widespread adoption of iron for military purposes. Iron had been known in China since at least the Spring and Autumn period, but it was during the Warring States that iron production reached the scale necessary to equip entire armies.

The advantages of iron over bronze were significant. Iron ore was more abundant than copper and tin, the components of bronze, making iron weapons cheaper to produce. Iron was also harder and more durable, giving weapons a sharper edge and longer lifespan. The result was a revolution in the accessibility and effectiveness of military equipment.

Archaeological evidence from Warring States sites reveals the scale of this transformation. Large iron foundries have been discovered in the former territories of Zhao, Qi, and Chu, indicating that multiple states had developed significant iron production capacity. The weapons found in Warring States tombs show a progression from bronze to iron over the course of the period, with iron eventually becoming the predominant material for blades and other cutting implements.

The diffusion of iron technology also contributed to changes in armor and protective equipment. Earlier armies had relied on leather or bronze armor, but iron armor became increasingly common during the Warring States. Shields, helmets, and body armor all benefited from iron construction, creating more heavily protected soldiers than ever before.

It is worth noting that the archaeological record presents a more complex picture than the simple narrative of bronze-to-iron transition might suggest. Bronze remained important throughout the period, and some bronze weapons actually improved in quality as metallurgical techniques advanced. Moreover, the distribution of iron technology was uneven across the Warring States, with some regions lagging behind others in adoption. Nevertheless, the overall trend toward iron was clear and consequential.

## The Crossbow and the Democratization of Violence

Alongside advances in metallurgy, the Warring States period saw the development and refinement of the crossbow—a weapon that would have profound implications for the conduct of warfare. The crossbow had been known in China since at least the Spring and Autumn period, but it was during the Warring States that it became a dominant feature of the battlefield.

The crossbow's primary advantage was its ability to transform an untrained individual into an effective soldier. Unlike the bow, which required years of training to master, the crossbow could be learned quickly. A soldier needed only to position the bolt, draw the string back, and release. This simplicity made the crossbow ideal for mass armies composed of conscripted peasants who had little or no military training.

The crossbow also offered tactical advantages on the battlefield. Its bolts traveled in a relatively flat trajectory and could penetrate the armor of the period effectively. Crossbowmen could fire from behind protective barriers, and their weapons required less physical strength to operate than longbows. The result was a weapon that maximized the combat effectiveness of individual soldiers while minimizing the training investment required.

Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* attributes particular importance to the crossbow in Qin's military tradition. According to traditional accounts, the state of Qin was the first to recognize the potential of the crossbow and to develop tactics that exploited its advantages. Whether or not this claim is entirely accurate, it is clear that crossbow-equipped infantry became a cornerstone of Qin military strategy.

The proliferation of crossbows also had social implications. The weapon's simplicity meant that military effectiveness was no longer limited to the aristocratic warriors who had traditionally dominated warfare. Commoners, slaves, and others who had previously been excluded from military service could now be armed and deployed effectively. This democratization of violence was closely linked to the broader social and political transformations of the period.

## Siege Warfare and the Engineering Corps

As the Warring States expanded and came into conflict with fortified cities, siege warfare became increasingly important. The fortifications of the period were often formidable—massive earthen walls, sometimes reinforced with brick, surrounding cities that could sustain prolonged sieges. The ability to capture such fortifications quickly and efficiently became a decisive factor in military success.

The Warring States period saw significant advances in siege techniques. The attacking armies developed a variety of methods for breaching walls, including siege towers, battering rams, and tunneling operations. They also developed techniques for defending against sallies from defenders and for maintaining supply lines during lengthy operations.

Siege warfare required specialized skills and equipment that went beyond the capabilities of ordinary soldiers. This led to the emergence of military engineering as a distinct discipline within the Warring States armies. Engineers who understood fortifications, siege equipment, and logistics became valuable assets for any state seeking to wage offensive warfare.

The Qin state was particularly noted for its engineering capabilities. The same organizational capacity that allowed Qin to mobilize mass armies also enabled the deployment of sophisticated siege equipment. Historical accounts describe Qin armies employing massive siege towers that could match the height of city walls, allowing soldiers to storm fortifications directly. Battering rams, protected by mobile shelters, were used to break through gates and weaker sections of wall.

The importance of siege warfare in the conquest of the Warring States cannot be overstated. Many of the campaigns of the period involved lengthy sieges of major cities, and the ability to reduce fortifications efficiently often determined the outcome of wars. The Qin conquest of the other states relied heavily on the systematic reduction of fortified cities, each of which had to be taken before a state could be fully subjugated.

## The Logistics of Empire

The scale of Warring States warfare created unprecedented logistical challenges. Armies of hundreds of thousands of men required enormous quantities of food, equipment, and other supplies. Moving these supplies across the vast distances of the Chinese heartland demanded sophisticated organizational systems that few states could maintain.

The logistical challenges of Warring States warfare had important strategic implications. States that could sustain prolonged campaigns had significant advantages over those whose supplies ran short. The ability to maintain armies in the field over multiple campaigning seasons became a critical factor in determining the outcome of conflicts.

The Warring States developed various solutions to these logistical challenges. Some states established forward supply bases near the theaters of operations, reducing the distances that supplies had to be transported. Others developed systems of forced contributions from conquered or allied territories, shifting part of the logistical burden to the local population. Still others invested in infrastructure—roads, canals, and warehouses—that improved the efficiency of supply networks.

Qin was particularly effective in solving logistical problems. The state's organizational capacity, developed through decades of Legalist reforms, allowed it to mobilize and coordinate resources on a scale that its rivals could not match. The construction of the Zhengguo Canal and other irrigation projects in Sichuan provided agricultural surplus that could support military operations. The road network built by Qin connected the capital with the frontiers, facilitating the movement of troops and supplies.

The logistical dimension of Qin's conquest strategy deserves particular attention. Historical accounts often focus on battles and campaigns, but the underlying ability to sustain those campaigns was equally important. Qin's victory over its rivals was not merely a matter of superior tactics or equipment—it was also a matter of superior organization.

## The Battle of Changping: Mass Warfare in Practice

The Battle of Changping in 260 BCE illustrates the transformation of warfare in the Warring States period and its consequences. Fought between Qin and Zhao, this battle was one of the largest and bloodiest in Chinese history prior to the modern era. According to traditional accounts, the two sides fielded a combined total of over one million soldiers.

The battle itself was a protracted affair. The Qin army, under the command of General Bai Qi (白起), surrounded a massive Zhao force at Changping and besieged it for months. The Zhao army, cut off from supplies, eventually collapsed. When the survivors surrendered, Bai Qi ordered the execution of approximately 400,000 soldiers—a figure that has been debated by modern scholars but that conveys the brutal scale of the engagement.

The Battle of Changping demonstrated both the possibilities and the dangers of mass warfare. On one hand, the ability to field and sustain such large armies represented a new level of military capacity. On the other hand, the battle showed the potential for catastrophic loss when such armies were defeated. The execution of the surrendered soldiers, whether accurately reported or not, became a symbol of the ruthlessness that characterized the era's warfare.

The battle also had significant strategic consequences. Zhao, previously one of the most powerful Warring States, was severely weakened by the defeat and was never fully able to recover. This opened the way for Qin to proceed with the conquest of the remaining states. The Battle of Changping thus represented a turning point in the struggle for supremacy.

## Military Thought and Theory

The transformation of warfare was accompanied by the development of military thought and theory. The Warring States period saw the emergence of military treatises that systematized knowledge of warfare and articulated strategic principles. These works reflected the practical needs of the period while also contributing to the intellectual development of military science.

The most famous of these works is *Sunzi's Art of War* (*Sunzi Bingfa*), traditionally attributed to a military strategist named Sun Wu who lived in the Spring and Autumn period. Although the text may have been compiled later, it captures strategic principles that were relevant to Warring States warfare. The emphasis on deception, the importance of knowing oneself and the enemy, and the relationship between political and military objectives all resonated with the military practitioners of the era.

Other important military texts from the period include *Six Secret Teachings* (*Liu Taoshi*), *Three Strategies of Huang Shigong* (*Huang Shigong Sanlue*), and *Questions and Answers between Tang and King Wu* (*Tang Wen Wu Wen*). These works varied in their specific recommendations, but they shared a common concern with the rational analysis of warfare and the development of systematic strategic principles.

The Legalist school also contributed to military thought, particularly in Qin. The *Book of Lord Shang* and other Legalist texts emphasized the importance of military strength and the rewards of martial valor. Legalist rulers were encouraged to view warfare as a rational instrument of state policy, to be pursued systematically in pursuit of clearly defined objectives.

## The Revolution Completed

By the time Qin launched its final conquest of the other Warring States, the transformation of warfare was essentially complete. The armies that conquered the Warring States were products of a military revolution that had unfolded over centuries—a revolution that had created new kinds of soldiers, new weapons, new tactics, and new systems of organization.

This revolution had important consequences beyond the immediate context of the unification war. The mass armies, sophisticated logistics, and advanced siege techniques that had enabled conquest would also be necessary for the administration of a unified empire. The same organizational capacity that had allowed Qin to defeat its rivals would be applied to the task of governing the newly unified China.

Yet the transformation also had darker implications. The scale of destruction in battles like Changping, the brutality of siege warfare, and the ruthless pursuit of victory all testified to the costs of the new warfare. The unification achieved by Qin would be followed by the collapse of the empire, in part because the methods that had enabled conquest could not sustain governance. The military revolution had created the possibility of empire, but it had not solved the problem of maintaining it.

### Chapter Notes

The transformation of warfare in the Warring States period has been the subject of extensive scholarly analysis. The concept of a "military revolution" was developed by Western historians studying European history, but it has been productively applied to the Chinese case as well. Scholars like Mark Edward Lewis have analyzed the specific features of Warring States warfare and their implications for political development.

The debate over the accuracy of casualty figures in ancient Chinese battles continues. Numbers in ancient sources must be treated with caution, as they often reflect rhetorical purposes rather than precise counts. Nevertheless, the scale of Warring States armies clearly exceeded anything seen in earlier periods.

The relationship between social change and military transformation is complex and has been debated by scholars. The abolition of aristocratic privileges in Qin created new possibilities for military recruitment, but similar changes occurred in other states as well. The specific causes of Qin's military superiority remain a matter of interpretation.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). Primary source for military campaigns and figures.
- *Sunzi Bingfa* (Sunzi's Art of War), trans. Ralph D. Sawyer (Boulder: Westview Press, 1994). Foundational military treatise.
- *Book of Lord Shang*, trans. J.J.L. Duyvendak (London: Arthur Probsthain, 1928). Legalist text with military implications.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of military transformation.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the period.
- Sawyer, Ralph D., *The Seven Military Classics of Ancient China* (Boulder: Westview Press, 1993). Collection of military texts with commentary.
- Creel, H.G., *The Origins of Statecraft in China* (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1970). Foundational study of early Chinese political development.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Analysis of Warring States political transformations.


---


# Chapter 11: The Fall of Han — The First Domino (230 BCE)

The conquest of the Warring States began not with a dramatic siege of a great capital or a decisive battle between rival armies, but with the quiet annexation of the smallest and weakest of the seven major powers. Han, wedged between Qin and the other states, had long survived by balancing between larger neighbors and adapting to the demands of more powerful rulers. In 230 BCE, this survival strategy finally failed. The fall of Han was the first step in Qin's systematic conquest of the Chinese world, and it marked the beginning of the end for the ancient order of competing states.

Understanding the conquest of Han requires understanding both the specific circumstances of the Han state and the broader strategic context in which Qin operated. Han was not merely a convenient first target—it was a strategic necessity, the opening move in a carefully planned campaign that would, within a decade, sweep all before it.

## The Han State: A Small Power in a Dangerous Neighborhood

Han was, by any measure, the smallest of the Warring States. Its territory, concentrated in the central plain around the Yellow River, encompassed only a fraction of the land area of its neighbors. The state lacked the natural defenses that protected some other states, and its population was correspondingly smaller. Han could not hope to match its larger rivals in military strength, and its survival depended on diplomatic skill and strategic flexibility.

Despite its weakness, Han played an important role in Warring States politics. Its central position meant that it lay at the crossroads of the Chinese world, and control of Han territory offered significant strategic advantages. Larger states frequently fought over Han, and Han rulers learned to navigate this dangerous environment by playing rivals against each other. The state's very weakness made it useful to larger powers as a buffer or a pawn, and this provided a measure of protection that might otherwise have been lacking.

Han was also notable for its cultural and intellectual contributions. The state was home to the School of Names (Mingjia), a philosophical tradition concerned with logic, paradox, and the nature of language. The famous sophist Gongsun Long (公孙龙) was associated with this tradition. Han also produced notable military theorists, including Sun Bin (孙膑), the author of the *Art of War* attributed to him, who served as a military advisor to the state of Qi.

Yet these intellectual achievements could not compensate for military weakness. By the mid-third century BCE, Han had been repeatedly weakened by wars with its neighbors, particularly Qin. The state's territory had been carved away piece by piece, and it had been reduced to a shadow of its former self. When Qin finally moved to complete the conquest, Han had little capacity for resistance.

## The Strategic Context: Qin Prepares for Conquest

The conquest of Han must be understood in the context of Qin's broader strategy for unification. By the late third century BCE, Qin had already established clear military superiority over its rivals.
