# Chapter 13: The Chu Campaign (224-223 BCE)

The conquest of Chu represented the most ambitious military undertaking in Qin's campaign of unification. Chu was not merely another Warring State; it was the largest and perhaps the most culturally sophisticated of all the rival kingdoms, a southern power that had long viewed itself as the rightful heir to the Zhou imperial tradition. With its vast territories spanning the Yangtze River basin and its capital at Shouchun, Chu commanded resources and manpower that dwarfed the other states of the Warring States system. The campaigns against Chu, which unfolded between 224 and 223 BCE, would test the limits of Qin's military capacity and reveal both the strengths and the vulnerabilities of the Qin war machine. The story of Chu's conquest is one of initial failure, strategic reassessment, and ultimately total triumph—a narrative that illuminates the character of the Qin generals and the determination of the king who led them. The conquest would also bring Qin into contact with the diverse peoples of the south and southwest, extending the boundaries of the Chinese world to encompass regions that had previously existed beyond the reach of the central平原 states.

## The Challenge of Chu

Chu occupied a vast territory stretching from the Yangtze River basin in the south to the Huang River region in the north, encompassing an area that covered much of modern-day Hubei, Hunan, Jiangxi, Anhui, and parts of Jiangsu and Sichuan provinces. The state had been a major power since the Spring and Autumn period, when it had emerged as one of the dominant states in the southern region. During the Warring States era, it had consistently ranked among the most formidable of the rival kingdoms, competing with Qin, Qi, and Zhao for hegemony over the Chinese world. At its height, Chu had controlled more territory than any other Warring State, encompassing regions that would later become some of the most prosperous and densely populated areas of imperial China. The Chu capital of Shouchun (壽春), to which the court had relocated in the fourth century BCE, was a major metropolitan center that could sustain prolonged resistance. The city's location on the upper reaches of the Yangtze River provided both strategic depth and economic vitality, connecting Chu to the valuable trade networks of the south.

The strategic challenges posed by Chu were immense. The state's geographic position meant that any Qin invasion would have to traverse difficult terrain, including mountainous regions, dense forests, and extensive wetland areas. The Chu military, while not as professionally organized as the Qin forces, was numerically substantial and fought with a distinctive style that emphasized mobility and the use of infantry in varied terrain. The Chu navy, which operated on the Yangtze River and its tributaries, gave the state additional strategic depth, allowing it to shift forces quickly and to threaten Qin flanks along the river systems.

Moreover, Chu possessed substantial economic resources. The fertile lands of the Yangtze valley produced abundant harvests, and the state's position on major trade routes gave it access to valuable resources from the south and southwest. The Chu capital of Shouchun (寿春), to which the court had relocated in the fourth century BCE, was a major metropolitan center that could sustain prolonged resistance.

Qin's previous encounters with Chu had been mixed. In the fourth century BCE, Qin had suffered significant defeats in its attempts to expand southward, and the memory of these failures had contributed to a certain caution in Qin's approach to Chu. However, by the third century BCE, the balance of power had shifted dramatically in Qin's favor. The reforms of Shang Yang and the subsequent militarization of Qin had created a war machine that had proven decisive against every state it had faced. Yet even the most confident Qin planners recognized that Chu would present unique challenges that would require careful strategy and substantial resources.

## The First Campaign: Overconfidence and Defeat

The first Qin campaign against Chu, which began in 224 BCE, reflected a dangerous degree of overconfidence. According to the historical accounts, Ying Zheng and his advisors underestimated the difficulty of the campaign and dispatched a force that proved inadequate to the task. The general chosen to lead this initial campaign was Li Xin (李信), a distinguished general unrelated to Li Si. Li You (李由), Li Si's son, appears later in history during the Chen Sheng rebellion of 209 BCE. This choice has been interpreted by scholars as reflecting a combination of political considerations and strategic miscalculation.

Li Xiang was not without military experience, but he lacked the extensive record of success that characterized veteran Qin commanders like Wang Jian. The decision to appoint him may have been influenced by political dynamics at the Qin court, where Li Si wielded considerable influence. However, the campaign's failure would demonstrate the dangers of allowing political considerations to override military judgment.

The campaign began with Qin forces advancing into Chu territory along the northern frontier. Initial progress appeared promising, and the Qin forces achieved some early successes against Chu border garrisons. However, as the army pushed deeper into Chu territory, it encountered increasingly stiff resistance. The Chu forces, commanded by Xiang (項) — likely Xiang Ya (項牙) or another member of the powerful Xiang clan — adopted a defensive strategy, withdrawing before the Qin advance while harassing its supply lines.

The terrain worked to Chu's advantage. The forests and wetlands of the south slowed the Qin advance and made it difficult to maintain lines of communication and supply. The Chu forces, familiar with the local geography, were able to operate effectively in this environment, while the Qin troops, trained primarily for warfare on the more open terrain of the central plains, found themselves at a disadvantage.

The campaign ended in failure when the Qin forces were decisively defeated at the Battle of Suqi (蘇醜). The details of the battle are not fully preserved in the historical sources, but it is clear that the Chu army achieved a significant victory, inflicting heavy casualties on the Qin forces and forcing them to retreat. The defeat was a shock to the Qin court and demonstrated that the seemingly invincible Qin war machine could be beaten when properly confronted.

## Wang Jian's Intervention and the Second Campaign

The failure of the first Chu campaign forced Ying Zheng to confront a difficult reality. The campaign had exposed fundamental weaknesses in Qin's approach: inadequate intelligence about Chu's military capabilities, insufficient forces for the scale of the operation, and a tendency to underestimate the challenges posed by the southern terrain. Most importantly, the failure had demonstrated the necessity of employing Qin's most capable military leadership.

Ying Zheng turned to Wang Jian, the veteran general who had already proven himself in campaigns against Zhao and other states. Wang Jian was by now an elderly man, but his reputation for careful planning and tactical brilliance made him the obvious choice for this crucial campaign. According to Sima Qian's account, Wang Jian initially demanded an enormous army for the campaign, requesting sixty thousand troops—virtually the entire available force of the Qin state (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

The size of Wang Jian's request reflected his understanding of the challenge ahead. Unlike the first campaign, which had proceeded on the assumption that Chu could be defeated with a relatively small force, Wang Jian recognized that overwhelming numerical superiority would be necessary to overcome Chu's advantages in terrain and manpower. His request was also a political statement: by demanding such a large army, Wang Jian was signaling that he would not accept half-measures or political interference in his strategic planning.

Ying Zheng, apparently convinced by Wang Jian's analysis, granted the requested forces. The decision to commit such substantial resources to a single campaign demonstrated the king's determination to eliminate Chu as a factor in the unification struggle. With sixty thousand troops at his disposal, Wang Jian now had the means to pursue a more comprehensive strategy.

## The Conquest of Chu

The second campaign against Chu, which began in 223 BCE, followed a fundamentally different approach from the first. Wang Jian's strategy combined careful preparation with decisive action, avoiding the overextension that had undone the earlier expedition.

Wang Jian began by assembling his forces at the border and conducting a thorough assessment of the Chu defensive布局. Rather than rushing into battle, he spent time building up supplies and establishing secure lines of communication. This methodical approach reflected Wang Jian's characteristic caution and his understanding that a prolonged campaign would require a solid logistical foundation.

Once the campaign commenced, the Qin forces advanced along multiple axes, coordinated to prevent Chu from concentrating its defenses in any single area. The Chu army, under the command of Xiang (項), attempted to mount a defense but found itself overwhelmed by the scale and coordination of the Qin assault. The two armies met in a decisive engagement at Suqi, where Wang Jian achieved the victory that had eluded his predecessor.

The Battle of Suqi was the turning point of the war. In a reversal of the previous year's outcome, the Qin forces now triumphed over the Chu army. The Chu forces were shattered, with casualties reported in the hundreds of thousands. Xiang (項) was killed in the battle, eliminating the commander who had led Chu's successful defense the previous year. With the destruction of the main Chu field army, the path to the Chu capital of Shouchun lay open.

Following the victory at Suqi, Wang Jian's forces advanced rapidly toward the Chu capital. The fall of Shouchun marked the end of Chu as an independent state. The Chu king, whose title was Fu (負), was captured and executed—a fate that awaited the rulers of all conquered states. The royal family of Chu was scattered, with some members executed and others exiled or integrated into the Qin system.

The conquest of Chu was comprehensive. Wang Jian's forces swept across the former Chu territories, eliminating resistance and establishing Qin administrative control. The vast southern lands, from the Yangtze valley to the borders of the Wu and Yue regions, were now incorporated into the Qin state. The speed and completeness of the conquest demonstrated the effectiveness of Wang Jian's strategy and the overwhelming power of the Qin military when properly deployed.

## The Fate of the Chu Royal Family and Nobility

The treatment of the Chu royal family and nobility followed the pattern established in earlier Qin conquests. The Chu king was executed, eliminating the possibility of a restoration movement. The aristocratic families of Chu were disbanded, their estates confiscated, and their members either executed, exiled, or absorbed into the Qin administrative system.

However, the conquest of Chu also revealed some of the challenges that Qin would face in administering its newly acquired territories. The Chu nobility, though stripped of their political power, retained a strong sense of regional identity and cultural distinctiveness. The differences between Chu and Qin in language, customs, and social organization would create lasting tensions in the post-conquest period. These cultural differences would contribute to the rebellions that would erupt following the collapse of the Qin empire, as former Chu nobles would play prominent roles in the movements that overthrew Qin rule.

The conquest of Chu also brought Qin into contact with the peoples of the south and southwest, including the cultures of Wu, Yue, and the various tribal groups of the region. These territories would present their own administrative challenges, and the expansion of Qin influence into these areas would be a gradual process that would extend beyond the unification of 221 BCE.

## The Administration of Conquered Chu

Following the military conquest, Qin faced the enormous challenge of integrating the vast Chu territories into its administrative system. The territories were organized into commanderies and counties, with Qin officials replacing the old Chu administrators. This process was complicated by the cultural differences between Qin and Chu, as well as by the resistance of local populations to foreign rule.

The Qin government implemented policies designed to assimilate the Chu population into the Qin system. These included the promotion of Qin laws and customs, the relocation of Qin settlers to key areas, and the suppression of Chu cultural practices. However, these policies were only partially successful, and Chu cultural traditions would persist for generations, eventually resurfacing in the rebellions that followed the fall of Qin.

## Strategic Consequences of Chu's Fall

The fall of Chu had profound strategic implications for the unification campaign. With Chu eliminated, Qin had removed the largest and most territorially extensive of the rival states. The southern frontier was now secure, and Qin could turn its attention to the remaining states without fear of a major threat emerging from the south.

The conquest also freed up substantial military resources. The forces that had been committed to the Chu campaign could now be redeployed against the remaining states: Zhao to the north, Yan to the northeast, and Qi to the east. The elimination of Chu also changed the strategic geometry of the Warring States system, leaving the other states increasingly isolated and vulnerable to Qin's combined pressure.

For Ying Zheng, the conquest of Chu was a vindication of his determination to pursue unification. The initial setback in 224 BCE had been painful, but the ultimate victory in 223 BCE had secured the largest prize of the entire campaign. The king who would soon declare himself the first emperor of a unified China could now look forward to the final phase of the conquest with confidence.

## Historical Perspectives on the Chu Campaign

The conquest of Chu has been the subject of considerable historical analysis, with scholars focusing on the reasons for the initial failure and the factors that contributed to eventual success. The traditional Chinese historiography, following Sima Qian's account, emphasized the importance of proper military leadership and the dangers of overconfidence. The first campaign was attributed to Li Xiang's inadequacy, while the success of the second campaign was credited to Wang Jian's skill and experience.

Modern scholars have expanded on this analysis, examining the campaign within the broader context of Warring States warfare and the strategic dynamics of the period. Some have noted that the first campaign may have been intended as a reconnaissance in force rather than a full-scale conquest, and that its failure forced Qin to adopt a more committed approach. Others have emphasized the importance of logistics and supply in enabling Wang Jian's successful campaign, noting that the careful preparation of supply lines was crucial to sustaining the larger army in unfamiliar terrain.

The cultural dimensions of the Chu conquest have also attracted attention. Chu had developed a distinctive cultural tradition that differed significantly from that of Qin, and the integration of Chu into the Qin system required overcoming not only military resistance but also cultural and social barriers. The long-term consequences of this cultural tension would become apparent in the rebellions that followed the fall of Qin, when Chu cultural identity would emerge as a powerful force in opposition to Qin rule.

### Chapter Notes

The primary source for the Chu campaign is Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), which provides the basic narrative of both the failed first campaign and the successful second campaign. However, the *Shiji* account is relatively brief, and many details of the campaigns remain unclear.

The identity of the Chu commanders in the campaigns has been the subject of some scholarly discussion. Sima Qian mentions a commander named Xiang (項), who is likely to be Xiang Ya (項牙), a member of the powerful Xiang clan that would later play a prominent role in the rebellions against Qin.

The chronology of the campaigns is generally established, with the first campaign occurring in 224 BCE and the second in 223 BCE. However, some scholars have proposed alternative chronologies based on analysis of astronomical records and other evidence.

The size of Wang Jian's army is recorded as sixty thousand troops in the *Shiji*, though some scholars have questioned whether this figure refers to a single army or to the total Qin forces committed to the campaign. The number may also reflect the terminology of the Han period, when Sima Qian was writing, rather than the actual military organization of Qin.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary historical source for the Chu campaigns and the role of Wang Jian.
- *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), trans. J.I. Crump (San Francisco: Chinese Materials Center, 1985). Contains diplomatic and strategic material relevant to the period.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of Qin's conquest campaigns.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the Warring States period.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Discussion of the political transformations of the period.
- Cook, Constance A., and John S. Major, eds., *Defining Chu: Image and Reality in Ancient China* (Honolulu: University of Hawai'i Press, 1999). Collection of essays on Chu culture and history.
- Major, John S., *Heaven and Earth in Early Han Thought* (Albany: SUNY Press, 1993). Discussion of Chu intellectual and cultural traditions.

---


# Chapter 14: The Northern Campaign — Zhao's End (228-222 BCE)

The campaigns against Zhao represented one of the longest and most complex military struggles in Qin's campaign of unification. Unlike the swift conquests of Han and Wei, or the decisive victory over Chu, the conflict with Zhao stretched across multiple years and required the deployment of Qin's finest military commanders. The northern campaigns, which unfolded between 228 and 222 BCE, would see the destruction of the Zhao state and the elimination of one of Qin's most determined adversaries. The story of Zhao's fall is also a story of heroism and tragedy, of generals who defended their state to the last and a royal family that was ultimately scattered to the winds. The campaigns against Zhao would also test Qin's capacity for prolonged warfare and demonstrate both the strengths and limitations of the Qin military system.

The Zhao campaigns hold a particular significance in Chinese historical memory, not only for the military events themselves but also for the dramatic personalities involved. The story of Li Mu, the great general who was executed by his own government while defending the capital, became a powerful symbol of the tragedy of loyalty betrayed. Similarly, the defense of Handan, which resisted the Qin siege for so long, entered the historical imagination as an example of courage in the face of overwhelming odds.

## Zhao on the Eve of Conquest

Zhao had long been one of Qin's most formidable rivals. Situated in the northern reaches of the ancient Chinese heartland, Zhao controlled the strategic approaches to the Ordos region and the frontiers of the steppe. The state had been strengthened by the reforms of King Wuling (武靈王), who had adopted the mounted archer tactics of the nomadic peoples to create a powerful cavalry force. This military innovation had transformed Zhao into a military powerhouse capable of matching Qin in conventional warfare and exceeding it in mobile cavalry operations.

By the late third century BCE, however, Zhao had been significantly weakened by decades of conflict. The state had borne the brunt of Qin's northern campaigns, and its military had been worn down by repeated wars. The death of the great Zhao general Lian Po (廉頗) and the discrediting of another capable commander, Li Mu (李牧), had deprived Zhao of the leadership it needed to resist the Qin advance. Political instability at the Zhao court had further weakened the state's capacity for resistance.

Yet despite these difficulties, Zhao remained dangerous. The state's northern position gave it strategic depth, and its cavalry force remained a formidable weapon. The Zhao capital of Handan (邯鄲) was one of the most strongly fortified cities in the Warring States system, and the state's remaining generals were determined to defend it to the last. When Qin finally turned its attention to the final conquest of Zhao in 228 BCE, it would face a determined enemy that would fight until the very end.

## The First Campaign: Wang Jian's Northern Campaign (228 BCE)

The campaign against Zhao began in 228 BCE, following the completion of the Chu conquest. Ying Zheng assigned the campaign to Wang Jian, who had proven his strategic mastery in the southern campaigns. Wang Jian's experience in northern warfare, where he had previously fought against Zhao and other states, made him the obvious choice for this crucial operation.

The campaign began with Qin forces advancing into Zhao territory from the west. The initial phase of the campaign was a direct military offensive aimed at breaking the Zhao field army and seizing territory. According to the historical accounts, Wang Jian achieved significant victories in the early stages of the campaign, defeating Zhao forces in several engagements and advancing toward the Zhao capital.

The campaign's progress was facilitated by internal divisions within Zhao. The Zhao court had been torn by political factionalism, and the military leadership had been compromised by political interference. The general Lian Po, who had been one of Zhao's greatest assets, had been driven into exile by political enemies at court. His replacement, which some sources identify as a commander named Bian (邊), was unable to rally the same level of confidence or effectiveness.

The death of the Zhao king during this period also contributed to the instability. King You (幽繆王), who had ruled Zhao since 236 BCE, died in 228 BCE, possibly as a result of wounds sustained in battles against Qin. The succession was contested, with a prince named Jia (嘉) establishing himself as the new king. This political turmoil further weakened Zhao's capacity for resistance.

Despite these advantages, the initial Qin campaign did not achieve the complete conquest of Zhao. The siege of Handan proved to be a prolonged and difficult operation. The city was heavily fortified and well-provisioned, and the defenders put up stubborn resistance. The Zhao forces, though weakened, were still capable of mounting effective defense, and the siege dragged on through the winter months.

Wang Jian recognized that the siege could not be brought to a quick conclusion with the forces available. Accordingly, he adopted a strategy of attriting the Zhao state while maintaining pressure on the capital. Qin forces occupied large portions of Zhao territory, seizing resources and disrupting Zhao's economic base. This strategy was designed to weaken Handan's capacity for resistance over time, creating the conditions for a final assault when the city was sufficiently weakened.

## The Role of Li Mu and the Defense of Zhao

The siege of Handan continued into 228 BCE, and as the months passed, the situation for Zhao grew increasingly desperate. However, the appearance of Li Mu (李牧) as the defender of Handan changed the dynamics of the siege. Li Mu, who had previously served as a general on Zhao's northern frontier, was recalled to the capital to organize the defense. His arrival marked a turning point in the campaign.

Li Mu was one of the most capable military commanders of the Warring States period. He had earned his reputation defending Zhao's northern borders against the nomadic peoples, and he brought a level of strategic sophistication that the Qin forces had not previously encountered. Under his leadership, the defense of Handan became organized and effective. The city's walls were reinforced, supplies were carefully managed, and the defenders were coordinated into an efficient force.

The traditional accounts describe Li Mu's defense in somewhat idealized terms, emphasizing his tactical skill and his ability to inspire the defenders. According to Sima Qian, Li Mu employed defensive tactics that frustrated the Qin assault, keeping the siege stalemated despite Qin's numerical superiority (Sima Qian, *Shiji*). The Qin forces, unable to breach the city's defenses or force a surrender, found themselves locked in a prolonged campaign that threatened to drain their resources.

The stalemate at Handan presented Wang Jian with a difficult strategic choice. He could continue the siege, hoping that starvation and attrition would eventually force a surrender, or he could withdraw and regroup for a future campaign. Each option carried risks: continuing the siege would expose the Qin forces to the dangers of prolonged campaigning in difficult conditions, while withdrawal would allow Zhao to recover and strengthen its defenses.

## The Second Campaign: The Fall of Handan (228-227 BCE)

The first campaign against Zhao ended without the capture of Handan, though Qin forces had occupied substantial portions of Zhao territory. Wang Jian withdrew his main forces to reorganize and prepare for a renewed offensive. The strategic initiative, however, remained with Qin, and the following year would see the resumption of the campaign with increased force.

The second campaign, which began in 227 BCE, was assigned to a new commander: Yang Duan (楊端). Wang Jian, now elderly, had apparently withdrawn from active command, and the younger Yang Duan was given responsibility for completing the conquest of Zhao. The choice of a new commander may have reflected concerns about the stalemate under Wang Jian, or it may have been part of a broader rotation of military leadership.

Yang Duan's campaign followed a similar pattern to Wang Jian's earlier effort. The Qin forces advanced toward Handan, engaging Zhao forces in a series of battles. The key difference this time was that Li Mu, the defender who had organized Handan's resistance, was no longer in command. According to the traditional accounts, Li Mu had been recalled from the front lines and executed on false charges of treason. This was a catastrophic blow to Zhao, eliminating the general who had given the capital its best hope for survival.

Without Li Mu's leadership, the defense of Handan quickly deteriorated. The Qin forces were able to press the siege more effectively, and the city's resources began to dwindle. The defenders, lacking the coordination and inspiration that Li Mu had provided, were unable to repel the Qin assaults. In 228 BCE, the city finally fell.

The fall of Handan marked the end of Zhao as an independent state. The Zhao king, whether You (幽繆王) or Jia (嘉) depending on the precise chronology, was captured and executed. The royal family was scattered, with members either killed or taken captive. The great northern capital, which had withstood so many sieges over the centuries, was now in Qin hands.

## The Resistance of the Zhao Remnant (227-222 BCE)

The fall of Handan did not immediately end Zhao resistance. A member of the Zhao royal family, apparently a prince named Cheng (成), established a rump state in the northern region of Dai (代) and continued to resist Qin rule for several years. This remnant state, sometimes referred to as Northern Zhao, maintained a degree of independence and military capability that troubled Qin administrators.

The resistance in the north was led by the former Zhao general, Sui (隨), who rallied what remained of the Zhao military to the defense of the remnant state. For several years, this small state managed to maintain its independence, aided by the difficult terrain of the northern region and the challenges that Qin faced in projecting power so far from its base.

The existence of the Zhao remnant was a persistent irritant to Qin. It provided a focus for opposition to Qin rule and a potential base for restoration movements. The Qin court was determined to eliminate this last source of Zhao resistance, and in 222 BCE, a final campaign was launched against the northern state.

The campaign of 222 BCE, commanded by Qin forces under the general Lian (獵), achieved complete success. The Zhao remnant was destroyed, the last prince Cheng (成) was captured, and the general Sui (隨) was killed. With the elimination of this final resistance, Zhao ceased to exist as a political entity. The entire territory of the former Zhao state was now under Qin control.

## The Fate of the Zhao Royal Family

The fate of the Zhao royal family illustrated the harsh realities of conquest in the Warring States period. The rulers of Zhao were executed, their titles abolished, and their estates confiscated. The treatment of the royal family followed the pattern established in other conquered states: elimination of the old ruling elite and their replacement with Qin administrators.

However, the Zhao royal family would not entirely disappear from history. Some members of the family survived, and their descendants would later play roles in the events following the fall of Qin. The most famous of these was Zhao Tuo (趙佗), who would establish the Nanyue kingdom in the far south during the chaotic period after Qin's collapse. This ironic twist—the transformation of a captured Zhao prince into the founder of an independent kingdom beyond Qin's reach—demonstrated the unexpected ways in which conquest could shape history.

The generals who had defended Zhao met various fates. Lian Po, the veteran commander who had been driven from office before the final crisis, apparently survived to see the fall of his state. Li Mu, executed on charges of treason, became a symbol of the injustice that could attend even the most loyal servants. His death was later cited by critics of the Qin system as an example of the regime's willingness to destroy its own best defenders out of suspicion and paranoia. The tragedy of Li Mu would become a recurring theme in Chinese historical writing, serving as a warning about the dangers of political persecution of military talent.

## The Legacy of Zhao's Resistance

The resistance of Zhao, though ultimately unsuccessful, left a lasting legacy in Chinese historical memory. The defense of Handan became a symbol of courage and determination in the face of impossible odds. The story of Li Mu, in particular, resonated through subsequent Chinese history, serving as a cautionary tale about the treatment of loyal officials.

The fall of Zhao also marked a significant moment in the unification of China. The elimination of the northern state completed Qin's control over the traditional Chinese heartland, creating the territorial foundation for the new empire. The conquest of Zhao also brought Qin into direct contact with the nomadic peoples of the steppe, a relationship that would define much of Chinese history in the centuries to come.

## Strategic Consequences of Zhao's Fall

## Strategic Consequences of Zhao's Fall

The conquest of Zhao completed Qin's control over the northern heartland. With Zhao eliminated, Qin now bordered the nomadic peoples of the steppe frontier, inheriting the defensive responsibilities that Zhao had long carried. The vast territories of the north, including the strategic Ordos region, were now incorporated into the Qin state.

The fall of Zhao also changed the strategic position of the remaining states. Yan, to the northeast, was now isolated, with Qin forces on its border. Qi, to the east, faced Qin across an ever-narrowing wedge of territory. The consolidation of Qin power in the north created the conditions for the final campaigns of unification.

For Ying Zheng, the conquest of Zhao was the culmination of a long struggle. Zhao had been one of Qin's most persistent and dangerous adversaries, and its elimination removed a major obstacle to the completion of unification. The king could now turn his attention to the final campaigns against Yan and Qi, which would bring the entire Chinese world under his rule.

## Historical Perspectives on the Zhao Campaigns

The campaigns against Zhao have been the subject of considerable historical analysis, with scholars focusing on the military, political, and cultural dimensions of the conflict. The traditional Chinese historiography emphasized the role of individual commanders and the moral dimensions of the struggle. Li Mu was portrayed as a tragic hero, done to death by political enemies, while the Qin conquest was seen as an example of ruthless ambition triumphing over loyalty and virtue.

Modern scholars have adopted more analytical approaches, examining the campaigns within the context of Warring States military organization and strategy. The prolonged nature of the Zhao conflict has been attributed to the strategic difficulties of siege warfare and the challenges of projecting power into the northern regions. The role of political instability in weakening Zhao has also been emphasized, with scholars noting that the internal divisions of the Zhao court contributed as much to its defeat as any military superiority on Qin's part.

The cultural dimensions of the Zhao conquest have also attracted attention. Zhao had developed a distinctive cultural tradition, shaped by its position on the northern frontier and its interactions with the nomadic peoples. The integration of this culture into the Qin system presented challenges that would outlast the unification itself.

### Chapter Notes

The primary source for the Zhao campaigns is Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), which provides detailed accounts of the military operations, the commanders involved, and the fate of the Zhao royal family. However, the *Shiji* account contains some chronological difficulties, and scholars have debated the precise sequence of events.

The identity of the commanders in the Zhao campaigns has been the subject of scholarly discussion. Wang Jian's role in the initial campaign is well-established, but the details of the later campaigns and the identity of Yang Duan are less clear. Some scholars have suggested that Yang Duan may have been the same person as Lian (獵), who commanded the final campaign against the Zhao remnant.

The execution of Li Mu is one of the most controversial episodes in the campaign. Sima Qian reports that Li Mu was executed on charges of treason, but the reliability of this account has been questioned. Some scholars have suggested that Li Mu may have died in battle or that the circumstances of his death may have been different from the traditional account.

The chronology of the final campaign against the Zhao remnant is also uncertain. Most sources place the final conquest in 222 BCE, but some alternative chronologies have been proposed.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary historical source for the Zhao campaigns.
- *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), trans. J.I. Crump (San Francisco: Chinese Materials Center, 1985). Contains diplomatic and strategic material relevant to the period.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of Qin's conquest campaigns.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the Warring States period.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Discussion of the political transformations of the period.
- Yang, Kuan, *Zhandong Zhanzheng Shi* (History of the Warring States Period) (Beijing: Renmin Chubanshe, 1956). Detailed Chinese scholarly study of the period.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, *Sanctioned Violence in Early China* (Albany: SUNY Press, 1990). Discussion of military organization and violence in the Warring States period.

---


# Chapter 15: The Eastern Campaigns — Yan and Qi (226-221 BCE)

The campaigns against Yan and Qi represented the final phase of Qin's campaign of unification. With Chu, Zhao, and Wei eliminated, only two major states remained: Yan in the northeast and Qi in the east. These campaigns, which unfolded between 226 and 221 BCE, would bring the Warring States era to its conclusion and create the first unified empire in Chinese history. The story of these final campaigns is one of contrast: Yan's desperate resistance and ultimate collapse, followed by Qin's diplomatic and military pressure on Qi, which surrendered almost without a fight. The completion of unification in 221 BCE marked the end of centuries of political fragmentation and the beginning of a new imperial age that would shape Chinese civilization for millennia.

The campaigns against Yan and Qi also had important implications for the future development of the Chinese empire. The incorporation of the northeastern territories brought Qin into contact with new peoples and cultures, while the eastern regions would become some of the most economically important parts of the new empire.

## The State of Yan on the Eve of Conquest

Yan occupied the northeastern corner of the Chinese world, controlling the territories around modern-day Beijing and Hebei Province. The state had a long history dating back to the Spring and Autumn period, and it had developed a distinctive cultural tradition shaped by its interactions with the nomadic peoples of the north and the various tribal groups of the northeast.

By the late third century BCE, Yan had been significantly weakened by its conflicts with Qi and Zhao. The state had suffered a catastrophic defeat at the hands of Qi earlier in the century, and it had never fully recovered. The territorial losses to Zhao in the west had further diminished Yan's resources and strategic position. Yet despite these weaknesses, Yan remained a significant power, and its geographic position made it a potential threat to Qin's northern flank.

The Yan court was aware of the danger posed by Qin's expansion. As Qin eliminated the other major states, Yan found itself increasingly isolated, with Qin forces on its border and no potential allies remaining. The Yan king, whose title was Xi (喜), recognized the existential threat and sought to prepare his state for the coming confrontation.

## The Campaign Against Yan (226-222 BCE)

The campaign against Yan began in 226 BCE, following the completion of the conquests of Chu and Zhao. The Qin forces were commanded by Wang Ben (王賁), the son of the veteran general Wang Jian, who had led the campaigns against Chu. Wang Ben had already proven himself in the campaign against Wei, and his selection for the Yan campaign reflected Qin's confidence in his abilities.

The campaign began with Qin forces advancing into Yan territory from the west. The initial stages of the campaign were successful, with Qin forces achieving several victories over the Yan army and advancing toward the Yan capital of Ji (薊). The speed of the Qin advance reflected the weakened state of Yan, which was unable to mount effective resistance with its diminished military.

The Yan king Xi (喜) recognized that the capital could not be held against the Qin assault. Rather than awaiting capture or execution, he chose to flee. According to the historical accounts, the king abandoned the capital and withdrew to the northeastern region of Liaodong (遼東), which was separated from the main Chinese heartland by mountainous terrain. This retreat was an attempt to preserve the Yan royal family and to maintain some base of resistance in the remote northeast.

Wang Ben pursued the fleeing Yan king, following the retreating forces across the mountains. The campaign in Liaodong was a difficult operation, as the terrain favored the defenders and the Qin forces were operating far from their base. However, the Qin army was eventually successful, and in 226 BCE, the Yan king Xi (喜) was captured and executed. The capital of Ji (薊) had already fallen, and with the death of the king, Yan ceased to exist as an independent state.

## The Suicide of the Prince of Yan at Ji

However, the elimination of the Yan king did not end all resistance. A member of the Yan royal family, known as the Prince of Yan (燕太子), escaped the initial conquest and sought to rally opposition to Qin rule. This prince, whose name is given in some sources as Tian (田) or in others simply as the Prince (太子), became the focus of resistance in the former Yan territories.

The Prince of Yan initially attempted to organize a guerrilla campaign against Qin rule. He gathered support among the local population and among nobles who remained loyal to the Yan dynasty. For a time, this resistance posed a significant challenge to Qin administrators, who struggled to maintain control over the restive population.

However, Qin forces under Wang Ben eventually tracked down the Prince of Yan at the city of Yi (衍), also identified as a location in the Liaodong region. The final engagement was decisive: the prince's forces were defeated, and the last major resistance in Yan territory was eliminated. According to some accounts, the Prince of Yan chose to commit suicide rather than be captured, ending his life in a final act of defiance (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

The death of the Prince of Yan marked the effective end of organized resistance in the former Yan territories. The Qin now faced the task of consolidating their control over the northeastern region and integrating it into the administrative structure of the empire. This process would take time, and the region would remain somewhat unstable in the years following the conquest.

## The Strategic Position After Yan's Fall

The fall of Yan had important strategic consequences for the unification campaign. With Yan eliminated, Qin now controlled the northeastern approaches to the Chinese heartland. The former Yan territories, including the strategically important Liaodong peninsula, were incorporated into the Qin state. This expansion brought Qin into closer contact with the peoples of the Korean peninsula and the Manchurian frontier, extending the reach of Chinese civilization into these regions.

The conquest of Yan also completed Qin's encirclement of the remaining state of Qi. With Yan gone, Qi was now surrounded on three sides by Qin-controlled territory, with only the sea to the east providing any separation. The strategic position of Qi had become untenable, and the final confrontation between Qin and Qi was now only a matter of time.

## The State of Qi on the Eve of Conquest

Qi occupied the easternmost position among the Warring States, controlling the fertile Shandong Peninsula and the surrounding regions. The state had been a major power in the fourth century BCE, but it had experienced a long decline that had left it unable to resist the rising power of Qin. By the time of the unification campaigns, Qi was a shadow of its former greatness, lacking the military capacity and the political cohesion to mount effective resistance.

The internal politics of Qi had contributed to its weakness. The state had been ruled by a series of kings who had proved unable to adapt to the changing strategic environment. The Qi military, while not insignificant, was no match for the professional forces of Qin. Most importantly, the Qi court had lost the will to resist, and many influential figures advocated surrender as the only realistic option.

This decline was reflected in Qin's assessment of the campaign. Unlike the campaigns against Chu, Zhao, or even Yan, the conquest of Qi was expected to be relatively easy. The strategic and political conditions favored a rapid resolution, and Qin prepared accordingly.

## The Campaign Against Qi (221 BCE)

The final campaign of unification began in 221 BCE, when Qin forces invaded Qi from the west. The campaign was led by the general Xin (新), though the historical sources provide limited details about his identity and background. What is clear is that the campaign was conducted with overwhelming force and with the expectation of a quick victory.

The Qi forces offered minimal resistance. The state's military, demoralized and outmatched, was unable to mount effective opposition to the Qin advance. The capital of Linzi (臨淄), one of the largest and most prosperous cities in the Warring States world, fell without a significant battle. The Qi king, whose title was Jian (建), was captured and executed, and the state was annexed into the Qin empire.

The conquest of Qi was remarkable for its speed and relative bloodlessness. Unlike the campaigns against Chu and Zhao, which had involved major battles and prolonged sieges, the Qi campaign was essentially a triumphal procession. The state simply collapsed before the Qin advance, reflecting the extent of its internal weakness and the psychological impact of Qin's earlier victories.

## The Diplomatic Surrender of Qi

The ease of the Qi conquest was not merely a matter of military superiority; it also reflected a deliberate strategy of diplomatic pressure. In the years preceding the final campaign, Qin had cultivated relationships with influential figures in the Qi court, encouraging them to advocate surrender rather than resistance. This diplomatic offensive was designed to weaken Qi's will to fight and to create internal divisions that would facilitate the conquest.

The effectiveness of this strategy became apparent as the Qin forces advanced. Rather than rallying to the defense of their king, many Qi nobles and officials simply accepted the new order. The surrender was almost bloodless, with the Qin forces meeting resistance only from a few isolated garrisons that were quickly overwhelmed.

This diplomatic approach was consistent with Qin's broader strategy of minimizing the costs of conquest wherever possible. By encouraging surrender and exploiting divisions within the enemy state, Qin was able to achieve its objectives without the massive casualties that would have attended a prolonged campaign. The result was the most efficient of all the major conquests, accomplished with minimal disruption to the existing social order.

## The Completion of Unification (221 BCE)

The conquest of Qi in 221 BCE marked the completion of the unification of China under Qin rule. All of the major Warring States had now been eliminated, and the entire Chinese heartland was under the control of a single ruler. The era of division and competition that had characterized the previous two and a half centuries had come to an end.

The news of the final victory was received with great excitement at the Qin court. Ying Zheng, now the ruler of a unified empire, took the title of Shi Huangdi (始皇帝), the First Emperor, signifying the beginning of a new imperial order. The title was carefully chosen to indicate that this was not merely another dynasty but a fundamentally new form of political organization, one that would serve as a model for all future emperors.

The territorial extent of the new empire was vast, encompassing the heartland of ancient China from the eastern coast to the western frontiers. The former territories of Chu, Zhao, Wei, Yan, and Qi, as well as the smaller states that had been absorbed along the way, were now integrated into a single political entity. The administrative machinery of the Qin state, which had been refined over generations, was now applied to the governance of this unprecedented realm.

## The Fate of the Vanquished

The treatment of the conquered states followed the established Qin pattern. The rulers of Yan and Qi were executed, their royal families scattered or eliminated, and their territories placed under the administration of Qin commanderies. The noble families of both states were stripped of their privileges and either integrated into the Qin system or, in some cases, exiled to remote regions.

However, the policy of elimination was not always complete. In some cases, branches of the royal families were permitted to survive, either because they had cooperated with Qin or because their elimination was deemed unnecessary. The descendants of the conquered royal houses would persist in the population, and in the chaos following the fall of Qin, some would emerge as leaders of restoration movements.

The generals who had led the campaigns were richly rewarded. Wang Ben, who had commanded the Yan campaign, was ennobled and given extensive estates. His father Wang Jian, though elderly, received honors for his role in the conquests. The military leadership of Qin had proven decisive in the unification, and the commanders were recognized accordingly.

## Historical Perspectives on the Final Campaigns

The final campaigns against Yan and Qi have been the subject of various historical interpretations. The traditional Chinese historiography emphasized the contrast between Yan's desperate resistance and Qi's passive surrender, drawing moral lessons about the importance of courage and loyalty in the face of conquest. The suicide of the Prince of Yan was portrayed as an example of noble sacrifice, while the Qi surrender was seen as a sign of degeneracy and moral failure.

Modern scholars have adopted more analytical approaches, examining the campaigns within the context of the broader strategic and political developments of the period. The ease of the Qi conquest has been attributed to a combination of factors: the internal weaknesses of the Qi state, the psychological impact of Qin's earlier victories, and the effectiveness of Qin's diplomatic offensive. The Yan's resistance, in contrast, has been seen as a reflection of the state's greater military capacity and its determination to fight despite overwhelming odds.

The completion of unification in 221 BCE has been interpreted in various ways by historians. Some have emphasized the transformative character of the event, viewing it as a fundamental turning point in Chinese history that created the framework for two millennia of imperial government. Others have stressed the continuities between the Qin empire and the earlier Zhou system, noting that many Qin administrative practices had precedents in the Warring States period.

## Conclusion

The campaigns against Yan and Qi brought the Warring States era to its conclusion. The conquest of Yan, though it required sustained military effort, eliminated the last major resistance in the northeast. The diplomatic and military pressure on Qi achieved a virtually bloodless surrender, completing the unification of China under Qin rule. The era of division and competition that had characterized Chinese politics for centuries was over, and a new imperial order had begun.

Yet the completion of unification was not the end of the story. The challenges of governing a vast empire would prove to be immense, and the methods that had enabled Qin to conquer would prove inadequate to the task of maintaining control. Within a few short years, the empire would begin to unravel, and the forces of reaction would sweep away the Qin order. But that is a story for another chapter.

For now, it is enough to recognize the magnitude of what had been achieved. In the space of just a few years, Qin had destroyed all of the major Warring States and created the first unified empire in Chinese history. The political map of the Chinese world had been transformed beyond recognition, and a new chapter in the history of civilization had begun.

## The Legacy of the Unification

The unification of China under Qin in 221 BCE represented one of the most significant turning points in world history. The creation of a centralized empire, governed by a uniform system of laws and administration, established a model that would be emulated and adapted by subsequent Chinese dynasties for over two millennia. The Qin unification also had profound implications for the development of Chinese culture, society, and identity, creating the foundation for the emergence of a distinctive Chinese civilization.

However, the unification was also the product of a particular historical moment, shaped by the specific circumstances of the Warring States period and the character of the Qin state. The methods that had enabled Qin to conquer—the ruthless application of military force, the elimination of rival elites, and the imposition of uniform administration—would prove to be both the strengths and the weaknesses of the new empire. Within years of the unification, the empire would begin to fracture, as the forces of resistance and the contradictions of Qin rule led to its collapse. Yet the memory of the unified empire would persist, serving as an inspiration and a model for all subsequent Chinese rulers.

### Chapter Notes

The primary source for the final campaigns is Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), which provides the basic narrative of the Yan and Qi conquests. However, the *Shiji* accounts are relatively brief, and many details of the campaigns remain unclear.

The identity of the generals who commanded the campaigns has been the subject of scholarly discussion. Wang Ben's role in the Yan campaign is well-established, but the identity of the commander of the Qi campaign is less certain. Some sources mention a general named Xin (新), but this may be a transcription error or an alternative name for a known commander.

The chronology of the campaigns is generally clear, with the Yan campaign beginning in 226 BCE and the Qi campaign concluding in 221 BCE. However, the precise sequence of events within these years is sometimes uncertain.

The suicide of the Prince of Yan is recorded in the *Shiji*, though the details are limited. Some scholars have questioned whether the prince died by suicide or was captured and executed, but the traditional account has been widely accepted.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary historical source for the Yan and Qi campaigns.
- *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), trans. J.I. Crump (San Francisco: Chinese Materials Center, 1985). Contains diplomatic and strategic material relevant to the period.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of Qin's conquest campaigns and the creation of the empire.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the Warring States period and the transition to empire.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Discussion of the political transformations of the period.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Detailed study of Qin's unification and the role of key figures.
- Yuri Pines, *Envisioning Eternal Empire: Chinese Political Thought of the Warring States Era* (Honolulu: University of Hawai'i Press, 2009). Discussion of political thought during the period of unification.

---


# Chapter 16: The Man Who Won the War — General Wang Jian

Among the great generals of ancient China, few achieved the military success and historical prominence of Wang Jian (王翦). As the commander who led Qin to victory in the conquest of Chu, the largest and most formidable of the Warring States, Wang Jian played a decisive role in the unification of China. His career spanned decades of service to the Qin state, and his military achievements represented the culmination of Qin's long preparation for conquest. To understand how Qin achieved unification, one must understand not only the political and economic foundations of Qin's power but also the generals who translated that power into victory on the battlefield. Wang Jian was the greatest of these generals, and his campaigns against Chu and the northern campaigns against the Xiongnu established the patterns of Qin expansion that would shape Chinese history for centuries.

This chapter examines the life and career of Wang Jian, exploring his military strategies, his relationship with Ying Zheng, and his lasting legacy as the architect of Qin's greatest victories. Wang Jian's story is not merely one of military achievement; it is also a window into the nature of power and trust in the Qin empire, where even the most successful generals walked a careful path between victory and danger.

## The Making of a General

Wang Jian's origins are somewhat obscure in the historical record. According to Sima Qian's *Shiji*, he was born in the state of Qin itself, likely in the western region that had been the heartland of the Qin state for centuries. Unlike some of the great generals of the Warring States period, who came from humble backgrounds or from other states, Wang Jian was a native of Qin, and his career was intimately tied to the development of the Qin state (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

The period of Wang Jian's emergence as a military leader corresponded with the reign of King Zhaoxiang (秦昭襄王) and the subsequent reigns that brought Qin to the brink of unification. During this period, the Qin military was transformed from a feudal levies-based force into a professional army capable of sustained campaigns far from its home territories. Wang Jian came of age within this system, rising through the ranks to become one of its most distinguished officers.

The transformation of the Qin military was closely linked to the Legalist reforms that had reshaped Qin society. Under the guidance of ministers such as Shang Yang and Li Si, Qin had developed a system that rewarded military achievement with land, titles, and official positions. This meritocratic system created powerful incentives for ambitious officers, and Wang Jian proved to be one of its greatest beneficiaries. His rise was not merely a matter of personal talent; it was also a product of the institutional structure that identified and promoted military talent.

## Early Campaigns and the Path to Prominence

Wang Jian's early military career is not well documented in the surviving sources, but it is clear that he had established himself as a significant figure in the Qin military by the middle of the third century BCE. The historical records indicate that he participated in several campaigns during the reign of King Zhaoxiang and King Xiaowen, gaining experience and reputation in the wars against the other Warring States.

One of Wang Jian's earliest documented campaigns was the campaign against Zhao in 270 BCE, during the major Qin-D Zhao war. This conflict, which lasted for several years, saw Qin make significant territorial gains at Zhao's expense. Wang Jian's role in this campaign, while not specified in detail in the sources, would have provided him with valuable experience in large-scale warfare against a formidable opponent.

The campaigns against Han and Wei in the 260s and 250s BCE also featured Wang Jian's service. These campaigns, which resulted in the elimination of Han as a significant power and the weakening of Wei, demonstrated the growing military superiority of Qin over its rivals. Wang Jian's participation in these campaigns established his reputation as one of Qin's most reliable commanders.

By the time of King Ying Zheng's accession to the throne, Wang Jian had become one of the senior generals in the Qin military. His experience, combined with his demonstrated competence, made him a natural choice for the most challenging campaigns that lay ahead. The conquest of Chu would be his greatest test.

## The Conquest of Chu (228-223 BCE)

The campaign against Chu was the largest and most ambitious military operation undertaken by Qin during the unification wars. Chu was not merely the largest of the Warring States in terms of territory; it was also a powerful state with a rich cultural tradition and significant military resources. The conquest of Chu would require not only superior force but also careful planning and strategic sophistication. Wang Jian was equal to the challenge.

The campaign began in 228 BCE, following the completion of the conquest of Zhao. The initial phases of the campaign involved the movement of massive Qin forces to the southern front, where they would confront the Chu army. According to the *Shiji*, Wang Jian led an army of 600,000 men into Chu territory, a number that has been debated by modern scholars but that clearly indicates the scale of the operation (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

The first major engagement of the campaign was the Battle of Chen (陳), which took place in 224 BCE. The Chu forces, commanded by Xiang Yan (項燕), one of the most capable generals of the period, initially offered determined resistance. However, the Qin forces under Wang Jian were able to defeat the Chu army in a decisive engagement that shattered the main Chu field army. Xiang Yan was killed in the battle, and the Chu forces collapsed in the aftermath.

Following the victory at Chen, Wang Jian's forces advanced rapidly into Chu territory. The Qin army marched on the Chu capital of Ying (郢), which fell in 223 BCE. With the capture of the capital and the destruction of the main Chu armies, the state of Chu ceased to exist as an independent entity. The territories of Chu were incorporated into the Qin state, creating the largest commandery in the new empire.

The speed of the Chu conquest was remarkable. In just a few years, Wang Jian had accomplished what many had thought impossible: the destruction of the state that had long been considered Qin's most dangerous rival. The victory was a testament to both Wang Jian's military skill and the overwhelming power of the Qin military machine.

## Strategic Insight and Adaptability

What made Wang Jian an exceptional general was not merely his ability to win battles but his capacity for strategic thinking and adaptation to different enemies. The conquest of Chu required different methods than the campaigns against the northern states, and Wang Jian demonstrated a keen understanding of how to adjust his approach to the specific circumstances of each campaign.

In the Chu campaign, Wang Jian faced an enemy that fought differently from the Zhao or Wei forces he had encountered in earlier campaigns. Chu's military was characterized by its large numbers and its use of different tactical approaches, including the deployment of war chariots and infantry in combined arms formations. Wang Jian's response was to emphasize the strengths of the Qin infantry, which had been trained and equipped for precisely this kind of engagement.

The *Zhangu Ce* (*Strategies of the Warring States*) preserves some of Wang Jian's strategic thinking, revealing a general who was not merely a battlefield commander but also a sophisticated strategist. Wang Jian understood the importance of logistics, the need to maintain supply lines over long distances, and the value of political intelligence in planning campaigns. His success was not simply a matter of bringing more troops to the field; it was the result of careful preparation and execution.

Wang Jian also demonstrated adaptability in his approach to different types of enemies. When called upon to conduct campaigns in the north against the Xiongnu nomads, he adjusted his methods to account for the mobility and guerrilla tactics of the nomadic forces. This ability to adapt would prove crucial in establishing Qin's dominance over the northern frontier.

## The Northern Campaigns and Pacification

Following the conquest of Chu, Wang Jian was called upon to conduct another critical campaign: the pacification of the northern frontier. The Xiongnu confederation posed a significant threat to the Qin heartland, and the new empire needed to secure its northern borders before it could fully consolidate its gains. Wang Jian was given command of the forces assigned to this task.

The northern campaigns involved campaigns against the Xiongnu and other nomadic peoples in the region of Hetao (河套), the area north of the Ordos Desert. This terrain was vastly different from the plains of central China where Wang Jian had won his earlier victories, and the enemy was unlike any he had faced before. The Xiongnu were highly mobile horse-archers who could strike quickly and withdraw before the slower-moving Qin infantry could engage them.

Wang Jian's response to this challenge was characteristically pragmatic. He organized the Qin forces into mobile units capable of matching the Xiongnu's speed, and he conducted a sustained campaign of attrition against the nomadic forces. The *Shiji* records that Wang Jian's forces achieved significant victories over the Xiongnu, driving them north of the Yellow River and securing the northern frontier (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

The northern campaigns were not merely military operations; they were also political statements. By demonstrating Qin's ability to project power into the frontier regions, Wang Jian helped establish the legitimacy of the new empire and its claim to dominion over all of China. The conquest of the northern territories expanded the empire's borders and created a buffer zone that would protect the heartland from future incursions.

## Relationship with Ying Zheng

One of the most notable aspects of Wang Jian's career was his relationship with Ying Zheng, the man who would become Qin Shi Huang. This relationship was complex, marked by both trust and the careful management of political risk. In the authoritarian atmosphere of the Qin court, even the most successful generals needed to navigate carefully between loyalty and suspicion.

The historical sources present Wang Jian as a figure who understood the political dimensions of military command. He was careful to maintain a close relationship with Ying Zheng, seeking the king's confidence and support for his campaigns. At the same time, he was aware of the dangers that came with great military success, particularly in a political system where rivals could easily turn a general's victories into accusations of treachery.

A revealing anecdote from the *Shiji* illustrates this dynamic. Before departing for the Chu campaign, Wang Jian reportedly asked Ying Zheng for substantial rewards and honors, including land and wealth, to demonstrate that he was not a threat. This request, which might seem presumptuous, was in fact a carefully calculated political gesture. By showing that he was motivated by material rewards rather than political ambitions, Wang Jian sought to allay any suspicions that he might harbor designs on the throne (Sima Qian, *Shiji*).

This episode has been interpreted by modern scholars as evidence of the precarious position of military commanders in the Qin system. The Legalist philosophy that undergirded Qin governance was deeply suspicious of individual power, and even the most loyal servants could find themselves targets of political purges if they were perceived as threats. Wang Jian's behavior suggests that he understood this reality and navigated it with skill.

## Retirement and Later Recall

Despite his enormous contributions to Qin's success, Wang Jian's career was not without its periods of retirement and recall. In the later years of his life, the general appears to have withdrawn from active service, only to be called back when circumstances required his unique talents.

The reasons for Wang Jian's retirement are not entirely clear from the historical record. Some scholars have suggested that he may have been the victim of political maneuvering at court, where rivals sought to diminish the influence of the aging general. Others have suggested that Wang Jian himself chose to step back, either because of age or because he recognized the changing nature of the empire's needs.

Whatever the cause, Wang Jian's retirement was not permanent. When the northern frontier demanded attention and the Xiongnu threats intensified, Qin Shi Huang turned to the one general he trusted to handle the challenge. Wang Jian was recalled from retirement and given command of the northern campaigns, demonstrating the emperor's continued confidence in his abilities.

This pattern of retirement and recall was not uncommon for successful generals in Chinese history, but it was particularly significant in the Qin context. The emperor's willingness to rely on Wang Jian, even in his advanced years, was a testament to the general's unparalleled reputation and proven capabilities. No other commander had demonstrated the same ability to achieve victory against the most formidable enemies.

## Legacy and Historical Significance

Wang Jian's legacy extends far beyond his individual victories. His career exemplified the transformation of Chinese warfare during the Warring States period, as the feudal levies of earlier centuries gave way to professional armies capable of sustained campaigns. The methods he employed—the emphasis on training, logistics, and strategic planning—became the model for subsequent Chinese military operations.

The conquest of Chu, in particular, had profound implications for the future of China. By eliminating the largest and most culturally distinct of the Warring States, Wang Jian paved the way for the unification of the Chinese heartland. Without his victory, the creation of the Qin empire would have been impossible, or at least would have required many more years of costly warfare.

Wang Jian also established patterns of frontier defense that would persist for centuries. His campaigns against the Xiongnu created a template for how subsequent Chinese dynasties would deal with the nomadic peoples of the north. The Great Wall, which Qin Shi Huang began constructing following Wang Jian's northern campaigns, was a physical manifestation of the defensive strategies that Wang Jian had helped develop.

The historical assessment of Wang Jian has been generally positive across the centuries. Chinese historiography has celebrated him as one of the great generals of unification, alongside figures such as Bai Qi and Lian Po. His reputation as a strategic genius who could adapt to any enemy and any terrain has endured.

Modern scholars have emphasized the institutional context of Wang Jian's achievements. His victories were not merely the product of individual genius; they were the result of the Qin military system that had been developed over generations. Wang Jian was the beneficiary of this system, but he was also its greatest champion, demonstrating its effectiveness on the battlefield.

## Conclusion

Wang Jian stands as one of the pivotal figures in Chinese history. His military achievements—the conquest of Chu, the pacification of the north, and his contributions to the overall unification of China—changed the trajectory of Chinese civilization. In him, the ambitions of the Qin state found their perfect expression: a general of unmatched skill, strategic insight, and political wisdom.

Yet Wang Jian's story is also a reminder of the complexities of power in ancient China. Even the most successful generals operated within a political system that demanded loyalty and punished ambition. Wang Jian navigated these challenges with skill, maintaining the trust of his ruler while achieving victories that seemed impossible. His career offers a window into the dynamics of command and leadership in the age of unification.

The legacy of Wang Jian endures in the military traditions of China and in the collective memory of a nation. He was the man who won the war, and his victories created the foundation upon which the Chinese empire was built. In the story of Qin's rise to dominance, no figure is more central, and no achievement more remarkable.

### Chapter Notes

The primary source for Wang Jian's career is Sima Qian's *Shiji*, which provides the main narrative of his campaigns. However, the *Shiji* account is sometimes brief and lacks detail, particularly for Wang Jian's early career. The *Zhangu Ce* (*Strategies of the Warring States*) provides additional strategic context, though its historicity is sometimes questioned.

The number of troops Wang Jian led against Chu—600,000 men as recorded in the *Shiji*—has been the subject of scholarly debate. Some modern historians argue that these numbers were exaggerated for dramatic effect, while others suggest that they reflect the massive scale of Qin military mobilization. The debate illustrates the challenges of using ancient sources for quantitative analysis.

Wang Jian's political relationship with Ying Zheng has been discussed by scholars such as Mark Edward Lewis, who has analyzed the dynamics of trust and suspicion in the Qin military system. The anecdote about Wang Jian requesting rewards before the Chu campaign is central to this discussion.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for Wang Jian's career and campaigns.
- *Strategies of the Warring States* (*Zhangu Ce*), trans. J.I. Crump (San Francisco: Chinese Materials Center, 1985). Contains strategic material attributed to Wang Jian.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Analysis of the Qin military system and the role of generals.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Discussion of military organization in the Warring States period.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Study of the political context of Qin's unification.
- Chang, K.C., *Shang Civilization* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 1980). Contextual background for the development of Chinese military traditions.
