# Chapter 19: The Emperor and His Title (221 BCE)

The year 221 BCE marked a watershed in Chinese political history. In that year, the king of Qin completed the conquest of the six remaining Warring States and proclaimed himself the first emperor of a unified China. This chapter examines the formal establishment of the Qin empire and the ideological significance of the new title that Ying Zheng adopted for himself. The ceremony in Xianyang, the creation of new imperial titulature, the rejection of the old title of "king" (王), and the adoption of the designation "First Emperor" (始皇帝) all reflected a conscious attempt to create a new political order fundamentally different from anything that had existed in Chinese history.

## The Ceremony at Xianyang

The formal proclamation of the empire took place at Xianyang, the capital of Qin, in 221 BCE. According to the traditional account preserved in the *Records of the Grand Historian*, the ceremony was an elaborate affair that symbolized the unprecedented nature of the new political order. The *Shiji* records that when the last of the six states—Qi—fell to Qin forces, the court ministers gathered to discuss the nature of the new political order that had been created (Sima Qian, *Shiji*, trans. Burton Watson).

The question before the court was not merely administrative but fundamentally ideological. The conquest of the six states had been completed, but what would this new political entity be called? What title would its ruler adopt? These were not trivial matters of protocol; they were questions about the nature of political authority itself.

The historical context for this moment was significant. For centuries, the Chinese world had been organized around the system of feudal lordship that had originated in the Zhou dynasty. The Zhou king had been the supreme ruler, granting titles of nobility to lords who governed territories in his name. Even as the power of the Zhou king declined and the Warring States system emerged, the ideological framework of feudal hierarchy persisted. Rulers of the various states still called themselves kings, a title that carried the implication of subordination to a higher authority—the Son of Heaven who ruled from the Zhou capital.

By 221 BCE, this framework had become obsolete. The Zhou dynasty had effectively ended, and no ruler claimed overlordship over the others. The six major states that remained after the conquest of Qi were all independent polities that recognized no superior. Yet the old terminology carried ideological baggage that did not fit the new reality. To call oneself a king, as the rulers of the Warring States had done, was to invoke a system of hierarchy that no longer existed in practice.

## The Rejection of the Kingly Title

The *Shiji* records a famous exchange between the Qin chancellor Li Si and other officials about the appropriate title for the new ruler. According to this account, someone proposed that the ruler adopt the title of "king" (王), which would have continued the nomenclature of the Warring States period. Li Si argued against this proposal, noting that the title of king had become devalued through overuse. "In ancient times, there was Yao, who gave the empire to Shun," Li Si reportedly said. "The three dynasties all followed each other, yet they did not emulate each other in their titles. Now the empire has been pacified, and the various states have been destroyed. The ruler's virtue has spread to all, and the achievements are equal to those of the ancient sages" (Sima Qian, *Shiji*, translation in Bodde, 1967).

Li Si's argument was political as much as historical. By suggesting that the new ruler's achievements were equal to those of the ancient sage-kings Yao and Shun, he was arguing for a title that would convey the unprecedented nature of the unification. The old title of king was insufficient because it had been used by many rulers of the Warring States, each of whom had governed only a portion of the Chinese world. The new ruler had accomplished something qualitatively different—he had unified the entire world under a single government.

The rejection of the kingly title was also a statement about the relationship between the ruler and his subjects. In the feudal system, kings had been at the apex of a hierarchy of lords and vassals. The new empire would have no such hierarchy; the emperor would stand alone at the summit of a centralized state. The title needed to express this radical break with the past.

## The Creation of the Imperial Title

The solution that Li Si proposed was the adoption of the title "皇帝" (huangdi), which is conventionally translated as "emperor" but which literally combines the characters for "august" (皇) and "sovereign" (帝). This title had no precedent in Chinese political history. The character 帝 had been used in ancient times to refer to the supreme deities of the Shang dynasty and later to legendary sage-rulers such as Yao and Shun. The character 皇, meaning "august" or "splendid," had been used as an honorific but never combined with 帝 in this way.

The new title was thus a creative synthesis that drew on ancient precedents while signifying something entirely new. By combining the characters for the highest divine authority (帝) and the most exalted human honorific (皇), the title "emperor" conveyed the ruler's claim to supreme authority over both the human world and the spiritual realm. It was a title that placed the Qin ruler above all previous Chinese monarchs, including the legendary figures of antiquity.

The *Shiji* records that Li Si proposed the title "huangdi" and that the king of Qin accepted this proposal. The full title adopted was "始皇帝" (shi huangdi), meaning "First Emperor." This title explicitly identified the new ruler as the first of a line of emperors who would inherit his authority. The use of the character "shi" (first, beginning) was significant—it acknowledged that future emperors would follow, while claiming for the first ruler a special status as the founder of the imperial line.

## The Ideological Foundations of Imperial Rule

The adoption of the imperial title was not merely a matter of nomenclature; it reflected a fundamental reconceptualization of political authority. The Qin empire represented a break with the feudal traditions that had dominated Chinese political life for centuries, and the imperial title was the symbolic expression of this break.

The feudal system had been based on a hierarchy of personal relationships. The Zhou king had granted lands to his lords, who in turn granted lands to their vassals. Authority flowed through these personal relationships, and the rights of each level in the hierarchy were defined by custom and tradition. The Legalist philosophy that had guided Qin's rise had always been critical of this feudal order, arguing that effective government required centralized authority rather than distributed power.

The imperial title expressed the centralization of authority in its very form. The emperor was not a king among kings, a first among equals; he was a singular figure whose authority extended directly to every corner of the empire. There were no intermediate lords between the emperor and his subjects, no feudal hierarchy to mediate the relationship between ruler and ruled.

The ideological foundation for this system drew on several intellectual traditions. The Legalist emphasis on law and centralized administration provided the practical framework. The ancient concept of the Mandate of Heaven (天命), which had originally been used to justify the rule of the Zhou dynasty over the Shang, was adapted to support the new imperial order. The emperor was seen as the recipient of Heaven's mandate to rule the unified empire, a mandate that could be withdrawn if the ruler failed to govern justly.

The doctrine of the Three Bonds (三纲) and the Five Constants (五常), which would later become central to Confucian orthodoxy, were still in the process of development during the Qin period. However, the imperial system created in 221 BCE established the political framework within which these ideas would eventually crystallize. The emperor became the pivot around which the entire social and political order revolved.

## The New Imperial Titulature

The creation of the imperial title was accompanied by a comprehensive reform of titulature throughout the empire. Just as the ruler needed a new title to reflect his unprecedented position, so too did the entire system of noble and official titles require revision to fit the new political order.

Under the feudal system, the hierarchy of noble titles had included the ranks of duke (公), marquis (侯), earl (伯), viscount (子), and baron (男). These titles had been inherited through aristocratic families and had carried with them rights to govern specific territories. The Qin conquest had abolished these feudal titles in the conquered states, and the new empire would not restore them.

Instead, the Qin empire created a new system of titles that was purely honorific. The highest ranks after the emperor were the three "Gong" (公) titles, which were granted to the most senior officials as a mark of distinction rather than as indicators of territorial authority. Below these were the ranks of Marquis (侯), which were granted for military or administrative achievements but did not carry feudal privileges.

This reform was consistent with the Legalist emphasis on merit over birth. By removing the hereditary element from noble titles, the Qin empire created a system in which advancement depended on service to the state rather than on the accidents of birth. The titles were rewards for achievement, not markers of inherited status.

The reform of titulature also extended to the language used to address the emperor. New terms of respect were created for use in official documents and court ritual. The emperor's commands were no longer simply "orders" (令) but "imperial decrees" (诏), a term that conveyed the sacred authority of the ruler. The emperor's words were recorded in special documents that were preserved as sacred texts, demonstrating the permanent authority of imperial utterances.

## The Symbolism of the Ceremony

The ceremony at Xianyang was rich with symbolic meaning. According to the *Shiji*, the new emperor ascended a special platform to receive the imperial seal, a jade symbol of authority that had been used by earlier rulers. The seal was inscribed with the words "Having received the Mandate from Heaven, may the emperor rule for ten thousand years" (受命于天，既寿永昌), a phrase that explicitly invoked the concept of the Mandate of Heaven while also expressing the hope for eternal rule.

The ceremony also included rituals that drew on the traditions of ancient Chinese kingship. The new emperor performed the suburban sacrifice (郊祭), a ritual that connected the ruler to the spiritual forces of the cosmos. This ritual, which had origins in the Shang and Zhou dynasties, was adapted to express the emperor's role as the intermediary between heaven and earth.

The location of the ceremony was itself significant. Xianyang had been the capital of Qin for generations, but the unification required a new relationship between the capital and the empire. The city was redesigned to reflect the imperial status of its ruler. According to historical accounts, construction began on a new palace complex that would dwarf all previous structures. The Epang Palace (阿房宫), whose construction would become one of the most notorious aspects of Qin's rule, was intended to symbolize the power and majesty of the new empire.

The ceremony at Xianyang thus established the template for imperial ritual that would persist throughout Chinese history. The elements introduced in 221 BCE—the new title, the imperial seal, the sacrificial rituals, the architectural program—would be adopted and adapted by every subsequent Chinese dynasty. The imperial system that Qin Shi Huang created became the standard form of Chinese government for the next two millennia.

## The Reaction of the Conquered States

The proclamation of the empire was met with a mixture of resignation and resistance among the peoples of the former Warring States. For centuries, the various states had maintained distinct identities, and the sudden imposition of unified rule was a profound disruption to established ways of life.

Some of the former ruling families of the conquered states were granted titles and stipends that allowed them to maintain a comfortable existence in the new order. This policy, which paralleled the earlier Qin practice of treating defeated rulers with a degree of magnanimity, was intended to secure the cooperation of the elite strata of the conquered societies. However, it also created a class of dispossessed nobles who had both the motive and the means to resist the new order.

Among the common people, the unification was initially received with a mixture of hope and fear. The end of decades of warfare was welcome, and the new empire promised a period of peace and stability. However, the harsh methods of Qin rule, which had been effective in conquering the states, proved difficult to maintain in peacetime. The heavy taxation, conscription for public works projects, and strict legal enforcement that characterized Qin administration generated resentment throughout the empire.

The ideological transformation required by the new imperial order was perhaps the most profound challenge. For centuries, loyalty had been to the individual state—to Qi, Chu, Yan, Wei, Zhao, or Han. The creation of the empire required the development of a new loyalty, to the emperor as an individual and to the imperial institution. This process of ideological transformation would take generations to complete and would remain incomplete throughout the Qin dynasty.

## The Legacy of the Imperial Title

The title "皇帝" that Qin Shi Huang adopted in 221 BCE became the standard designation for Chinese rulers for the next two thousand years. Although the Qin dynasty itself would collapse within years of its founder's death, the imperial title endured. Subsequent dynasties—Han, Wei, Jin, Sui, Tang, Song, Yuan, Ming, Qing—all claimed the title of emperor, acknowledging their debt to the system that Qin Shi Huang had created.

The endurance of the title is remarkable given the relatively brief duration of the Qin dynasty. It suggests that the imperial system, despite its flaws, fulfilled a deep need in Chinese political culture. The idea of a single supreme ruler, governing a unified empire from a centralized capital, became a fundamental assumption of Chinese political thought. Even when dynasties fell and the empire fragmented, the goal of reunification remained the dominant political aspiration.

The title also had implications for the relationship between ruler and ruled that extended beyond the Chinese world. The Chinese emperor claimed universal authority, not merely over the Chinese heartland but over all peoples who could be brought within the ambit of the imperial system. This claim to universal sovereignty would shape Chinese foreign relations for millennia, influencing interactions with neighboring states in East Asia and beyond.

## Conclusion

The year 221 BCE witnessed not merely the military conquest of the six Warring States but the creation of a new political order. The ceremony at Xianyang, the rejection of the old title of king, and the adoption of the unprecedented designation "皇帝" (emperor) all expressed a fundamental break with the feudal past. The new title drew on ancient concepts while signifying something entirely new—a centralized empire ruled by a single supreme authority.

The ideological foundations of this system drew on Legalist principles of centralized administration, the concept of the Mandate of Heaven, and the emerging ideas about the relationship between ruler and subject that would later be formalized in Confucian orthodoxy. The imperial title expressed the radical centralization of authority that distinguished the Qin empire from all previous Chinese polities.

The legacy of this moment extended far beyond the third century BCE. The imperial system that Qin Shi Huang created became the standard form of Chinese government, persisting in various forms until the early twentieth century. The title "皇帝" became synonymous with supreme political authority in the Chinese world. The decision made at Xianyang in 221 BCE thus shaped not only the immediate aftermath of the unification but the entire subsequent history of Chinese civilization.

### Chapter Notes

The traditional account of the adoption of the imperial title comes primarily from Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), written more than a century after the events described. Modern scholars recognize that this account may reflect later interpretations as much as contemporary records. The dialogue attributed to Li Si, in particular, has been analyzed as a retrospective justification for the imperial system rather than a record of actual historical statements.

The date of the proclamation has also been subject to scholarly debate. Some scholars have suggested that the ceremony may have occurred in 222 BCE rather than 221 BCE, though the latter date remains standard in most historical treatments.

The ideological significance of the imperial title has been a subject of extensive scholarly analysis. Mark Edward Lewis has argued that the title represented a fundamental reconceptualization of political authority that drew on both Legalist and ancient cosmological traditions. Other scholars have emphasized the practical political considerations that motivated the rejection of the kingly title.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the unification and the adoption of the imperial title.
- Han Feizi, *The Complete Works of Han Fei*, trans. W.K. Liao (London: Arthur Probsthain, 1959). Legalist philosophical texts that informed Qin political thought.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Analysis of the ideological foundations of the imperial system.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Detailed study of the unification and its ideological context.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the transformation from state to empire.
- Pines, Yuri, *The Foundations of Chinese History* (New York: Columbia University Press, 2009). Discussion of the political transformations of the Warring States and imperial periods.
- Kern, Martin, *The Stele Inscriptions of Ch'in Shih-huang: Text and Context in the Publishing of Empire* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2000). Analysis of the ideological representation of imperial authority.


---


# Chapter 20: The New Imperial Order

The proclamation of the empire in 221 BCE was only the first step in the creation of a new political order. Having conquered the six Warring States, Qin Shi Huang faced the daunting task of governing the unified territory. The solution that emerged was a comprehensive system of centralized administration that replaced the feudal structures of the past with a rationalized hierarchy of officials appointed by the central government. This chapter examines the administrative structure of the Qin empire, the abolition of feudal enfeoffment, and the establishment of the commandery (郡) and county (县) system that became the foundation of imperial governance.

## The Abolition of Feudal Enfeoffment

The most fundamental transformation of the Qin empire was the abolition of feudal enfeoffment. For centuries, the Chinese political system had been organized around the distribution of territory to noble families who governed their domains with considerable autonomy. The feudal lords collected taxes, administered justice, and raised armies within their territories, owing only nominal allegiance to the king who had granted them their titles. This system had provided the framework for political organization since the Zhou dynasty, and its abolition represented a radical break with the past.

The Qin conquest had already destroyed the independence of the major feudal states. The rulers of Qi, Chu, Yan, Zhao, Wei, and Han had been defeated, their capitals captured, and their territories incorporated into the Qin domain. However, the mere conquest of these states did not automatically produce a centralized administration. In previous transitions of power, the conquerors had typically preserved the existing feudal structures, granting conquered territories to members of the ruling family or to loyal followers who would govern them as vassals.

Qin Shi Huang and his advisors rejected this approach. The Legalist philosophy that had guided Qin's rise was fundamentally opposed to the distribution of power to semi-independent lords. Han Fei, the Legalist philosopher whose ideas had profoundly influenced Qin Shi Huang, had argued that the only effective form of government was one in which all authority flowed from a single central source. Feudalism, with its multiple centers of power, was inherently unstable because it created rivals who might challenge the ruler's authority.

The abolition of feudal enfeoffment was therefore both a political and an ideological decision. The conquered territories would not be granted as fiefs to noble families; they would be governed directly by officials appointed by the central government. The old aristocratic families of the conquered states would be removed from power, their lands confiscated, and their roles assumed by bureaucrats accountable to the emperor alone.

This policy was implemented with characteristic Qin ruthlessness. According to historical accounts, the former rulers of the six states were relocated to the capital region, where they could be kept under surveillance. Their noble titles were abolished, and they were granted stipends that allowed them to live in comfort but denied them any political authority. The aristocratic families of the conquered states faced similar treatment, their estates broken up and their privileges revoked.

The abolition of feudal enfeoffment was not without opposition. The aristocratic families of the conquered states had deep roots in their local communities, and their removal created a power vacuum that was difficult to fill. Moreover, the policy flew in the face of centuries of tradition, and many within the empire questioned the wisdom of abandoning the old ways. However, the Qin government pressed forward with its program of centralization, confident that the benefits of direct administration outweighed the costs of disruption.

## The Commandery System

The primary administrative innovation of the Qin empire was the establishment of the commandery (郡) system. The empire was divided into administrative units called commanderies, each of which was governed by a military commander and a civil administrator appointed by the central government. This system replaced the patchwork of feudal states with a uniform grid of administrative divisions.

The number of commanderies varied over time, but the initial division established 36 commanderies covering the entire empire. These commanderies were distributed across the former territories of the six states, ensuring that no region would have sufficient local identity to challenge imperial authority. The commandery boundaries were drawn without regard to historical boundaries, breaking the connection between geography and political identity that had characterized the Warring States system.

Each commandery was further divided into counties (县), creating a hierarchical structure of administration that extended from the capital to the smallest local unit. The counties were the basic units of imperial administration, responsible for tax collection, population registration, local security, and the maintenance of public order. The county magistrates were appointed by the commandery authorities, who in turn were appointed by the central government.

The commandery system represented a fundamental transformation in the relationship between ruler and territory. Under the feudal system, the land had been owned by lords who owed allegiance to a superior. Under the imperial system, the land was administered by officials who owed their positions to the emperor. The change was not merely semantic; it altered the very nature of political authority.

The commandery governors held significant powers within their jurisdictions. They were responsible for tax collection, which provided the revenue that sustained the imperial government and its military forces. They commanded the local militia, maintaining internal order and suppressing any signs of rebellion. They administered justice, applying the uniform legal code that the central government had promulgated throughout the empire.

However, the commandery governors did not have the autonomy that feudal lords had enjoyed. Their appointments were temporary, and they were subject to constant evaluation by the central government. The system of mutual surveillance that had been developed in Qin—where officials were required to report on each other's conduct—was extended throughout the empire, creating a network of accountability that minimized the risk of independent action.

## The Appointment of Administrators

The administration of the Qin empire depended on a corps of trained officials who served at the pleasure of the emperor. The recruitment and training of these officials was a major undertaking, requiring the development of new institutions and the codification of new standards of competence.

The Qin government had long maintained a system of merit-based selection that emphasized practical skills over hereditary status. This system was extended throughout the empire, creating a bureaucracy that was unprecedented in Chinese history. The officials who served as commandery governors, county magistrates, and subordinate administrators were selected on the basis of their abilities, not their birth.

The training of these officials began in the local schools that the Qin government established throughout the empire. These schools taught the legal code, administrative procedures, and the writing system that was essential for official business. Students who demonstrated competence were eligible for appointment to administrative positions, beginning their careers as assistants to senior officials and advancing as their abilities were recognized.

The merit-based system represented a profound change from the aristocratic recruitment that had characterized earlier periods. Under the Zhou dynasty and the Warring States, government positions had been the preserve of noble families, passed down from generation to generation. The Qin system opened these positions to talented individuals regardless of their social background, creating a bureaucracy that could draw on the full range of talent within the empire.

The officials who served in the Qin empire faced demanding requirements. They were expected to master the legal code, which was complex and frequently updated. They were required to maintain detailed records of their administration, including population registers, tax assessments, and judicial proceedings. And they were subject to rigorous evaluation, with promotions and demotions depending on their performance.

The system was not without its flaws. The emphasis on strict adherence to regulations sometimes produced inflexible administration that failed to respond to local conditions. The constant evaluation created incentives for officials to prioritize easily measurable outcomes over more subtle forms of good governance. And the removal of aristocratic families from positions of authority created resentment that would contribute to the empire's instability.

## The Elimination of Noble Titles

Alongside the abolition of feudal territories, the Qin government eliminated the noble titles that had accompanied them. The complex hierarchy of dukes, marquises, earls, viscounts, and barons that had characterized the feudal order was replaced by a simplified system of ranks that carried no territorial authority.

The Qin government did not eliminate the practice of granting titles entirely. It created a new category of noble ranks—known collectively as the "twenty ranks of nobility" (二十等爵)—that could be earned through military service or administrative achievement. These ranks conferred certain privileges, including exemption from corvée labor and preferential treatment in legal proceedings, but they did not carry the right to govern territories or to collect taxes from a defined domain.

The new noble ranks were purely personal honors, tied to the individual rather than to a territory or a family. When the holder of a rank died, the rank did not automatically pass to his heir; it had to be earned anew through service to the empire. This represented a fundamental break with the hereditary principle that had characterized the feudal system.

The elimination of hereditary nobility was one of the most controversial aspects of Qin rule. The aristocratic families of the former Warring States had deep social standing and cultural influence, and the loss of their political privileges was a profound disruption to their way of life. Many of these families would play important roles in the rebellions that eventually toppled the Qin dynasty, motivated at least in part by the desire to restore their lost privileges.

The Qin government justified its policy on both ideological and practical grounds. Ideologically, the Legalist philosophy held that hereditary privilege was inherently unjust and that government should be based on merit rather than birth. Practically, the concentration of authority in the central government required the elimination of alternative centers of power, and hereditary lords represented exactly such alternative centers.

## The Centralization of Authority

The commandery-county system was the administrative expression of a broader principle of centralization that characterized the Qin empire. All significant authority was concentrated in the capital at Xianyang, where the emperor and his close advisors made decisions that affected every corner of the realm.

The central government was organized around the Three Lords and Nine Ministers (三公九卿), a hierarchical structure that divided administrative responsibilities among specialized departments. The Three Lords—the Chancellor (丞相), the Imperial Clerk (御史大夫), and the Commander-in-Chief (太尉)—were the highest-ranking officials, advising the emperor on matters of policy and overseeing the work of the Nine Ministers who managed specific areas of government.

The Nine Ministers (九卿) were responsible for various aspects of imperial administration: ritual matters, revenue, justice, the imperial household, agriculture, commerce, defense, and other essential functions. Each minister headed a department staffed by trained officials, creating a bureaucracy that could manage the complex affairs of the empire.

This structure represented a significant advance in administrative capacity. The specialization of functions allowed for greater expertise in each area of government, while the hierarchical organization ensured coordination among departments. The system was not perfect—the boundaries between departments were sometimes unclear, and the concentration of authority in the center created bottlenecks in decision-making—but it was far more effective than the fragmented administration of the feudal period.

The centralization of authority extended to the legal system as well. The Qin legal code, which had been developed over the preceding centuries, was promulgated throughout the empire, replacing the varied legal traditions of the former states. Everyone, from the highest official to the humblest peasant, was subject to the same laws, administered by the same courts, and punished according to the same standards. This uniformity was a hallmark of Qin administration and a source of both its strength and its notoriety.

## The Challenge of Governing a Unified Empire

The administrative system established in 221 BCE represented an ambitious attempt to govern a unified empire through centralized bureaucracy. However, the system faced numerous challenges that would test its viability from the beginning.

The most fundamental challenge was the sheer size of the territory to be governed. The Qin empire encompassed a vast area, from the mountainous regions of the southwest to the plains of the east and the frontiers of the north. Communication between the capital and the distant commanderies was slow, and the central government had limited ability to monitor the actions of its local officials. The infrastructure of roads and canals that the Qin government would develop was intended to address this problem, but it could never fully overcome the geographic constraints.

The cultural diversity of the empire presented another challenge. The former Warring States had developed distinct regional identities, and the imposition of uniform administration required the suppression of these identities. The standardization of writing, law, and administration was intended to create a unified culture, but it also generated resentment among peoples who valued their distinct traditions.

The economic demands of the empire were also significant. The maintenance of the bureaucracy, the construction of public works, and the defense of the frontiers required substantial resources. The tax system that the Qin government implemented was efficient but burdensome, extracting a high proportion of agricultural production to fund imperial activities. This burden would become increasingly difficult to bear as the empire aged.

Finally, the ideological challenge of legitimating the new order should not be underestimated. The feudal system had been justified by centuries of tradition and the concept of the Mandate of Heaven, but the Qin empire represented such a radical break with the past that traditional justifications were inadequate. The Legalist philosophy that justified Qin rule was controversial, and many intellectuals remained skeptical of its premises. The regime would need to develop new ideological foundations if it was to endure.

## The Legacy of the Administrative System

Despite the challenges it faced, the commandery-county system established by the Qin empire became the model for subsequent Chinese dynasties. When the Han dynasty restored imperial rule after the collapse of Qin, it retained the basic structure of centralized administration, though it modified certain features to address the problems that had contributed to Qin's downfall.

The commandery system would persist, in various forms, for over two millennia. The number of commanderies and their boundaries would change with the vicissitudes of dynasties, but the fundamental principle—that the empire should be governed through a hierarchy of appointed officials rather than feudal lords—remained constant. The system was so deeply embedded in Chinese political culture that its origins in the Qin conquest were often forgotten.

The abolition of feudal enfeoffment was also a lasting transformation. Although later dynasties would sometimes grant territories to members of the imperial family or to meritorious officials, these grants were always conditional and revocable. The hereditary feudal lords of the Zhou period never returned, replaced by a class of officials whose authority derived from appointment rather than inheritance.

The system of merit-based recruitment that Qin developed would also persist, though it would undergo significant evolution. The imperial examination system, which would not emerge until the Sui dynasty centuries later, was in many ways the fulfillment of the principle that government should be based on merit rather than birth. The Qin empire had established the foundation; later dynasties would build upon it.

## Conclusion

The administrative transformation of 221 BCE represented one of the most significant changes in Chinese political history. The abolition of feudal enfeoffment, the establishment of the commandery-county system, the appointment of administrators based on merit, and the centralization of authority all reflected a vision of government that differed fundamentally from the feudal traditions of the past.

The new imperial order was not merely a larger version of the Qin state that had conquered the Warring States; it was a qualitatively different political entity. The empire was governed through a rationalized bureaucracy, administered by officials appointed on the basis of merit rather than inherited status. The territory was divided into uniform administrative units that reported to a central government rather than to local lords. And all authority flowed from the emperor, who stood at the apex of a hierarchical structure of administration.

This system faced significant challenges, and it would prove unable to sustain the Qin dynasty indefinitely. However, the basic framework that Qin Shi Huang established would endure for over two millennia, influencing the political organization of China through successive dynasties. The imperial order created in 221 BCE thus represented not merely a particular historical configuration but a lasting transformation in the nature of Chinese government.

### Chapter Notes

The administrative system of the Qin empire has been the subject of extensive scholarly analysis. The traditional view, derived from Sima Qian's *Records of the Grand Historian*, presents the commandery-county system as a comprehensive and well-ordered structure. Modern scholarship has emphasized the complexity and messiness of the actual administration, noting that the system was implemented unevenly and that local variations persisted.

The number of commanderies established in 221 BCE is a matter of some scholarly dispute. The traditional figure of 36 is well-attested in early sources, but some scholars have suggested that this number was achieved gradually rather than all at once.

The relationship between the central government and the local administrations has also been debated. Some scholars emphasize the capacity of the Qin state to monitor and control its officials, while others have argued that the distance from the capital limited effective oversight.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the administrative structure of the Qin empire.
- *The Book of Han* (*Han Shu*), trans. Homer H. Dubs (New York: Columbia University Press, 1938). Provides additional information on the early imperial administration.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of imperial administration.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Detailed study of Qin administration and its key figures.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of the transformation of Chinese administration.
- Qian, Mu, *Qin Han zhi ji* (Beijing: Zhonghua Shuju, 2002). Chinese scholarly analysis of the Qin-Han transition.
- Ch'ien, Ssu-ma, *The Grand Secretary of the Ch'in Dynasty*, in *Chinese Government in Ming Times*, ed. Charles O. Hucker (New York: Columbia University Press, 1969). Analysis of the bureaucratic structure.
- Wilhelm, Hellmut, *From Myth to Myth: The Case of the Ch'in Dynasty*, in *Chinese Civilization and Bureaucracy*, ed. Arthur F. Wright (New Haven: Yale University Press, 1964). Discussion of the ideological foundations of Qin administration.

# Chapter 21: Standardization — One Empire, One System

The unification of China under Qin Shi Huang in 221 BCE was not merely a political conquest; it was an ambitious project of cultural and social transformation. The new empire required not only a new political order but also a new set of common standards that would allow the diverse populations of the former Warring States to function as a single political community. This chapter examines the comprehensive standardization program that the Qin government implemented, encompassing weights, measures, currency, writing, roads, and law. These reforms represented a conscious attempt to create "one empire, one system," and their impact on Chinese civilization would endure for millennia.

## The Ideology of Standardization

The drive to standardize the empire drew on both practical and ideological motivations. From a practical standpoint, the unification of weights, measures, currency, and writing facilitated commerce, administration, and communication across the vast territory of the empire. A merchant traveling from the eastern coast to the western frontier could now use the same scales, measures, and coins throughout his journey. An official dispatched to a distant commandery could read the same characters and understand the same legal code as his colleagues in the capital.

However, the standardization program was more than a matter of administrative convenience. It reflected a philosophical commitment to uniformity that was central to the Legalist vision of government. The Legalist thinkers who shaped Qin policy believed that a well-ordered state required the application of uniform standards throughout its territory. Variations in practice created opportunities for corruption and confusion, while standardization ensured clarity, predictability, and control.

The ideology of standardization also served political purposes. By replacing the distinctive traditions of the former states with common standards, the Qin government was attempting to create a unified imperial culture that would supersede the parochial identities of the Warring States. The standardization of writing, in particular, was intended to create a shared medium of communication that would foster a sense of common identity among the diverse populations of the empire.

## The Unification of Writing

Perhaps the most consequential of the standardization programs was the unification of the writing system. Before the Qin unification, the various states had developed distinct scripts that, while sharing a common origin, had diverged significantly over the centuries. A document written in the state of Chu might be nearly incomprehensible to a reader from Zhao, and the differences between the scripts created serious obstacles to communication and administration.

The standardization of writing was associated with the figure of Li Si, the Qin chancellor who played a central role in shaping the imperial system. According to historical accounts, Li Si developed a standardized script—known as the small seal script (小篆)—that was adopted as the official writing system throughout the empire. This script was simpler than the variants used in the various states, facilitating both learning and writing.

The new script was promulgated throughout the empire through the establishment of writing standards and the distribution of model texts. Official documents were required to use the standardized script, and local officials were trained in its use. The penalties for using non-standard forms were severe, creating strong incentives for compliance.

The standardization of writing had profound consequences that extended far beyond the immediate administrative benefits. By creating a common medium of communication, the Qin government facilitated the spread of ideas and information throughout the empire. The standardized script also created the foundation for a shared literary culture that would develop in the Han dynasty and subsequent periods.

The script that Li Si developed eventually gave way to other forms—the clerical script (隶书) of the Han period and the regular script (楷书) of later centuries—but the basic principles of the Chinese writing system remained constant. The characters that the Qin government standardized in the third century BCE are essentially recognizable to modern Chinese readers, a testament to the enduring impact of the standardization program.

The unification of writing was also significant for the transmission of knowledge. Before the standardization, texts had to be copied by scribes who were familiar with the particular script of the original, a process that was time-consuming and prone to error. With a standardized script, texts could be copied accurately throughout the empire, facilitating the preservation and dissemination of knowledge.

## Weights and Measures

The standardization of weights and measures was another essential component of the Qin program. Before the unification, each state had maintained its own systems of measurement, creating confusion in trade and complicating the calculation of taxes. A measure of grain that was valid in one state might be significantly different from the equivalent measure in another, and the discrepancies created opportunities for fraud and dispute.

The Qin government established uniform standards for all weights and measures throughout the empire. These standards were promulgated from the capital and enforced through the official system of verification. Officials were required to ensure that the weights and measures used in their jurisdictions conformed to the imperial standards, and violations were punished as serious offenses.

The basic unit of length was the chi (尺), approximately 23 centimeters in modern measurement. This unit was subdivided into smaller units and aggregated into larger ones, creating a coherent system of linear measurement. The standard chi was reproduced in bronze and distributed throughout the empire, providing physical objects against which local measures could be checked.

Volume was measured in the dou (斗), a unit that was similarly standardized and verified. The dou was the basic unit for measuring grain and other commodities, and its standardization facilitated the collection of taxes in kind. The uniformity of measures meant that the tax burden was distributed more equitably, though the overall level of taxation remained high.

Weight was measured in the jin (斤), a unit that would persist in Chinese usage for over two millennia. The standardization of weight was essential for the proper functioning of markets, where goods were bought and sold by weight. The uniformity of standards meant that a merchant could trust that the weight indicated by his scale was accurate, facilitating commerce across the empire.

The standardization of weights and measures also had important administrative implications. The calculation of taxes, the allocation of resources, and the provisioning of the military all depended on accurate measurement. By establishing uniform standards, the Qin government created the foundation for efficient administration throughout the empire.

## Currency

The unification of currency was another essential element of the standardization program. Before the Qin conquest, the various states had minted their own coins, using different metals, different shapes, and different denominational systems. The coins of Qi, for example, were not interchangeable with the coins of Chu, and the diversity of currencies created significant obstacles to inter-state trade.

The Qin government established a uniform currency system throughout the empire. The standard coin was the ban liang (半两), a round bronze coin with a square hole in the center, weighing half a liang (approximately 8 grams). This coin was minted at the capital and distributed throughout the empire, replacing the varied currencies of the former states.

The ban liang was not the first round coin in Chinese history—the Warring States states had already begun minting round coins as a replacement for the spade money that had characterized earlier periods—but its uniform adoption throughout the empire was unprecedented. The coin's design was simple and efficient, and its weight was standardized to ensure consistency.

In addition to the standard coin, the Qin government also regulated the use of precious metals as currency. Gold, in the form of ingots known as "yi" (镒), was used for large transactions, while bronze coins served for everyday commerce. This bimetallic system provided flexibility while maintaining the uniformity that the empire required.

The standardization of currency had immediate economic benefits. Merchants could now conduct business throughout the empire without having to exchange one currency for another. The uniform coinage facilitated long-distance trade and contributed to the economic integration of the formerly separate states.

However, the currency system also faced challenges. The ban liang coin was relatively heavy, making it inconvenient for small transactions. The government attempted to address this by minting smaller denominations, but the supply of small coins was often inadequate. Moreover, the high taxes that the Qin government imposed meant that much of the available coinage flowed to the capital, creating shortages in the provinces.

## The Legal Code

The standardization of law was perhaps the most controversial aspect of the Qin program. The various Warring States had developed distinct legal traditions, and the imposition of a uniform legal code throughout the empire required the replacement of local practices with standardized regulations. The Qin legal code was rigorous, comprehensive, and notorious for its severity.

The codification of Qin law had begun before the unification, under the direction of Li Si and other Legalist reformers. The resulting code was a comprehensive system of regulations covering all aspects of social and economic life. The laws addressed matters ranging from agriculture and commerce to family relations and criminal justice, creating a comprehensive framework for the regulation of human behavior.

The fundamental principle of Qin law was the equality of all before the legal code. Unlike the legal systems of the feudal period, which had treated aristocrats differently from commoners, Qin law applied the same standards to everyone. An official who committed a crime was subject to the same punishment as a peasant who committed the same offense.

The punishments prescribed by Qin law were severe. The code included a wide range of corporal punishments, including mutilation and death, as well as punishments that affected entire families. The principle of collective punishment meant that the relatives of a criminal could be held responsible for his actions, creating strong incentives for conformity.

The harshness of Qin law has been widely criticized, both in ancient times and in the modern period. Confucian scholars, in particular, viewed the Legalist legal system as morally bankrupt, arguing that it relied on coercion rather than moral education. The rebellions that eventually toppled the Qin dynasty were motivated in part by resentment against the harsh legal system.

However, it is important to recognize the functions that the legal system served. The certainty of punishment created incentives for compliance, and the uniformity of the legal code ensured that individuals knew what was expected of them. The system was designed to maintain order and discipline, and in this it was largely successful.

The Qin legal code would not survive the dynasty, but many of its principles would persist in subsequent legal systems. The idea that law should be uniform and that everyone should be subject to the same standards was a lasting contribution to Chinese political culture, even if the specific provisions of the Qin code were modified or abandoned.

## Roads and Communication

The maintenance of communication across the vast territory of the empire was essential for effective administration. The Qin government therefore invested heavily in the construction of roads and the development of communication systems that would allow information to flow between the capital and the most distant regions.

The most famous of the Qin road projects was the straight road (直道) that connected the capital at Xianyang to the northern frontier. This road, which was constructed during the reign of Qin Shi Huang, stretched over 800 kilometers and traversed difficult terrain. According to historical accounts, the construction of the road required massive labor inputs and involved significant engineering challenges.

The straight road was designed primarily for military purposes, allowing the rapid deployment of forces to the northern frontier where the Xiongnu posed a constant threat. However, the road also facilitated civilian communication and commerce, contributing to the economic integration of the empire.

In addition to the major roads, the Qin government constructed an extensive network of smaller roads that connected the commandery capitals with the county seats and the surrounding countryside. This network facilitated the collection of taxes, the movement of officials, and the transmission of orders from the central government.

The road system was complemented by a communication system that allowed the central government to receive information from throughout the empire. Official messengers traveled the roads, carrying documents and reports between the capital and the provinces. The speed of communication was a measure of the efficiency of the administrative system, and the Qin government maintained a corps of professional messengers who could cover great distances quickly.

The construction of roads and the development of communication systems required massive labor inputs. The corvée labor system that the Qin government employed for public works projects drew on the entire adult male population, requiring individuals to spend a specified number of days each year working on infrastructure projects. This system was efficient but burdensome, and it generated significant resentment among the population.

## The Axial Standardization

One of the most distinctive aspects of the Qin standardization program was the regulation of cart axle lengths. Before the unification, the various states had used carts with different axle widths, meaning that carts from one state could not travel efficiently on the roads of another. This variation created obstacles to trade and complicated the transport of goods throughout the Chinese world.

The Qin government established a uniform standard for cart axle lengths, requiring all carts used on imperial roads to conform to a single specification. This standardization facilitated the movement of goods and people throughout the empire, contributing to economic integration.

The axle standard also had important implications for road construction. By ensuring that all carts used the same gauge, the government could construct roads that were optimized for the standard cart design. This created a positive feedback loop in which better roads led to more traffic, which in turn justified further investment in road infrastructure.

The standardization of axle lengths was a relatively minor aspect of the overall program, but it illustrates the comprehensiveness of the Qin approach to unification. Even the most technical aspects of daily life were brought under imperial regulation, creating a uniformity that was unprecedented in Chinese history.

## The Construction of the Imperial Network

The standardization program was complemented by an ambitious construction program that created the physical infrastructure for the unified empire. In addition to roads, the Qin government constructed canals, palaces, and defensive works that facilitated administration and demonstrated the power of the new regime.

The most significant canal project was the connecting of the various river systems of the empire through a network of artificial waterways. These canals facilitated the transport of grain from the productive southern regions to the capital and the northern frontier, providing the logistical foundation for the empire's military and administrative operations.

The construction of the Epang Palace (阿房宫) at the capital was intended to symbolize the power and majesty of the imperial regime. Although the palace was never completed, its planned dimensions indicate the scale of Qin imperial ambitions. The palace complex was designed to accommodate the emperor and his court, providing a setting for the elaborate rituals that expressed imperial authority.

The Qin government also constructed a network of watchtowers and defensive works along the northern frontier. These fortifications, which would eventually be connected to form the Great Wall, were designed to protect the empire from the nomadic peoples of the steppe. The construction of these works required massive labor inputs and contributed to the resentment that would eventually topple the dynasty.

## Resistance and Enforcement

The standardization program was not implemented without resistance. The populations of the former Warring States had developed distinct traditions over centuries, and the imposition of uniform standards required the abandonment of practices that were deeply ingrained. The Qin government relied on a combination of incentives and penalties to ensure compliance.

The enforcement of standards was the responsibility of the official bureaucracy. Local magistrates were required to verify that weights, measures, and coins in their jurisdictions conformed to imperial standards. Officials who failed to maintain proper standards were subject to punishment, creating incentives for rigorous enforcement.

The legal system supported the standardization program by prescribing severe penalties for violations. Merchants who used false weights or measures were subject to harsh punishments, as were officials who failed to detect or report such violations. The certainty of punishment created strong incentives for compliance.

However, the standardization program also faced more subtle forms of resistance. In many regions, the old practices persisted despite official prohibition, particularly in areas distant from the centers of administrative control. The standardization was most effective in the core regions of the former Qin state, where the administrative infrastructure was strongest, and less effective in the more distant territories.

## The Legacy of Standardization

The standardization program of the Qin empire had lasting consequences that extended far beyond the dynasty itself. The uniform systems of writing, weights, measures, currency, and law that the Qin government established became the foundation for Chinese civilization for the next two millennia.

The standardization of writing was perhaps the most enduring achievement. The script that Li Si developed in the third century BCE became the basis for the Chinese writing system, which would remain essentially unchanged despite the political upheavals that followed. The ability to read and write standardized characters became a mark of education and refinement, creating a shared literary culture that transcended regional boundaries.

The standardization of weights and measures also persisted, with the basic units of length, volume, and weight remaining in use for over two thousand years. The jin, the chi, and the dou would be used in Chinese markets until the twentieth century, a testament to the effectiveness of the Qin standardization.

The legal principles that the Qin code established—that law should be uniform, that officials should be subject to the same standards as ordinary citizens, that punishment should fit the crime—would continue to influence Chinese legal thinking even after the specific provisions of the Qin code were abandoned. The idea of a comprehensive legal code that governs all aspects of social life was a lasting contribution to Chinese political culture.

The infrastructure of roads and canals that the Qin government constructed facilitated economic integration and administrative control. The basic network of transportation routes that the Qin created would remain important throughout Chinese history, even as the specific roads were maintained, improved, or allowed to decay.

## Conclusion

The standardization program that the Qin government implemented after the unification of 221 BCE represented one of the most ambitious projects of social engineering in human history. The attempt to create "one empire, one system"—with uniform writing, weights, measures, currency, law, and infrastructure—was unprecedented in scale and in its implications for the future of Chinese civilization.

The motivations for standardization were both practical and ideological. Practically, uniform systems facilitated administration, commerce, and communication across the vast territory of the empire. Ideologically, standardization reflected the Legalist belief that order required uniformity and that variation created opportunities for confusion and corruption.

The implementation of the standardization program was not uniform in its effectiveness. Some aspects—particularly the standardization of writing and the legal code—had lasting impact, while others were more ephemeral. Moreover, the methods used to enforce standardization were often harsh, generating resentment that contributed to the instability of the regime.

Despite these limitations, the legacy of Qin standardization was profound. The systems that the Qin government established became the foundation for Chinese civilization for over two millennia. The unified writing system created a shared medium of communication that transcended regional boundaries. The standardized weights and measures facilitated commerce and administration. The uniform legal code established principles that would influence Chinese jurisprudence for centuries. And the infrastructure of roads and canals contributed to the economic integration of the empire.

The Qin standardization program thus represents one of the most consequential achievements of the first emperor's reign. The "one empire, one system" that Qin Shi Huang created shaped the political, economic, and cultural development of China for generations to come.

### Chapter Notes

The standardization program of the Qin empire has been the subject of extensive scholarly attention. The traditional view, derived from sources such as the *Records of the the Grand Historian*, presents the program as a comprehensive and well-ordered system implemented uniformly throughout the empire. Modern scholarship has emphasized the complexity and unevenness of the actual implementation, noting that local variations persisted despite official standardization.

The dating of specific standardization measures is sometimes uncertain. The unification of writing is generally attributed to the period immediately following the conquest of the six states, but the details of when each element of the standardization program was implemented are not always clear.

The assessment of the standardization program's impact has also been debated. Some scholars emphasize its practical benefits, arguing that it facilitated economic development and administrative efficiency. Others have focused on the hardships that enforcement created, noting the resentment that resulted from the harsh methods used to ensure compliance.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the standardization program and its implementation.
- Han Feizi, *The Complete Works of Han Fei*, trans. W.K. Liao (London: Arthur Probsthain, 1959). Legalist philosophical texts that informed the standardization program.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Comprehensive analysis of Qin standardization.
- Bodde, Derk, *China's First Unifier: A Study of the Ch'in Dynasty as Seen in the Life of Li Ssu* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1967). Detailed study of Li Si and the standardization program.
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013). Contextual analysis of standardization and its impact.
- Kern, Martin, *The Stele Inscriptions of Ch'in Shih-huang: Text and Context in the Publishing of Empire* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2000). Analysis of the ideological aspects of standardization.
- Hulsewé, A.F.P., *Remnants of Ch'in Law* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1985). Detailed analysis of the Qin legal code.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, *Writing and Authority in Early China* (Albany: State University of New York Press, 1999). Analysis of the standardization of writing.
- Needham, Joseph, *Science and Civilisation in China*, Vol. 4, Part 2 (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1965). Discussion of Chinese technology and standardization.
- Swann, Nancy Lee, *Food and Money in Ancient China* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1950). Analysis of the economic aspects of Qin standardization.
