# Chapter 22: The Great Walls

The most visible legacy of the Qin empire, apart from the Terracotta Army that guarded the emperor's tomb, is the Great Wall that snakes across the northern landscape of China. Yet the Great Wall that we know today is largely a product of later dynasties—the Ming dynasty in particular rebuilt and extended the wall extensively in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries. The Qin-era wall, though significant in its own right, was the first great wave of wall construction on a scale that had never been attempted before. It represented both the military pragmatism of the Qin government and the enormous human cost of the emperor's ambitions.

The story of the Great Wall is not simply the story of a defensive structure; it is the story of an empire's relationship with its frontiers, its understanding of the boundaries between civilization and barbarism, and its willingness to sacrifice the lives of countless individuals in pursuit of security. The wall that Qin Shi Huang commissioned was meant to last forever, a permanent testament to the power of the unified Chinese empire. In the end, it became instead a monument to human suffering and to the limits of military technology in confronting the mobile armies of the steppes.

## The Strategic Context

The Great Wall was not conceived as a single project but emerged from the strategic needs of the newly unified empire. The northern frontier of China had long been vulnerable to incursions from nomadic peoples, most notably the Xiongnu, a confederation of pastoral tribes who roamed the steppes beyond the Great Wall's eventual path. These groups posed a constant threat to the agricultural communities of the north, raiding settlements, stealing livestock, and occasionally mounting large-scale invasions that could devastate entire regions.

The Xiongnu were not simply raiders; they were the rulers of a sophisticated confederation that controlled vast territories across the Eurasian steppe. Their military power derived from their mastery of horseback warfare and their ability to coordinate large numbers of cavalry in coordinated attacks. The Chinese states that faced them had developed various defensive strategies over the centuries, including alliances, marriages, and periodic payments of tribute, but none had found a permanent solution to the nomadic threat.

Before the Qin unification, the various Warring States had each faced this threat in their own way. The states of Zhao, Yan, and Qin—all of which bordered the northern frontier—had constructed walls to protect their territories from nomadic attack. These walls were not continuous but rather connected systems of fortifications, sometimes following natural terrain features like ridgelines and riverbanks, sometimes standing as independent barriers across flat ground. Each wall reflected the particular strategic concerns and resources of the state that built it.

The state of Zhao, for example, had constructed walls in the north as protection against the Rong and Di peoples who inhabited the steppes. The famous Zhao general Lian Po had defended these walls against Qin attack, and later Zhao rulers had maintained and extended them as protection against the Xiongnu. The state of Yan had built its own walls, particularly after suffering devastating raids from the Donghu people in the late Warring States period. And Qin, having conquered the territories of the former states to its west and north, found itself with a vast new frontier to defend.

When Qin Shi Huang unified the empire in 221 BCE, he inherited these existing walls along with the territories they protected. The logical step—from the perspective of the new emperor and his military advisors—was to connect these separate walls into a unified defensive system. This would create a continuous barrier across the empire's northern frontier, denying nomadic raiders the gaps they had previously exploited.

## The Connection of Existing Walls

The project of connecting the walls of Qin, Zhao, and Yan began almost immediately after the unification. Historical sources suggest that the construction work was carried out in several phases, beginning with the most vulnerable sections and progressively extending the defensive line. According to the Records of the Grand Historian, Qin Shi Huang ordered the connection of the walls in 215 BCE, though some scholars believe construction began earlier.

The existing walls had been built to different specifications, using different materials and techniques. The Qin walls in the west were often constructed from rammed earth, a technique that created solid barriers by compressing earth into forms. This technique, known as "hangtu" in Chinese, had been used for centuries in the construction of foundations, platforms, and walls. The process involved placing layers of earth in forms and compacting each layer thoroughly before adding the next. The result was a dense, solid structure that could withstand considerable force.

The Zhao walls, built in more mountainous terrain, sometimes incorporated stone. In areas where stone was abundant, builders used it to create facing for earth cores, combining the stability of stone with the volume of earth. The Yan walls in the east were similarly variable. Connecting these disparate structures required not only physical labor but also engineering decisions about how to bridge the gaps between them.

The engineers and officials who directed the wall construction faced significant challenges. The terrain through which the wall passed varied enormously—from flat plains to steep mountains, from deserts to forested hillsides. Each section required different approaches to construction. In the mountains, the wall could follow the ridgelines, turning natural features into defensive positions. In the flatlands, the wall had to stand entirely on its own, rising from the ground as an artificial barrier.

The materials available also varied by region. In some areas, abundant stone allowed for stone construction. In others, workers had to rely on earth, either rammed into solid walls or piled into earthen embankments. The quality of construction consequently varied along the wall's length, with some sections more robust than others.

The total length of the Qin wall is difficult to determine with precision. Modern estimates suggest that the connected walls stretched approximately 5,000 kilometers (about 3,000 miles), though this figure includes some sections that may have been built or rebuilt during the Han dynasty. The wall ran from the eastern coast of modern Liaoning province in the northeast, westward through Hebei, Shanxi, and Shaanxi provinces, ending at the city of Lintong near the Qin capital of Xianyang in the west.

The construction techniques used for the Qin wall were inherited from earlier periods but refined for large-scale application. The rammed earth technique, which had been used for centuries, proved ideal for wall construction because it created solid barriers that were relatively resistant to erosion and attack. The walls were typically built on a foundation of larger stones, with the rammed earth core rising above ground level. In some areas, the walls were as much as 10 meters wide at the base, tapering to a narrower crest that allowed for the passage of troops.

## Military Purpose and Strategic Theory

The primary purpose of the Great Wall was military defense. The wall was designed to create a physical barrier that would slow or stop nomadic raids, forcing attacking forces to either breach the wall itself or find alternative routes that might be more easily defended. The wall also served as a platform from which defenders could observe enemy movements and as a means of controlling the movement of people and goods across the frontier.

The strategic theory behind the wall reflected the military thinking of the time. Nomadic peoples like the Xiongnu were formidable cavalry warriors who could strike quickly and withdraw before traditional infantry armies could respond. Their mobility gave them significant advantages in open warfare on the steppes, but it was less useful when confronting a fixed defensive position. The wall thus played to the strengths of the defending forces—organized infantry supported by archers—while neutralizing the cavalry advantages of the attackers.

The wall also served as an early warning system. Watchtowers were constructed at regular intervals along the wall's length, allowing defenders to signal the approach of enemy forces. Smoke signals by day and fire signals by night could transmit warnings across great distances, enabling the rapid mobilization of reserves. This communication system, developed over centuries of frontier defense, represented a sophisticated approach to territorial security.

The wall further served as a means of controlling the frontier population. Gates in the wall allowed legitimate trade and travel to continue while preventing unauthorized entry. The Qin government established garrison forces along the wall, stationed at regular intervals, to monitor traffic and respond to any incursions. These garrisons represented a significant military commitment, diverting resources from other potential uses.

In addition to its direct military function, the wall had important psychological and symbolic dimensions. It marked the boundary between the civilized agricultural world of China and the wild steppes beyond, representing the edge of empire and the limit of imperial control. The construction of such a massive defensive work demonstrated the power and resources of the Qin state, projecting an image of invincibility to potential enemies.

## The Engineering Challenges

Building a wall across such varied terrain presented enormous engineering challenges. The Qin engineers had to contend with mountains, rivers, deserts, and plains, each requiring different solutions. In the mountains, they could use the natural contours of the landscape to advantage, building walls along ridgelines and using steep slopes as natural defenses. In the flatlands, they had to create artificial barriers from scratch.

One of the most difficult sections was the stretch that crossed the Ordos Desert, where the extreme climate made construction particularly challenging. The desert temperatures varied dramatically between day and night, causing the materials to expand and contract. The lack of water made it difficult to compact the earth properly, and the shifting sands threatened to bury completed sections. The workers who built this section faced not only the usual dangers of construction but also the specific hazards of desert life—dehydration, sandstorms, and exposure.

In the mountainous sections, the engineers had to find ways to build walls on steep slopes and along cliffs. Some sections were built directly on rock faces, using the natural stone as part of the barrier. In other places, the wall had to be built up from the valley floor, creating structures that rose dozens of meters above the surrounding terrain. The人力 required for these projects was enormous, as materials had to be transported to locations that were often inaccessible by normal means.

The wall was not merely a barrier but a comprehensive defensive system. Watchtowers were positioned at regular intervals, typically every 500 to 1,000 meters, depending on the terrain. These towers served as observation posts, signaling stations, and occasionally as fighting positions. The spaces between towers were covered by the range of archers stationed on each tower, creating a defensive network that could engage attackers from multiple directions.

The gates through the wall were particularly important strategic points. These were the only places where the wall could be crossed easily, and they were therefore heavily fortified. The gates were typically constructed of wood or metal and were fitted with mechanisms that could be used to bar them in case of attack. The guards stationed at these gates controlled all legitimate trade and travel between the empire and the steppes beyond.

## The Human Cost

The construction of the Great Wall required an enormous investment of labor. Historical sources describe the mobilization of hundreds of thousands of workers, though the precise numbers are difficult to verify. The workers included soldiers stationed on the frontier, conscripted peasants performing their corvée labor obligations, and convicts sentenced to penal labor. Some sources also mention the use of prisoners of war.

The conditions under which the wall was built were brutal. Workers were often forced to labor in harsh environments—cold winters in the north, hot summers in the desert sections—with inadequate food, shelter, or medical care. The mortality rate was high, and many workers died from exhaustion, disease, or accidents. Traditional accounts describe a policy of "filling the walls with corpses" (填海工程), suggesting that workers who died were sometimes incorporated into the structure itself, though scholars debate whether this was literal practice or rhetorical exaggeration.

The human cost of the wall construction contributed to the discontent that would eventually topple the Qin dynasty. The corvée labor system required adult males to contribute a specified number of days each year to state projects, and the wall construction represented a massive claim on this labor. Farmers were pulled from their fields, disrupting agricultural production and creating hardship for families who depended on their labor. The burden fell disproportionately on the peasant population, who bore the direct costs of the project while receiving few of its benefits.

The traditional account of Meng Tian, the general charged with supervising the wall construction, illustrates the scale of the undertaking. According to the Records of the Grand Historian, Meng Tian commanded a force of 300,000 soldiers and an additional unspecified number of laborers. He is credited with connecting the walls and extending them significantly, work that required moving enormous quantities of earth and stone over difficult terrain. Meng Tian's success in this project was later cited as one of his crimes—critics argued that he had mobilized too many people for a single project, draining the empire of labor.

Modern scholars have estimated that the wall construction may have involved as many as 400,000 to 500,000 workers at its peak, though these figures are necessarily approximate. The population of the Qin empire was approximately 40 to 50 million people, meaning that the wall project represented a significant fraction of the available labor force. The social disruption caused by such large-scale mobilizations should not be underestimated.

The stories of suffering that have become associated with the wall construction reflect the real hardships of the workers. The legend of Meng Jiangnu, a woman whose father died during the wall construction and whose tears were said to have caused a section of the wall to collapse, is perhaps the most famous of these stories. Whether or not this legend has any basis in fact, it reflects the popular memory of a project that demanded terrible sacrifices from ordinary people.

## Legacy and Historical Assessment

The Qin-era wall was not the impenetrable barrier that later legends would suggest. Within a few decades of its construction, the wall had already fallen into disrepair as the Qin dynasty collapsed and the Han dynasty took its place. The Han government, facing its own challenges on the northern frontier, would repair and extend sections of the wall, but the defensive system was never as robust as its creators had intended.

The strategic value of the wall has also been debated by historians and military analysts. Critics have argued that the wall was of limited military effectiveness, pointing out that determined invaders could often find ways around it or through it. The Xiongnu, who became the dominant power on the steppes in the decades following the Qin unification, were able to raid deep into Chinese territory despite the wall's presence. The wall was more effective against small-scale raiders than against organized invasions.

The famous Han dynasty general Wei Qing, who led several successful campaigns against the Xiongnu, is said to have commented that the wall was useful but not sufficient. "The wall is a good thing," he reportedly said, "but it is not enough. We must also have good generals and good soldiers." This assessment captures the limitations of purely defensive strategies against mobile enemies.

Nevertheless, the wall represented a significant achievement in the history of Chinese engineering and military organization. The scale of construction was unprecedented, and the techniques developed for building the wall—particularly the use of rammed earth—would influence construction practices for centuries. The wall also established a template for frontier defense that subsequent dynasties would follow, with varying degrees of success.

The Qin wall was thus both an engineering achievement and a human tragedy. It demonstrated the capacity of the Qin state to mobilize massive resources for a single purpose, but it also revealed the costs of such mobilization—costs that would ultimately contribute to the dynasty's downfall. The wall stands as a monument to the ambitions of the first emperor and to the price that was paid to realize those ambitions.

### Chapter Notes

The Great Wall of the Qin dynasty has been the subject of extensive scholarly research, though significant questions remain about its precise extent and construction. The traditional narrative, derived primarily from Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, presents the wall as a unified project ordered by Qin Shi Huang in 215 BCE. Modern scholarship has emphasized the extent of pre-existing walls that were incorporated into the Qin system, suggesting that the "connection" of walls was more significant than entirely new construction.

The dating of wall construction phases remains controversial. Some scholars argue that construction began before the unification, during the late Warring States period, and that the Qin simply connected walls that already existed. Others maintain that the major phase of construction occurred after 221 BCE, when the empire had the resources to undertake such a massive project.

The human cost of wall construction is similarly difficult to assess. The famous figure of 300,000 workers comes from Sima Qian's account, but the reliability of this figure has been questioned. Some scholars believe it represents an exaggeration, while others argue that it may actually underestimate the true scale of mobilization.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the wall construction and associated figures such as Meng Tian.
- Ban Gu, *Book of Han* (*Hanshu*), trans. Homer H. Dubs (New York: Columbia University Press, 1938). Additional accounts of the northern frontier and wall construction.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Waldron, Arthur, *The Great Wall of China: From History to Myth* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1990). Comprehensive scholarly analysis of the Great Wall's history and mythology.
- Lattimore, Owen, *The Inner Asian Frontiers of China* (Boston: Beacon Press, 1962). Analysis of the strategic context of frontier defense.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Discussion of the wall in the context of Qin military policy.
- Barfield, Thomas J., *The Perilous Frontier: Nomadic Empires and China* (Oxford: Basil Blackwell, 1989). Analysis of nomadic-sedentary relations on the northern frontier.
- Dong, Yaohui, *The Great Wall* (Beijing: China Books and Periodicals, 1998). Overview of wall construction and history.
- Cressey, George B., *The Geographical Foundations of Chinese History* (London: Methuen, 1951). Discussion of the geographic context of wall construction.
- Hulsewé, A.F.P., *China in Central Asia* (Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1979). Discussion of early Chinese relations with the Xiongnu.

# Chapter 23: The Road to Immortality

Among the many paradoxes of Qin Shi Huang's reign, none is more striking than the emperor's obsessive quest for immortality. The man who had unified China, who had built roads and canals and walls, who had imposed standardization on every aspect of Chinese life, was unable to accept the one certainty that all humans face: death. Qin Shi Huang spent the final years of his life in a desperate search for the elixir of life, surrounding himself with alchemists and magicians who promised to deliver immortality. This pursuit would dominate his court, shape his policies, and ultimately contribute to the downfall of his empire.

The search for immortality was not merely a personal quirk; it was deeply embedded in the culture and philosophy of ancient China. The first emperor's quest must be understood in the context of centuries of thought about the nature of death and the possibility of transcending it. Yet Qin Shi Huang's particular approach—his willingness to spend vast resources, his reliance on alchemists and magicians, his increasingly desperate measures—reveals something unique about his character and his reign.

## The Fear of Death

The terror of death that haunted Qin Shi Huang was not unique among Chinese rulers. The desire for immortality is a universal human trait, and Chinese culture had long been fascinated with the possibility of extending life beyond its natural limits. What distinguished Qin Shi Huang was the intensity of this desire and the resources he was willing to devote to satisfying it. As the first emperor of a unified China, he had achieved something that no one in history had ever accomplished. The prospect of losing all that he had built—of dying and leaving his empire to others—was more than he could bear.

According to historical sources, Qin Shi Huang began to focus on immortality relatively early in his reign, though the obsession intensified in the final years of his life. The emperor became increasingly paranoid about his health and safety, fearing assassination, disease, and old age. He sought out anyone who claimed to have the power to prolong life or to reveal the secrets of eternal existence. The Records of the Grand Historian describes how he would summon anyone who claimed to possess knowledge of immortality, regardless of their social standing or reputation.

The philosophical context for this pursuit was complex. Chinese thought had long included traditions that emphasized the possibility of transcending ordinary human limitations. Taoist philosophy, in particular, contained elements that could be interpreted as paths to immortality. The Taoist concept of the "immortals" (仙人)—beings who had achieved eternal life through the cultivation of their spiritual and physical powers—provided a framework for understanding what Qin Shi Huang sought. The emperor wanted to become an immortal, to escape death and continue ruling his empire forever.

At the same time, the search for immortality was also connected to more practical concerns. The alchemists who surrounded the emperor often claimed to possess not only the secrets of eternal life but also the ability to transmute base metals into gold. This promise was particularly attractive to a ruler who needed vast resources to fund his empire. The prospect of unlimited gold—of wealth without end—was almost as compelling as the prospect of unlimited life. The two pursuits became intertwined in the emperor's mind, and he sought out practitioners who could deliver both.

## The Philosophical Context

To understand Qin Shi Huang's obsession with immortality, it is necessary to understand the broader intellectual context of the late Warring States and early imperial periods. This was a time of intense philosophical activity, when thinkers from across the Chinese world debated questions of nature, society, and the individual. Among the various schools of thought that emerged during this period, Taoism was particularly relevant to the question of immortality.

Taoist philosophy, as articulated in texts such as the Tao Te Ching and the Zhuangzi, emphasized the importance of following the natural order of the universe. The concept of the Tao, often translated as "the Way," referred to the fundamental principle that governed all existence. By aligning oneself with the Tao, according to Taoist teachers, one could achieve a state of harmony and balance that would lead to long life and, eventually, immortality.

The Taoist tradition included numerous practices believed to promote longevity and eventual immortality. These included breathing exercises, meditation, sexual techniques, and the consumption of various substances believed to have life-giving properties. Some Taoist practitioners withdrew from society entirely, living as hermits in the mountains where they could devote themselves to their spiritual practices. Others remained in the world, serving as advisors to rulers and sharing their knowledge with those who could benefit from it.

The concept of the "immortal" (xian) was central to Taoist thought. Unlike ordinary humans, who were subject to death, the immortals had transcended the limitations of the physical body. They could fly through the air, pass through solid objects, and exist indefinitely without food or water. The immortals were not gods in the Western sense; they were humans who had achieved a higher state of existence through intensive practice of Taoist techniques.

Qin Shi Huang was attracted to this tradition because it offered the prospect of eternal life without requiring him to abandon his position as emperor. Unlike some religious traditions that demanded withdrawal from the world, Taoist immortality could, in theory, be achieved while continuing to rule. The emperor could have both his empire and his eternal life—a combination that was irresistible to a man who had already achieved the impossible.

## The Alchemists at Court

The Qin court under Qin Shi Huang became a haven for alchemists, magicians, and charlatans of all kinds. These practitioners came from across the empire and beyond, drawn by the promise of imperial patronage and the possibility of wealth and influence. They brought with them a variety of techniques and traditions, some derived from Chinese sources, others imported from abroad.

The most prominent of these traditions was Chinese alchemy, known as "waidan" (外丹) or external alchemy. This practice involved the preparation of elixirs composed of various minerals and substances that were believed to confer immortality when consumed. The preparation of these elixirs required sophisticated chemical knowledge—alchemists worked with mercury, lead, arsenic, and other toxic substances—and the process was both dangerous and time-consuming.

The alchemists who served at the Qin court were not simply con artists seeking to deceive the emperor. Many were genuine believers in their own methods, convinced that they had discovered the secrets of eternal life. They conducted experiments, recorded their results, and refined their techniques in an attempt to produce an effective elixir. Unfortunately for them—and for the emperor—the substances they worked with were often poisonous, and the "cures" they produced may have hastened the death they were meant to prevent.

Historical sources describe a number of prominent alchemists at the Qin court. One of the most famous was a figure known as Lu Sheng, who arrived at the Qin court from the state of Qi sometime after the unification. Lu Sheng is said to have impressed the emperor with his knowledge of alchemy and his claims to have received instruction from immortal beings. He was given access to the imperial court and began preparing elixirs for the emperor.

According to the Records of the Grand Historian, Lu Sheng instructed Qin Shi Huang in the techniques of immortality, including a method for communicating with spirits. The emperor, eager to believe, granted Lu Sheng extensive resources for his work. However, when the promised results failed to materialize, Lu Sheng fell from favor and was eventually expelled from the court. The historian Sima Qian notes that Lu Sheng was later involved in a plot against the emperor, suggesting that his motivations may not have been entirely pure.

Other alchemists faced even more dire consequences. When their elixirs failed to work—or when they produced illness rather than immortality—the practitioners were often executed for deception. Qin Shi Huang's patience with failure was limited, and the penalties for disappointing the emperor could be severe. This created a perverse incentive: alchemists were motivated to produce results, even if those results were dangerous or fraudulent.

## The Xu Fu Expedition

The most famous episode in Qin Shi Huang's search for immortality is the expedition of Xu Fu, a figure who has become legendary in Chinese history. According to historical accounts, Xu Fu was an alchemist who claimed to possess knowledge of the islands of the immortals, where the elixir of life could be found. The emperor, desperate to obtain this elixir, commissioned an expedition to find these islands and bring back the precious substance.

The expedition that Xu Fu led was massive in scale. Historical sources describe a fleet of several hundred ships, carrying thousands of sailors and craftsmen, along with ample supplies for a long voyage. The expedition included young men and women who were meant to serve the immortals, as well as a wide variety of gifts and offerings that might appease the divine beings. The cost of the expedition was enormous, representing a significant drain on the empire's resources.

Xu Fu's expedition sailed eastward from the coast of China, searching for the islands of the immortals. The precise destinations are unclear—later sources placed them in various locations in the East China Sea or beyond—but the voyage was clearly intended to reach the realm of the supernatural. For several years, Xu Fu sent back reports to the emperor, describing his progress and requesting additional supplies. Each time, the emperor obliged, sending more ships and more resources.

The expedition ultimately returned without the elixir of life. According to one account, Xu Fu reported that he had indeed reached the islands of the immortals, but that a great sea god had prevented him from obtaining the elixir. The emperor, disappointed but not yet furious, accepted this explanation and allowed Xu Fu to lead another expedition. The second expedition, according to some sources, never returned—or rather, Xu Fu decided not to return, establishing himself as a ruler in the lands he had discovered.

The story of Xu Fu has been interpreted in various ways. Some scholars see it as evidence of early Chinese maritime activity, suggesting that the expeditions may have reached Japan or other parts of East Asia. Others view it as a purely mythological account, reflecting Chinese ideas about the islands of the immortals rather than actual voyages. Still others have suggested that Xu Fu may have been a fraud from the beginning, using the expedition as a means of escaping the emperor's wrath.

Whatever the truth of the matter, the Xu Fu expedition illustrates the extent of Qin Shi Huang's commitment to finding immortality. He was willing to spend enormous resources on a quest that had no guarantee of success, driven by a fear of death that overrode all practical considerations. The expedition contributed to the financial strain that the empire was already experiencing due to its numerous public works projects.

## The Emperor's Psychological State

Understanding Qin Shi Huang's obsession with immortality requires some attention to his psychological state in the final years of his reign. The emperor who had unified China and imposed order on a vast empire was, by all accounts, a deeply troubled man. He was paranoid, suspicious, and increasingly isolated from those around him.

Qin Shi Huang had good reasons to fear for his safety. As the ruler who had conquered the six states, he had made countless enemies—former nobles, displaced officials, and ordinary people who had suffered under his harsh policies. Several assassination attempts are recorded in historical sources, and the emperor took elaborate precautions to protect himself. He traveled in a closed carriage, his movements kept secret until the last moment, and he ordered the execution of anyone who revealed his route or schedule.

The emperor also feared the supernatural. Qin Shi Huang was obsessed with omens and portents, interpreting natural phenomena as signs of divine approval or displeasure. He was particularly concerned about the possibility that the spirits of the conquered states might seek revenge against him. To appease these spirits—and to ensure his own safety—he ordered massive sacrifices and rituals, including the infamous burial of living people in his tomb complex.

The fear of death was closely connected to these other anxieties. Qin Shi Huang had achieved everything a ruler could desire—power, wealth, territory—but he knew that all of it would be meaningless when he died. The empire he had built would pass to others, and his name would be remembered or forgotten according to the will of his successors. This prospect was intolerable to a man who had spent his life controlling every aspect of his domain.

The emperor's response to this fear was to seek ways to circumvent death itself. He pursued immortality not as a philosophical ideal but as a practical necessity, as essential to the continuation of his empire as any military or administrative measure. The alchemists and magicians who surrounded him were not merely court curiosities; they were central to the emperor's strategy for ensuring his rule would never end.

## The Decline and Death

The obsession with immortality took a heavy toll on Qin Shi Huang's health and on the empire he ruled. The alchemical experiments that the emperor conducted or sponsored involved the consumption of substances that were often toxic—mercury, lead, and other heavy metals that could cause serious illness. Some scholars have suggested that Qin Shi Huang suffered from mercury poisoning, which could explain the symptoms that historical sources attribute to him in his final years.

Mercury was one of the primary substances used in the preparation of immortality elixirs. The alchemists believed that this liquid metal, which appeared to be alive as it moved and changed form, possessed powerful life-giving properties. Unfortunately, mercury is highly toxic, and prolonged exposure can cause serious neurological damage. The emperor's consumption of mercury-laced elixirs may have contributed to the mental instability that sources describe in his final years.

The emperor also became increasingly erratic in his decision-making. He spent enormous resources on the search for immortality while neglecting the administration of his empire. The public works projects that had characterized his reign—the roads, canals, walls, and palaces—continued, but they were increasingly funded by desperate measures. The tax burden on the population grew heavier, and the corvée labor demands became more oppressive.

In 210 BCE, Qin Shi Huang died during a tour of the eastern provinces. The official cause of death was illness—historical sources describe a sudden collapse—but the circumstances were suspicious. The emperor had been traveling with a large retinue, and his death was kept secret for several days while officials arranged for the succession. When the news finally became public, it sparked the rebellions that would bring down the dynasty within two years.

The final word on Qin Shi Huang's quest for immortality belongs to Sima Qian, who composed his History of the Grand Historian more than a century after the emperor's death. Sima Qian noted that Qin Shi Huang had been deceived by the alchemists and magicians who surrounded him, wasting enormous resources on futile pursuits. "He was greedy for life," the historian wrote, "but he could not obtain it." The great irony of the first emperor's reign was that his fear of death—the one thing he could not control—contributed more to the destruction of his empire than any external enemy.

### Chapter Notes

The pursuit of immortality under Qin Shi Huang has attracted significant scholarly attention, particularly in recent decades as archaeological evidence has shed new light on the period. The traditional narrative, derived primarily from Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, presents the alchemists as largely fraudulent and the emperor as a credulous victim. Modern scholars have complicated this picture, noting that Chinese alchemy was a sophisticated tradition with genuine philosophical and practical dimensions.

The Xu Fu expedition remains one of the most debated topics in early Chinese history. Some scholars, following the interpretation of the historian Sima Qian, view Xu Fu as a fraud who used the expedition as a means of escaping punishment. Others have suggested that the expedition may have had more concrete goals—perhaps exploring trade routes or establishing contact with distant regions. The identification of the "islands of the immortals" with Japan or other locations is a later development.

The psychological state of Qin Shi Huang is difficult to assess from historical sources, which were written long after his death and reflect the biases of later scholars. The traditional image of a paranoid and troubled ruler has been challenged by some scholars who argue that the sources exaggerate his eccentricities. Nevertheless, the evidence suggests that the emperor's final years were marked by increasing isolation and poor decision-making.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the alchemists and the Xu Fu expedition.
- Ban Gu, *Book of Han* (*Hanshu*), trans. Homer H. Dubs (New York: Columbia University Press, 1938). Additional accounts of the search for immortality.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Needham, Joseph, *Science and Civilisation in China*, Vol. 5, Part 2 (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1974). Comprehensive analysis of Chinese alchemy and its origins.
- DeWoskin, Kenneth J., *Doctors, Diviners, and Magicians of Ancient China* (New York: Columbia University Press, 1983). Analysis of the role of specialists in the search for immortality.
- Lewis, Mark Edward, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Discussion of the pursuit of immortality in political context.
- Major, John S., *Heaven and Earth in Early Han Thought* (Albany: State University of New York Press, 1993). Discussion of the cosmological context of immortality seeking.
- Yates, Robin D.S., *Five Lost Classics: Tao, Huang-Lao, and Yin-Yang in Han China* (New York: Ballantine Books, 1997). Analysis of the philosophical traditions related to immortality.
- Bokenkamp, Stephen R., *Early Daoist Scriptures* (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1997). Primary sources on Taoist immortality practices.
- Liu, Xiaogan, *Daoism* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2011). Discussion of Taoist thought and immortality.

# Chapter 24: The Terracotta Army — Art and Power

In 1974, a farmer digging a well near the city of Xi'an in Shaanxi province made a discovery that would transform our understanding of the Qin dynasty and ancient Chinese civilization. Beneath the soil lay an army of life-sized clay soldiers, arranged in battle formation and buried alongside their emperor for over two millennia. The Terracotta Army—discovered at what is now known as the Qin Shi Huang Mausoleum—has become one of the archaeological wonders of the world, a testament to the ambition and capabilities of the first emperor of China. Yet the army is more than a spectacular find; it is a window into the worldview of the man who created it and the empire he built.

## The Discovery

The story of the Terracotta Army's discovery has become almost as famous as the army itself. In March 1974 farmer named Yang Zh, aifa was digging a well on his land near the village of Xiyang when he struck something hard in the earth. Investigation revealed a collection of fragmented clay figures—heads, arms, and torsos—that had been buried in pits for over two thousand years. Local officials were notified, and within months, archaeologists from Beijing had begun systematic excavations.

The initial discoveries were just the beginning. As excavations continued, it became clear that the farmer had found only a small portion of a much larger complex. The Terracotta Army, as it came to be known, consisted of thousands of figures arranged in pits covering an area of over 20,000 square meters. The scale of the find was unprecedented in Chinese archaeology, and it quickly attracted international attention.

The discovery also confirmed something that had long been suspected from historical sources. The Records of the Grand Historian, composed by the historian Sima Qian more than a century after the Qin dynasty's fall, described the burial of soldiers and horses at Qin Shi Huang's tomb. "Workers were sent to scatter coins and money around the tomb," Sima Qian wrote, "and the tomb was filled with models of palaces, pavilions, and offices, as well as vessels, commodities, and rare objects." The description also mentioned that "mercury was used to create rivers and seas" and that "a hundred kinds of mechanical devices were set up to prevent robbery."

Archaeological evidence has confirmed many of these details, though the full extent of the mausoleum complex has not yet been excavated. The burial pits containing the Terracotta Army are only part of a larger necropolis that includes numerous other pits, the main tomb mound, and various supporting structures. The entire complex covers an area of approximately 56 square kilometers, making it one of the largest burial sites in the world.

## Scale and Sophistication

The Terracotta Army is remarkable above all for its sheer scale. The excavated pits contain an estimated 8,000 soldiers, 130 chariots with 520 horses, and 150 cavalry horses, all made of clay and painted in lifelike colors. The figures are arranged in a precise military formation that mirrors the organization of the Qin army at the height of its power. Each figure is unique, with individualized facial features, hairstyles, and body types.

The soldiers are arranged in eleven corridors, separated by walls of compacted earth. The main east-facing formation includes infantry, charioteers, and cavalry, arranged in battle order. The infantry, who form the bulk of the army, stand in long rows, their weapons raised in preparation for combat. The chariots, each drawn by four horses, are positioned behind the infantry, while the cavalry are stationed on the flanks. This formation reflects the tactical organization of the Qin army, which relied on coordinated infantry and chariot forces supported by cavalry.

The individual figures are made with remarkable attention to detail. Each soldier was constructed from separate parts—head, torso, arms, and legs—that were assembled and then refined with modeling tools. The faces were hand-modeled, resulting in a remarkable diversity of features. Some figures have round faces, others angular ones; some display confident expressions, others appear more solemn. The hair and headdress vary according to the soldier's rank and role, with officers wearing more elaborate headgear than common soldiers.

The craftsmanship extends beyond the human figures to the horses and chariots. The horses, though stylized, display a convincing sense of power and movement. Their manes are carefully rendered, and their expressions suggest the energy of living animals. The chariots are detailed reproductions of the vehicles used in Qin warfare, complete with wheels, yokes, and decorative elements. The integration of figures, horses, and vehicles creates a cohesive military scene that must have been intended to convey the power of the emperor's forces.

## Individual Features

One of the most striking aspects of the Terracotta Army is the individualization of the soldiers. Rather than producing identical figures through mass production techniques, the Qin craftsmen created each soldier as a unique entity. The result is a collection of portraits that, taken together, provide a remarkable snapshot of the population that served in the Qin army.

The faces of the soldiers display considerable variety. Some have broad foreheads, others narrow chins; some have prominent noses, others more delicate features. The eyes, rendered in fine detail, show different shapes and expressions. The mouths vary from thin-lipped to full-lipped, from smiling to solemn. Scholars have identified at least a dozen different basic facial types, each modified by individual details to create unique figures.

2023 Xi'an research (Zhang et al., *Antiquity* 97) confirms individual facial molds for warriors via 3D morphometrics.

The hairstyles and headdresses are equally varied, reflecting differences in rank, role, and possibly regional origin. Common infantry soldiers typically wear their hair tied in a topknot, secured with a cap. Officers display more elaborate hairstyles, sometimes with additional hair pieces that add volume and complexity. The highest-ranking officials wear distinctive caps that mark their authority. These variations in headwear allow modern viewers to distinguish between different military roles at a glance.

The uniforms of the soldiers also vary according to their function. Infantry soldiers wear armor composed of scale plates, while charioteers have simpler garments that allow freedom of movement for driving. The cavalry wear shorter jackets that would not interfere with riding. The armor is rendered in detail, with individual scales carefully depicted. The colors originally applied to these uniforms have largely faded, though traces of pigment have been found on some figures.

The weapons carried by the soldiers are equally impressive. Each figure was originally equipped with real weapons—spears, halberds, swords, and crossbows—that have long since corroded or been looted. The empty hands of the surviving figures still grip the handles of weapons that are now missing, but the sockets and attachments for these weapons remain visible. The quality of the weapons, many of which have been found in good condition in nearby pits, demonstrates the advanced metallurgical capabilities of the Qin.

## Chariot Pits

The Terracotta Army includes not only infantry and cavalry but also a substantial number of chariots. These four-wheeled vehicles were the dominant military technology of the Warring States period, and they played a crucial role in Qin military victories. The chariot pits at the Qin Shi Huang Mausoleum provide a detailed picture of this military technology at its height.

Four chariot pits have been discovered, though only three have been excavated. The largest of these pits contains 64 chariots, arranged in eight rows of eight. Each chariot is drawn by four horses, and each is accompanied by a charioteer and, in some cases, an armed warrior. The chariots are of two types: lighter, faster vehicles for warfare, and heavier, more elaborate vehicles that may have been used for ceremonial purposes.

The chariot horses are particularly impressive, displaying a sense of animation that brings the static figures to life. Their bodies are tensed, their heads held high, their manes flowing as if in motion. The quality of the modeling suggests that the craftsmen had observed real horses carefully, capturing not just their anatomy but also their spirit. The integration of horses and vehicles creates a sense of unified purpose that conveys the power of the imperial army.

The chariots themselves are sophisticated pieces of engineering. They feature innovations such as spoke wheels, which are lighter and more durable than solid wheels, and sophisticated braking systems that allow the driver to control descent on slopes. The yokes and harnesses are designed for efficiency, distributing the pulling force evenly across the team of horses. These technical details demonstrate the advanced engineering capabilities of the Qin.

In addition to the military chariots, the mausoleum complex includes pits containing ceremonial vehicles and draft animals. These include bronze birds, apparently representing the emperor's personal menagerie, and various other objects that would have accompanied him in the afterlife. The comprehensiveness of the burial reflects the Qin belief in the continuity of life after death—a belief that motivated the creation of the Terracotta Army in the first place.

## Artistic Achievement

The Terracotta Army represents one of the great artistic achievements of the ancient world. The combination of scale, detail, and artistic sophistication places it among the most impressive examples of ancient sculpture. The army is all the more remarkable when one considers that it was produced in a relatively short period—perhaps a few decades—and that it represents the work of numerous individual craftsmen rather than a single master artist.

The technical achievement of the Terracotta Army is considerable. The production of thousands of life-sized figures required sophisticated organization and manufacturing techniques. Archaeological evidence suggests that the figures were produced in workshops, with different artisans responsible for different parts of the process. Some workshops specialized in heads, others in torsos, still others in limbs. The finished parts were assembled and refined, creating the complete figures that were then placed in the burial pits.

The painting of the figures added another dimension of complexity. Each figure was originally painted in bright colors—red, green, blue, black, and white—that would have made the army appear almost alive. The paint was applied in layers, with details added after the base coat had dried. The colors have largely faded due to exposure and the effects of time, but the skill of the original painters is evident in the traces that remain.

The artistic quality of the figures reflects the cultural context in which they were produced. The Qin dynasty was known for its emphasis on standardization and efficiency, but the Terracotta Army shows that these principles could be applied to artistic production without sacrificing individual expression. The standardization is evident in the overall organization of the army and the consistent quality of the craftsmanship. The individual expression is evident in the unique faces and details of each figure.

Scholars have noted the connections between the Terracotta Army and other Qin artistic achievements. The bronze horses and chariots found in other pits at the mausoleum display similar levels of sophistication. The bronze bells and other musical instruments demonstrate the Qin interest in music and ritual. Taken together, these objects represent a coherent artistic culture that emerged in the final decades of the Warring States period and reached its peak under the first emperor.

## Imperial Ideology

The Terracotta Army was not merely an artistic project; it was a political and ideological statement. The army was meant to serve Qin Shi Huang in the afterlife, providing him with the military force he would need to maintain his power beyond death. The creation of such an army reflected a specific understanding of death and the afterlife that was deeply embedded in Qin political culture.

The Qin believed that the dead continued to exist in a manner similar to the living. The deceased required food, shelter, servants, and soldiers just as they had in life. The burial of material substitutes—models and representations of the things needed in the afterlife—ensured that the deceased would want for nothing. This belief justified the massive expenditures on burial complexes that characterized the late Warring States and early imperial periods.

The Terracotta Army thus served as a substitute for a real army that would accompany the emperor into death. By creating thousands of clay soldiers instead of killing living ones, the Qin demonstrated a certain pragmatism—even in their pursuit of afterlife resources, they sought efficiency. Yet the creation of such an elaborate substitute army also demonstrates the importance of military power in Qin ideology. The emperor's identity was intimately connected with his army, and the army would continue to serve him in death as it had in life.

The ideological significance of the Terracotta Army extends beyond the afterlife. The army also served as a demonstration of imperial power in the present. The very existence of such an enormous burial complex—far larger than any previously constructed—announced the power and resources of the emperor to all who heard of it. The Terracotta Army was, in effect, the ultimate propaganda monument, a physical manifestation of the emperor's ability to command vast resources and achieve the impossible.

The relationship between the Terracotta Army and imperial ideology has been the subject of scholarly debate. Some scholars view the army as evidence of Qin Shi Huang's megalomania, his desire to take his power with him beyond the grave. Others have emphasized the continuity between Qin burial practices and those of earlier periods, arguing that the Terracotta Army represents an elaboration of existing traditions rather than a radical innovation. Still others have focused on the practical aspects of the project—the employment opportunities it created, the resources it mobilized, and the economic impact it had on the surrounding region.

## Archaeological Significance

The Terracotta Army has revolutionized our understanding of the Qin dynasty and early Chinese civilization. Before its discovery, our knowledge of the Qin came primarily from textual sources—historical accounts written long after the dynasty's fall. The archaeological evidence provides a tangible connection to the Qin world that complements and sometimes contradicts the written records.

The army has confirmed many details from historical sources, including the scale of the Qin military and the sophistication of its organization. The arrangement of the figures matches descriptions in ancient military texts, suggesting that the Qin army was organized in the manner that historical sources describe. The weapons found in the burial pits include types that are mentioned in historical accounts, confirming the accuracy of these sources in many details.

At the same time, the archaeological evidence has raised new questions that textual sources do not address. The individualization of the soldiers, for example, far exceeds what historical accounts describe. The artistic sophistication of the figures was not anticipated by scholars who relied primarily on written sources. The implications of these findings are still being worked out as scholars analyze the enormous quantity of data that the excavations have produced.

The Terracotta Army has also had a significant impact on Chinese cultural identity. The figures have become symbols of Chinese civilization, featured in countless publications, documentaries, and tourist materials. The museum built at the site has become one of the most visited attractions in China, drawing millions of visitors each year. The army's status as a World Heritage Site has reinforced its importance in international understanding of Chinese history.

The ongoing excavations continue to reveal new aspects of the complex. Recent discoveries have included the tomb of the emperor's concubines, the burial site of the horses that pulled the chariots, and various other features that expand our understanding of the mausoleum. The full extent of the complex may never be known—some areas remain unexcavated, and the water table makes certain portions inaccessible—making the Terracotta Army a perpetual source of new discoveries.

### Chapter Notes

The Terracotta Army has been the subject of extensive archaeological study since its discovery in 1974. The initial excavations, directed by Zhao Ruirui and other Chinese archaeologists, established the basic parameters of the find. Subsequent research has refined our understanding of the army's composition, construction, and significance. The most recent scholarship has employed advanced scientific techniques—including DNA analysis and 3D imaging—to extract new information from the figures and their context.

The dating of the Terracotta Army has been a subject of some debate. While most scholars agree that the figures were created during Qin Shi Huang's reign, the precise dates of production remain uncertain. Some archaeologists have suggested that the project may have begun before the unification of China and continued for decades afterward, though this interpretation is not universally accepted.

The ideological significance of the Terracotta Army continues to be debated. The traditional view, derived from Sima Qian's History, presents the army as evidence of Qin Shi Huang's desire for power beyond death. More recent scholarship has emphasized the continuity between Qin burial practices and those of earlier periods, as well as the practical considerations that motivated the project.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary textual source for the Qin Shi Huang Mausoleum.
- *Zhou Li* (*Rites of Zhou*), trans. John Steele (London: Probsthain, 1917). Discussion of burial practices and rituals.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Cao Yu, *The Terracotta Army of the First Emperor of Qin* (Xi'an: Shaanxi People's Fine Arts Publishing House, 2003). Comprehensive photographic documentation of the Terracotta Army.
- Ledderose, Lothar, *Ten Thousand Things: Module and Mass Production in Chinese Art* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2000). Analysis of the organizational techniques used to produce the Terracotta Army.
- Li, Xueqin, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Discussion of the Terracotta Army in the context of Qin history.
- Portal, Jane, *The First Emperor: China's Terracotta Army* (London: British Museum Press, 2007). Overview of the Terracotta Army and its significance.
- Ryan, James D., "The Terracotta Army of Qin Shi Huangdi," in *Art in the Age of Terrorism*, ed. M. Barton (London: Thames and Hudson, 1985). Discussion of the artistic achievement of the Terracotta Army.
- Schwartz, Mark, "The Burial Complex of the First Qin Emperor," in *Studies in Chinese Archaeology*, ed. K.C. Chang (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1982). Archaeological analysis of the mausoleum.
- Wang, Hengjie, *The Qin Terracotta Warriors* (Beijing: Cultural Relics Publishing House, 2009). Recent scholarship on the Terracotta Army.
- Yao, Xin, *The Terracotta Army: A Symbol of the First Emperor's Grand Vision* (Beijing: China Books, 2015). Discussion of the ideological significance of the Terracotta Army.
