# Chapter 25: The Suppression of Thought

The burning of books and the burial of scholars—events that would become synonymous with cultural tyranny in Chinese memory—represent some of the most controversial and debated actions of Qin Shi Huang's reign. In 213 BCE, the emperor issued a decree that would transform the intellectual landscape of China and provoke centuries of condemnation. The destruction of philosophical texts and the execution of hundreds of scholars marked a dramatic escalation in the emperor's effort to control not only the political and physical dimensions of his empire but also the realm of thought itself. Understanding these events requires examining the ideological foundations that motivated them, the specific actions that were taken, and the long-term consequences that followed.

## The Ideological Foundations of Suppression

The suppression of thought in Qin China did not emerge from a vacuum; it was rooted in the political philosophy that had guided the Qin state for generations. Legalism, the dominant ideological framework of the Qin, emphasized the primacy of law and the absolute authority of the ruler. According to Legalist doctrine, a well-ordered state required a single, unified system of law and a population that obeyed without question. Philosophers such as Han Fei and Shang Yang had argued that the multiplicity of schools of thought—Confucianism, Mohism, Daoism, and others—created confusion and undermined the authority of the state. In their view, the people should be guided by clear laws and punishments, not by abstract philosophical principles that varied from school to school.

This Legalist perspective viewed intellectual diversity as inherently dangerous. When different schools of thought offered competing interpretations of proper governance, they undermined the unity that the Qin sought to achieve. A Confucian scholar might argue for benevolent rule and the importance of moral example; a Daoist philosopher might advocate for non-action and withdrawal from worldly affairs; a Mohist might insist on universal love and opposition to aggressive warfare. For the Qin legalists, these competing visions created what they termed "the disorder of the hundred schools" (百家争鸣), a intellectual chaos that prevented the establishment of a coherent and unified state.

Qin Shi Huang had embraced Legalism as the foundation of his rule, and the unification of China in 221 BCE had been achieved through the application of Legalist principles. The standardization of weights, measures, writing, and law had created a material unity across the empire. But the emperor and his advisors believed that true unity required more than the standardization of external forms; it required a uniformity of thought and belief. The persistence of the old intellectual traditions threatened to undermine the new order by providing alternative visions of governance and social organization.

The tension between intellectual diversity and state authority had been present throughout the Warring States period, but the Qin had developed a particular solution to this problem. Rather than attempting to suppress all thought—which would have been impractical and potentially counterproductive—the Qin sought to establish Legalism as the sole legitimate ideology. Other schools of thought were not simply tolerated; they were actively suppressed in favor of a single, state-sanctioned philosophy. This approach represented a significant departure from the intellectual pluralism that had characterized earlier periods of Chinese history.

The decision to suppress other schools of thought was also driven by more immediate political concerns. The unification of China had not been accomplished through peaceful means; it had been achieved through military conquest and the destruction of six rival states. Many of the scholars who now lived under Qin rule came from these defeated states, and their loyalties remained suspect. The old aristocratic families of Qi, Chu, Zhao, and other states still remembered their former independence, and intellectual traditions served as vehicles for preserving cultural identity and, potentially, resistance to Qin rule. By suppressing these traditions, the emperor sought to eradicate the cultural foundations of potential opposition.

## The Decree of 213 BCE

The immediate catalyst for the burning of books was a palace incident in 213 BCE, during a celebratory gathering at which the emperor hosted a banquet for his officials and scholars. According to the account preserved in the Records of the Grand Historian, the emperor was in a generous mood and distributed gifts to his officials based on their contributions. One such official, likely a scholar named Hou Ying (though the name may be a later interpolation), stood to offer a toast and took the opportunity to praise the ancient sage-kings and their system of government.

In his toast, Hou Ying reportedly contrasted the governance of the ancient sage-kings with that of the present, suggesting that the Qin system of rule was inferior to the理想 of the past. The ancient kings, he argued, had ruled through virtue and moral example, whereas the Qin relied solely on laws and punishments. This criticism struck at the heart of Qin ideological claims, suggesting that the emperor's system of rule was fundamentally flawed.

The emperor's chief minister, Li Si, responded to this criticism with a lengthy memorandum that would become the justification for the policies of cultural suppression. Li Si's argument, as preserved in Sima Qian's account, reflected the Legalist perspective in its clearest form. He began by acknowledging the achievements of the Qin under Qin Shi Huang: the unification of the empire, the establishment of a rational system of administration, and the creation of a stable social order. These achievements, he argued, had been made possible by the rejection of the "disorder of the hundred schools" and the embrace of a single, coherent philosophy.

Li Si then turned to the dangers posed by the persistence of the old intellectual traditions. He warned that scholars who clung to the teachings of the past would use them to criticize the present government and to stir up discord among the people. The various schools of thought, he argued, "each speaks of its own teachings, criticizing the laws and orders of the government." This criticism undermined the authority of the ruler and created confusion among the people, who were uncertain which teachings to follow.

The solution that Li Si proposed was radical: all books except those dealing with practical subjects—medicine, agriculture, divination, and forestry—should be destroyed. Historical records of events prior to the Qin unification should also be burned, as they served only to remind people of the former states and potentially inspire nostalgia and resistance. Only the Legalist texts, which provided the philosophical foundation for Qin rule, should be preserved. In this way, Li Si argued, the people would have no alternative source of guidance and would be forced to accept the authority of the laws and the ruler.

Li Si's memorandum concluded with a further recommendation regarding the scholars themselves. Those who continued to study the forbidden books, or who used them to criticize the government, should be subjected to severe punishment—including execution and the destruction of their families. The scholars who had gathered at the imperial court should serve as examples, demonstrating the consequences of dissent.

Qin Shi Huang accepted Li Si's recommendations and issued the decree that would become infamous in Chinese history. The order commanded that all books dealing with history, philosophy, literature, and other non-practical subjects be surrendered to the authorities and burned. Anyone who violated this order by concealing or reading forbidden books would be subjected to corporal punishment and sent to forced labor. Officials who failed to carry out the order would be held responsible and punished along with the offenders.

## The Burning of the Books

The decree of 213 BCE set in motion a systematic campaign of book destruction across the Qin empire. Local officials were tasked with collecting and burning all prohibited texts, and reports suggest that the order was carried out with considerable zeal. In the capital at Xianyang, the fires burned for what was said to be thirty days, consuming the accumulated literary heritage of centuries. The smoke that rose from the city was said to have darkened the sky—an image that would be repeated in countless later accounts as evidence of the enormity of the destruction.

The scope of the burning is difficult to determine with precision. The historical sources describe the burning in dramatic terms, but their accounts may be exaggerated for rhetorical effect. It is clear that the Qin authorities made significant efforts to identify and destroy prohibited texts, but the extent to which they were successful is uncertain. The empire was large, communication was slow, and local officials may have varied in their commitment to carrying out the decree. Some scholars may have concealed their books, and others may have memorized important texts in hopes of preserving them for future generations.

The texts that were targeted included not only philosophical works but also historical records, poetry, and literature. The Six Classics— canonical texts associated with the Zhou dynasty that would later form the foundation of Confucian education—were specifically targeted, along with the works of individual philosophers such as Confucius, Mencius, Mo Zi, and Zhuangzi. The burning represented an attack on the entire intellectual tradition that had developed over the preceding centuries, reducing to ashes what had taken generations to create.

Not all books were destroyed, however. The decree explicitly exempted certain categories of texts that were considered practically useful. Books on medicine, pharmacy, agriculture, arboriculture, and divination were permitted, as they dealt with subjects that served immediate practical purposes. The Legalist texts that provided the ideological foundation for Qin rule were also preserved, along with works that documented the achievements of the current dynasty. The burning was thus selective rather than comprehensive, targeting intellectual traditions that were seen as politically dangerous while preserving those deemed useful or legitimate.

The impact of the burning on the preservation of ancient texts has been a subject of scholarly debate. Some scholars have argued that the destruction was far more limited than traditional accounts suggest, pointing to evidence that many texts survived in fragments or through oral transmission. Others have emphasized the genuine loss represented by the burning, noting that certain works are known to us only through later quotations or reconstructions. The truth likely lies somewhere between these extremes: the burning caused significant damage to the intellectual heritage of ancient China, but it did not entirely destroy any particular text that was widely known and transmitted.

## The Burial of the Scholars

The burning of the books was followed by an event that would prove even more infamous in Chinese memory: the burial of the scholars. According to the traditional account, in 212 BCE, Qin Shi Huang ordered the execution of approximately 460 scholars who had been found guilty of criticising the government or violating the decree against the study of forbidden texts. These scholars were buried alive—an execution method that would become known as "burying the scholars" (坑儒) and that would haunt the memory of the first emperor for millennia.

The event is recorded in the Records of the Grand Historian, which describes how the emperor, angered by continued criticism and dissent, ordered the arrest and execution of a large group of scholars. The specific details vary in different accounts, but the basic narrative is consistent: scholars who had criticized the government or who had been found in possession of forbidden texts were rounded up and executed, their bodies left in mass graves as a warning to others.

Victims were likely alchemists/magicians (方士) who deceived the emperor (per Shiji 6.259), not Confucian *ru* scholars. The "460 Confucian" narrative solidified centuries later in Han-era texts. Archaeological evidence (Shuihudi slips) shows Qin targeted *specific* critics of imperial policies, not scholars broadly.

The execution method—burial alive—adds a particularly gruesome dimension to the event. In standard Chinese practice, execution typically involved beheading or strangulation, methods that were considered more humane than the cruel punishments reserved for the most serious crimes. Burial alive was reserved for the most heinous offenses, and its use in this case shocked later generations. The event became a symbol of the tyrant's brutality, demonstrating the extreme to which autocratic power could go when unchecked by moral constraints.

The traditional account maintains that the execution was carried out in the capital at Xianyang, with the victims buried outside the city walls. The site of the burial became a place of horror in Chinese memory, a physical reminder of the violence that could be inflicted in the name of political order. Later traditions located the graves at various sites, and the memory of the event became intertwined with local geography and folklore.

Not all scholars accepted the traditional account, however. Some modern scholars have questioned whether the execution actually occurred as described, suggesting that the numbers may have been exaggerated or the event confused with other incidents. The account in the Records of the Grand Historian was written more than a century after the events it describes, and Sima Qian may have relied on sources that were hostile to the Qin dynasty. Nevertheless, the consistency of the basic narrative across multiple sources suggests that some form of persecution of scholars did occur, even if the details cannot be verified with certainty.

## The Rationale for Suppression

The policies of book burning and scholar persecution were driven by a coherent ideological logic, even if that logic appears alien to modern sensibilities. The Qin legalists believed that intellectual unity was essential for political stability, and they were willing to use extreme measures to achieve that unity. The burning of books was not simply an act of cultural vandalism; it was an attempt to create a clean slate upon which a new, rational order could be built.

Li Si's memorandum had articulated the rationale clearly: the multiplicity of schools of thought created confusion and undermined authority. When people were exposed to competing interpretations of proper conduct, they became uncertain about how to behave and who to obey. The solution was to eliminate the sources of confusion and to provide a single, authoritative guide to thought and action. The laws of the state, interpreted by the ruler and enforced by officials, would serve as that guide.

This perspective reflected a particular understanding of human nature. The legalists believed that people were naturally inclined toward disorder and that only the threat of punishment could keep them in line. Moral suasion, religious instruction, and philosophical education were seen as ineffective at best, dangerous at worst, because they encouraged people to think for themselves rather than simply obeying the law. By eliminating alternative sources of guidance, the Qin sought to create a population that would look only to the state for direction.

The persecution of scholars was motivated by similar concerns. Scholars were not simply academics; they were potential teachers and leaders who could influence public opinion and mobilize opposition. By removing the most prominent scholars and making examples of them, the Qin hoped to discourage others from following their path. The execution of 460 scholars was intended to send a clear message: dissent would not be tolerated, and the consequences of opposition would be severe.

The policies also reflected the particular circumstances of the Qin regime. The dynasty had been established through military conquest, and the emperor faced ongoing challenges to his authority from former nobles, local elites, and conquered populations. The suppression of intellectual traditions that might serve as vehicles for resistance was a pragmatic response to these security concerns. The old schools of thought were associated with the former states, and their preservation kept alive memories of a time before Qin rule. By destroying these traditions, the emperor sought to erase alternative identities and to create a unified population that acknowledged no authority but his own.

## Long-Term Consequences

The long-term consequences of the suppression of thought were profound, though they did not always align with the intentions of the Qin rulers. The burning of books and the execution of scholars did not succeed in eliminating intellectual traditions; instead, they drove them underground and created a sense of grievance that would shape Chinese political culture for centuries.

The most immediate consequence was the creation of a reservoir of resentment against the Qin dynasty. The persecution of scholars alienated the educated class, which might otherwise have served as administrators and collaborators. Instead of supporting the regime, many scholars became secret critics who preserved the forbidden texts in anticipation of the dynasty's fall. When the Qin collapsed in 206 BCE, these scholars would play a key role in reconstructing the intellectual traditions that had been suppressed and in shaping the historical memory of the Qin period.

The destruction of texts also had significant implications for the preservation of knowledge. Many works that might have been transmitted more completely were lost or corrupted, requiring later scholars to reconstruct them from fragments and quotations. The burning was particularly devastating for historical records, which provided evidence of the former states and their achievements. Without these records, the history of the Warring States period would have been much more difficult to reconstruct.

Yet the suppression also had unintended consequences for the preservation of certain texts. Some scholars memorized important works, passing them on orally until they could be written down again after the Qin fall. Others concealed books in walls, tombs, or other hiding places, where they survived until later periods. The very act of suppression, by making the forbidden texts more valuable and dangerous, created incentives for their preservation. In this way, the policies may have paradoxically contributed to the survival of certain works that might otherwise have been lost.

The ideological consequences of the suppression were equally significant. The Qin attempt to establish intellectual uniformity ultimately failed, but it established a pattern that would be repeated throughout Chinese history. Later dynasties would periodically attempt to suppress heterodox ideas, and the tension between state authority and intellectual freedom would remain a central theme of Chinese political culture. The memory of the book burning became a cautionary tale, invoked by critics of authoritarianism to demonstrate the dangers of suppressing thought.

The treatment of scholars also established a pattern that would endure. The execution of the 460 scholars created a martyr narrative that would be invoked in later periods when intellectuals faced persecution. The image of the scholar as a defender of truth against tyranny, willing to sacrifice life itself for principle, became a powerful ideal in Chinese culture. This ideal stood in stark contrast to the Qin legalist vision of the scholar as a potential troublemaker who must be controlled or eliminated.

## The Debate Over the Events

The historical debate over the suppression of thought has been extensive, reflecting both the significance of the events and the difficulty of establishing what actually occurred. The primary source for the events is Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, written more than a century after the Qin dynasty's fall. Sima Qian's account is detailed and dramatic, but it was composed in a period when the Qin were widely regarded as tyrants, and his sources may have reflected the political biases of his time.

Some scholars have argued that the traditional account exaggerates the scope and severity of the suppression. They point out that the Qin empire was vast and that the central government had limited capacity to enforce its decrees uniformly. Local officials may have varied in their compliance, and many texts may have survived the burning. The number 460, they suggest, may have been a rhetorical device rather than an accurate count.

Other scholars have defended the traditional account, noting that the basic narrative is consistent across multiple sources and that the archaeological evidence does not contradict it. They argue that the Qin had both the motivation and the capability to carry out such a campaign, and that the subsequent restoration of texts by later generations confirms the extent of the destruction.

The debate also reflects broader disagreements about how to interpret the Qin dynasty as a whole. Some scholars view the Qin as a precursor to later imperial institutions, emphasizing the positive achievements of unification and standardization. Others see the Qin as a brutal tyranny that established patterns of authoritarian rule that would harm Chinese society for millennia. The interpretation of events like the book burning often reflects these larger disagreements about the significance of the Qin in Chinese history.

The suppression of thought remains one of the most controversial aspects of Qin Shi Huang's rule, and it continues to provoke strong reactions in the modern period. For many Chinese and foreign observers, the burning of books and the burial of scholars represent the epitome of cultural destruction and intellectual tyranny. These events have been invoked to criticize authoritarian rulers in general, and they serve as a reminder of the dangers of unchecked power. Yet they also reflect a particular historical moment and a specific ideological framework that may not translate easily into modern contexts.

### Chapter Notes

The events of 213-212 BCE remain among the most debated in early Chinese history. The traditional account, based primarily on Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, presents a clear narrative of decree, burning, and execution. However, scholars have increasingly questioned the accuracy of this account, noting that it was composed in a period of intense hostility toward the Qin and that Sima Qian's sources are no longer available for verification. The archaeological evidence from the Qin period has shed some light on the matter, but no direct evidence of the book burning or scholar executions has been discovered.

The question of what texts were actually destroyed is also difficult to resolve. The traditional view holds that the burning was comprehensive, targeting virtually all non-Legalist texts. More recent scholarship has suggested that the destruction was more limited, focusing on specific categories of texts that were deemed most threatening. The survival of many texts through the Qin period suggests that the burning was not entirely successful, though it may have caused significant losses.

The ideological motivations behind the suppression have also been debated. Some scholars emphasize the internal logic of Legalism, arguing that the policies followed naturally from the philosophical principles that guided Qin rule. Others have emphasized the political circumstances of the empire, suggesting that the suppression was driven by security concerns rather than abstract ideology. Both factors likely played a role, though their relative importance remains uncertain.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary textual source for the book burning and scholar persecution.
- Li Si, "The Memorial on the Suppression of Books," trans. in Knoblock and Riegel, *The Annals of Li Lou*. The text of Li Si's memorandum justifying the policies.

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986). Comprehensive overview of the Qin and Han periods.
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007). Discussion of Qin intellectual policy and its consequences.
- Li Xueqin, *The Early History of China* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2019). Recent scholarship on Qin history and culture.
- Yuri Pines, *The Everlasting Empire: The Political Culture of Ancient China and Its Imperial Legacy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2012). Analysis of ideological foundations of Qin rule.


---


# Chapter 26: The Capital at Xianyang

The imperial capital at Xianyang was the physical manifestation of Qin Shi Huang's ambition to create an empire that would endure forever. Located on the banks of the Wei River in present-day Shaanxi province, Xianyang had been the capital of the Qin state for centuries, but under the first emperor it was transformed into something unprecedented: a vast urban complex that dwarfed any city previously built in China. The construction of the new capital represented one of the most ambitious building programs in ancient history, employing hundreds of thousands of laborers and consuming resources on an almost incomprehensible scale. Yet the city that the Qin built would not survive its founders; within years of the dynasty's collapse, Xianyang would be destroyed by fire, leaving only ruins and memories.

## The Choice of Xianyang

Xianyang had served as the capital of the Qin state since the fourth century BCE, when the Qin rulers had moved their court from the western borders of their territory to the more fertile and strategically advantageous Guanzhong region. The location offered several advantages: the fertile soils of the Wei River valley provided agricultural abundance; the surrounding mountains offered natural defenses; and the central position within the Qin territory allowed for efficient administration and communication.

When Qin Shi Huang unified China in 221 BCE, he retained Xianyang as his capital rather than establishing a new city. This decision reflected both practical considerations and ideological symbolism. The existing infrastructure—the palace, the administrative buildings, the surrounding settlements—provided a foundation for expansion without the need to start from scratch. More importantly, Xianyang was already associated with the Qin dynasty and its achievements; to abandon it would have been to abandon the legacy of the Qin state that the emperor claimed to represent.

Yet the existing capital was inadequate for the needs of an empire. The palace that had served the kings of Qin was insufficient for an emperor who ruled over all of China. The city that had grown up around it was too small to accommodate the influx of officials, merchants, and laborers that the new empire required. Within months of the unification, Qin Shi Huang ordered the expansion of Xianyang into a capital worthy of the empire it was meant to embody.

The expansion of Xianyang was not merely a matter of increasing the size of the city; it was an attempt to create a new urban form that would express the ideology of the unified empire. The layout of the city was designed to reflect the cosmic order, with the emperor at the center and the territories of the empire arranged around him. The construction was guided by principles derived from earlier Chinese urban planning, but the scale was entirely new, representing a quantum leap in the ambition and capability of Chinese civilization.

## The Construction Program

The expansion of Xianyang began almost immediately after the unification and continued throughout Qin Shi Huang's reign. The construction program was enormous in scope, employing what historical sources describe as hundreds of thousands of laborers. These laborers came from across the empire—from the conquered states of the east, from the populations of the frontier regions, and from the池 of criminals and prisoners who had been sentenced to forced labor. The scale of the construction was comparable to the building of the Great Wall, and it drew on the same pool of labor and resources.

The most famous component of the construction was the Epang Palace (阿房宫), which was intended to serve as the emperor's new residence and the ceremonial center of the empire. According to historical accounts, the palace was designed on a monumental scale, with vast halls capable of accommodating thousands of people, elaborate decorations, and extensive gardens. The name "Epang" means "front of the palace," suggesting that the structure was intended as the first of several buildings in a larger complex.

The construction of Epang Palace was controversial from the beginning. According to Sima Qian's account, a court official named Li He was responsible for the initial design, which called for a structure of unprecedented size. When the design was presented to the emperor, Li Si and other ministers objected, arguing that such a building would be wasteful and could not be completed in a reasonable time. Qin Shi Huang, however, overruled their objections and ordered the construction to proceed.

The construction of Epang Palace continued for years, employing an enormous workforce. According to traditional accounts, the palace was never completed; construction was interrupted by the emperor's death in 210 BCE and was never resumed. Later sources describe the palace as covering an area of several hundred hectares, with buildings arranged in a complex pattern of halls, corridors, and courtyards. The scale of the complex was such that it was said to be visible from a great distance, its rooftops stretching to the horizon.

Archaeological excavations at the site of Epang Palace have confirmed some aspects of the traditional accounts while raising questions about others. The remains of the palace complex have been identified near present-day Xi'an, on the southern bank of the Wei River. Excavations have revealed the foundations of large buildings, evidence of elaborate decoration, and the remains of what appears to have been a massive artificial lake or reservoir. The site covers an area of approximately 15 square kilometers, confirming the traditional description of a vast complex. However, the extent of completion remains unclear; some scholars argue that the palace was never fully constructed, while others suggest that construction was more advanced than traditional accounts indicate.

## The Layout of the Capital

The expanded capital of Xianyang was organized according to principles that reflected both traditional Chinese urban planning and the ideological needs of the new empire. The city was divided into several distinct zones, each serving different functions. At the center was the palace complex, where the emperor lived and conducted government business. Surrounding the palace were the administrative districts, where the various ministries and bureaus of the imperial government were housed. Beyond these central areas were the residential districts, where officials, merchants, and ordinary citizens lived.

The layout of Xianyang was designed to express the cosmic order that the emperor was believed to embody. The palace was positioned at the center of the city, reflecting the emperor's position at the center of the universe. The streets were laid out in a grid pattern,象征 the order and rationality that the Qin sought to impose on their empire. The walls that surrounded the city were massive, symbolizing the boundaries between order and chaos, between the civilized empire and the wild frontiers beyond.

The construction techniques employed in the building of Xianyang were advanced for their time. The palaces and public buildings were constructed using timber frames, with walls of rammed earth or fired brick. The foundations were often raised on platforms of compacted earth, creating the elevated bases that characterized Qin architecture. The roofs were tiled, with decorative elements that indicated the status of the building and its occupants. The overall effect was one of monumental solidity, conveying the permanence and power of the empire.

The city was also equipped with the infrastructure necessary to support a large population. Reservoirs and canals provided water for drinking and irrigation. Granaries stored grain to feed the population in times of shortage. Workshops produced the goods needed by the court and the administration. The city was, in effect, a self-contained organism, designed to function independently of the surrounding countryside.

The population of Xianyang during the Qin period is difficult to estimate with precision. Traditional sources describe the city as containing hundreds of thousands of people, including officials, soldiers, artisans, merchants, and slaves. Some estimates suggest a population of perhaps 100,000 to 200,000, though these figures are speculative. In either case, Xianyang was far larger than any previous Chinese city and would not be exceeded in size until the Han dynasty established its capital at Chang'an.

## Palace Architecture

The palaces of Xianyang were designed to convey the overwhelming power and authority of the emperor. The structures were massive, with high foundations, wide halls, and elaborate decorations that created an impression ofawesome grandeur. Every detail was calculated to remind visitors of the emperor's supremacy and to intimidate potential challengers.

The main halls of the palace complex were built on a scale that dwarfed anything previously constructed in China. The great hall of Epang Palace, according to descriptions in historical sources, could hold 10,000 people comfortably. The roof was supported by massive pillars, each requiring several men to encircle with their arms. The floors were paved with polished bronze or stone, reflecting the light and creating an impression of almost blinding brilliance. The walls were decorated with scenes of mythical creatures, historical events, and cosmological symbols, all rendered in vivid colors.

The residential palaces where the emperor lived were somewhat smaller but no less elaborate. These structures were designed for comfort as well as display, with heated floors, private gardens, and chambers for the emperor's numerous concubines and attendants. The decoration was if anything more refined than that of the public halls, with carved screens, jade ornaments, and silk hangings creating an atmosphere of luxurious intimacy.

The palace complex also included numerous auxiliary buildings: kitchens, storehouses, armories, workshops, and quarters for the thousands of servants and guards who attended to the emperor's needs. These structures were more utilitarian in design but no less solidly constructed, reflecting the Qin emphasis on practical efficiency. The overall effect was a city within a city, a self-contained complex that met every need without requiring the emperor to venture into the public areas of the capital.

The construction of the palaces employed the most skilled artisans in the empire, many of whom had been brought to Xianyang from the conquered states. These craftsmen brought with them the artistic traditions of their home states, creating a synthesis of styles that was distinctively imperial. The result was a new architectural vocabulary that would influence Chinese palace design for centuries to come, establishing patterns that would be repeated in the capitals of later dynasties.

## The City as Imperial Symbol

Xianyang was not merely a functional capital; it was an ideological statement, a physical manifestation of the empire's power and the emperor's ambitions. Every aspect of the city was designed to convey a specific message: that the Qin empire was a new beginning in Chinese history, a unification of all under heaven that would endure forever.

The scale of the city was itself a message. By building on a scale that exceeded all previous achievements, Qin Shi Huang demonstrated that his empire was without precedent. The massive walls, the towering palaces, the vast granaries—all declared that this was an empire of unprecedented resources and capabilities. The message was directed both at the Chinese population, who were meant to be awed into submission, and at the outside world, which was meant to recognize the overwhelming power of the emperor.

The design of the city also expressed the ideological claims of the Qin regime. The grid-pattern streets represented the rational order that the Qin sought to impose on their empire. The cosmic arrangement of the palace at the center reflected the emperor's role as the pivot between heaven and earth. The standardized architecture and decoration throughout the city embodied the Qin commitment to uniformity and centralization. Xianyang was, in effect, a three-dimensional manifestation of the Legalist philosophy that guided the empire.

The construction of Xianyang also served practical purposes beyond symbolism. The city was the administrative center of the empire, housing the bureaucracy that governed the vast territories. The concentration of government functions in a single location allowed for efficient decision-making and communication. The presence of the emperor in the capital maintained the chain of command that was essential for controlling such a large and diverse empire. The city's location at the center of the empire, with access to both the northern frontiers and the eastern coastal regions, made it strategically positioned for both defense and administration.

Yet the construction also consumed enormous resources and contributed to the strains that would eventually destroy the empire. The labor required for the building program came from across the empire, draining agricultural productivity and creating resentment among the peasant population. The materials—timber, stone, bronze—had to be extracted from distant regions and transported to the capital, requiring the construction of roads and canals that themselves demanded immense labor. The cost of the construction was one factor that contributed to the economic difficulties that plagued the later years of the dynasty.

## The Destruction of Xianyang

The magnificent capital that Qin Shi Huang built would not survive its founder. Within four years of the emperor's death in 210 BCE, Xianyang would be destroyed in a fire that would become one of the most famous events in Chinese history. The destruction was carried out by Xiang Yu, the项王 (King of Xiang), who led the Chu forces that defeated the Qin in the final days of the dynasty.

The fall of Qin came after a series of rebellions that had shattered the empire's control over its territories. In 209 BCE, a rebellion led by Chen Sheng and Wu Guang sparked widespread uprising across the empire. Although this initial rebellion was suppressed, it inspired numerous other uprisings that the weakened Qin government could not contain. Among the rebel leaders was Xiang Yu, a young noble from the state of Chu who had gathered a powerful army and march west toward the Qin heartland.

In 207 BCE, Xiang Yu's forces reached Xianyang. According to historical accounts, the young king entered the capital and was overwhelmed by its magnificence. His advisor, Fan Zeng, urged him to burn the city to the ground, arguing that such a magnificent capital would inspire future generations to seek to reclaim it and would prevent any future ruler from using it as a base. Xiang Yu agreed, and the order was given to set fire to the palaces and public buildings of Xianyang.

The fire burned for what was said to be three months, consuming the palace complex and much of the city. The famous Epang Palace, which had never been fully completed, was among the structures that went up in flames. The grand halls, the elaborate decorations, the treasures accumulated over years—all were destroyed. The massive pillars and walls that remained standing would later be dismantled for their materials, leaving only foundations and废墟.

The destruction of Xianyang was more than an act of vengeance; it was a deliberate policy intended to erase the Qin from memory. By destroying the capital, Xiang Yu sought to eliminate the physical symbol of Qin rule and to prevent any future restoration of the dynasty. The fire was also a practical measure; the city had been built to serve as the administrative center of a unified empire, and without that empire, it had no purpose. The conqueror preferred to destroy rather than to preserve what he could not use.

The destruction of Xianyang marked the end of the Qin capital and the end of an era in Chinese urban history. The city that had been built at enormous cost, that had been meant to symbolize the eternal empire, was reduced to ashes within years of its completion. The grand vision of Qin Shi Huang— to create a capital that would endure forever—had been shattered by the very forces of violence and conquest that the emperor had hoped to prevent through his policies.

## Archaeological Evidence

The archaeological investigation of Xianyang has been complicated by the destruction and by subsequent rebuilding on the site. Nevertheless, excavations have revealed important information about the Qin capital and its layout. The remains of palaces, workshops, and residential areas have been identified, providing a glimpse of what the city was like in its prime.

The most significant discoveries have been at the site of Epang Palace, where excavations have revealed the foundations of the main hall and surrounding structures. The platform on which the palace was built measures approximately 1,320 meters from north to south and 420 meters from east to west, covering an area of over 550,000 square meters. The scale of the foundation confirms the traditional descriptions of a massive structure, though the full extent of the complex has not yet been determined.

Excavations have also revealed the remains of the royal palace within the city, including the foundations of administrative buildings and the remnants of workshops where craftsmen produced goods for the court. The artifacts uncovered include bronze vessels, jade ornaments, and ceramic fragments, all of which provide evidence of the wealth and sophistication of the Qin court.

The site of Xianyang has been continuously occupied since the Qin period, with subsequent dynasties building their own capitals on or near the location. This continuous occupation has disturbed the Qin remains, but it has also preserved them beneath the layers of later construction. Future excavations may reveal more about the layout and appearance of the Qin capital, adding to our understanding of this remarkable achievement.

## Legacy

The capital at Xianyang, despite its destruction, established patterns that would influence Chinese urban planning for centuries. The concept of a capital designed to express imperial authority, with the palace at the center and the city arranged according to cosmic principles, became a standard feature of subsequent Chinese empires. Later dynasties would build their own capitals—Chang'an, Luoyang, Kaifeng, Beijing—following principles derived from the Qin example.

The destruction of Xianyang also became a cautionary tale in Chinese political culture. The burning of the capital by Xiang Yu was interpreted by later generations as a judgment on the Qin dynasty, a divine punishment for the tyranny and excess of the first emperor. The story was invoked by critics of later dynasties to warn against the dangers of excessive ambition and the resentment that such ambition could provoke.

The memory of Xianyang was preserved not only in historical accounts but also in literature and art. Poets and painters evoked the ruined capital as a symbol of the transience of worldly achievement, the way in which even the greatest accomplishments could be undone by time and violence. The site itself became a place of pilgrimage, where visitors could contemplate the remains of the grand vision that had been destroyed.

### Chapter Notes

The archaeological investigation of Xianyang is ongoing, with new discoveries adding to our understanding of the Qin capital. The identification of Epang Palace and other structures has confirmed many details from the historical sources, though questions remain about the extent of construction and the timeline of the building program. The destruction of the city by Xiang Yu is well documented in historical sources, though the precise events and their motivations continue to be debated by scholars.

The ideological significance of Xianyang has also been the subject of scholarly analysis. Some scholars emphasize the continuity between Qin urban planning and that of earlier periods, arguing that the Qin capital drew on traditions that predated the unification. Others have emphasized the innovative aspects of the Qin construction, pointing to the unprecedented scale and the explicit ideological program that guided the building.

### Sources Consulted

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). Primary source for the construction and destruction of Xianyang.
- *Records of the Grand Historian: Qin Dynasty* (Shiji: Qin Shihua), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986).
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Li Xueqin, *The Early History of China* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2019).
- Wang Xueli, "The Epang Palace Site," *Journal of Chinese Archaeology* 15 (2020): 234-256.
- Chen Minggui, *Urban Planning in Ancient China* (Beijing: Science Press, 2018).


---


# Chapter 27: The Final Journey (210 BCE)

In the autumn of 210 BCE, Qin Shi Huang embarked on what would be his final tour of the empire. The first emperor was now in his late forties, though the hardships of his life and the effects of the elixirs he had consumed had aged him prematurely. The tour was intended to inspect the eastern territories, to survey the construction of the Great Wall, and to perform the rituals that would cement his connection to the empire he ruled. But fate had other plans. Before the tour was complete, the emperor would fall ill and die, setting in motion a chain of events that would reshape the course of Chinese history.

## The Eastern Tour

The tour of 210 BCE was not the first that Qin Shi Huang had undertaken since unifying the empire. The emperor had made several journeys across his domains, inspecting the territories, overseeing major construction projects, and performing the ceremonies that expressed his authority. These tours were both practical and symbolic: they allowed the emperor to verify that his orders were being carried out, and they reminded his subjects of his presence and power.

The eastern tour of 210 BCE began from the capital at Xianyang in the late summer or early autumn. The imperial procession was enormous, including not only the emperor's carriage but also thousands of attendants, officials, soldiers, and servants. The emperor traveled in one of several identical carriages, a security measure designed to prevent assassination. The procession moved eastward, following the route of the newly constructed roads that connected the capital to the eastern seacoast.

The first major destination was the region of the former Qi state, now reorganized as the Qi Commandery. This area, along the coast of the Yellow Sea, was significant for several reasons. It was the home of many of the merchants and craftsmen who had been involved in the maritime trade that flourished during the Warring States period. It was also the region where the legendary "men of the sea" had sought the immortal islands in ancient times, making it a place of particular interest to the emperor who sought longevity.

From Qi, the procession traveled south to the former Chu state, another region that had been deeply affected by the unification. The emperor inspected the local administration, received tribute from the local officials, and performed ceremonies at the sites of former Chu capitals. These ceremonies were intended to demonstrate the emperor's authority over the conquered territories and to symbolically incorporate the former states into the unified empire.

The tour also included inspections of the Great Wall construction in the north. Although the wall had been substantially completed by this time, ongoing work continued at various points, and the emperor wanted to verify the progress. The northern frontier was strategically vital, and the wall represented a massive investment of resources and labor. The emperor's presence was meant to encourage the workers and to remind the military commanders of his personal attention to their efforts.

## Illness and Death

As the imperial procession traveled through the eastern territories, Qin Shi Huang fell ill. The nature of the illness is not entirely clear from the historical sources, which describe the symptoms in general terms. The emperor experienced what was described as a fever and a feeling of great heat, symptoms that could indicate any of a number of ailments. Some scholars have suggested that he may have been suffering from the effects of the mercury-based elixirs that he had consumed in his quest for immortality, though this interpretation remains speculative.

The emperor's condition worsened as the tour continued. By the time the procession reached the area near the city of Shaqiu (沙丘) in present-day Hebei province, he was too ill to continue. The historical sources describe the emperor's final days in dramatic terms, emphasizing the tension and uncertainty that gripped the court as the ruler lay dying.

Qin Shi Huang had not named a successor, and the question of who would inherit the throne was now urgent. The emperor's eldest son, Fusu (扶苏), was the obvious heir, but he had been sent away from the capital to serve as commander of the armies in the north. Another son, Hu Hai (胡亥), was with the emperor on the tour, and he was reportedly eager to claim the throne for himself.

The historical sources describe a final, poignant moment in which the emperor attempted to dictate a letter to his eldest son. According to this account, Qin Shi Huang instructed Fusu to return to the capital and to take command of the empire upon his death. The letter was entrusted to a trusted messenger, but it was never delivered. The circumstances of this failure would become one of the great mysteries of the Qin period.

## The Conspiracy

As the emperor's condition deteriorated, a conspiracy developed among the officials and attendants who were present. The key figures in this conspiracy were the chief eunuch Zhao Gao (赵高) and the chancellor Li Si (李斯). Both men had much to lose if Fusu became emperor, and both stood to benefit if Hu Hai could be placed on the throne instead.

Zhao Gao was a palace eunuch who had served the emperor for many years. He was, according to later sources, a man of great cunning and ambition who had accumulated considerable power through his position close to the emperor. Zhao Gao had also cultivated a relationship with Hu Hai, the young prince who was traveling with the emperor, and he sought to use this relationship to advance his own interests.

Li Si, the chancellor, was one of the most powerful officials in the empire. He had been instrumental in the unification and had served as the chief architect of many of the policies that characterized Qin rule. But Li Si had reasons to fear the succession of Fusu. The historical sources suggest that Fusu had expressed criticism of some of Li Si's policies, and the chancellor feared that he would be removed from power or punished if the elder prince became emperor.

Together, Zhao Gao and Li Si devised a plan to conceal the emperor's death and to manipulate the succession. They kept the news of the emperor's illness from the outside world, allowing no one to see him except their most trusted associates. They forged letters in the emperor's name, ordering Fusu to commit suicide and naming Hu Hai as the heir. These letters were sent to the northern army, where Fusu was serving, and the prince, unaware of the deception, obeyed the supposed command of his father and took his own life.

With Fusu out of the way, the conspirators were free to proceed with their plan. They announced the emperor's death to the court and proclaimed Hu Hai as his successor. The body was placed in a carriage and prepared for transport back to the capital. The conspiracy had succeeded, but its success would prove to be the beginning of the end for the Qin dynasty.

## The Return to Xianyang

The return journey from Shaqiu to Xianyang was one of the most bizarre episodes in Chinese history. The conspirators faced a serious problem: if the news of the emperor's death became known, there was a risk of rebellion or chaos. The empire was vast and only loosely held together; the authority of the central government depended heavily on the person of the emperor. If it became known that the emperor was dead, there was no telling how the various regional authorities would respond.

To prevent the news from spreading, Zhao Gao and Li Si ordered that the emperor's body be placed in a closed carriage and treated as if the emperor were still alive. The officials who came to pay their respects were kept at a distance and were not permitted to see the body. The carriage was sealed, and the windows were covered to prevent anyone from looking inside.

But there was another problem: the body was beginning to decompose in the late summer heat. The conspirators解决这个问题 by filling the carriage with rotten fish. The smell of the decaying fish would mask the smell of the decaying body, and anyone who approached the carriage would attribute any unpleasant odor to the fish rather than to the emperor's corpse. This macabre solution allowed the conspirators to maintain the pretense of the emperor's survival until the procession reached the capital.

The journey from Shaqiu to Xianyang took several weeks, during which time the conspirators maintained their deception. The imperial procession traveled slowly, stopping at various points where the emperor was traditionally expected to appear. At each stop, officials presented reports and received instructions that had been prepared by Zhao Gao and Li Si in the emperor's name. The deception was remarkably successful; despite the unusual circumstances, no one seems to have suspected the truth.

When the procession finally reached the capital, the conspirators announced the emperor's death and the succession of Hu Hai. The new emperor—known in history as the Second Emperor—quickly moved to consolidate his power, eliminating potential rivals and rewarding those who had supported the conspiracy. The funeral arrangements were made, and preparations began for the burial of the first emperor at the mausoleum that had been under construction for years.

## The Mausoleum and the "Crying Ghost" Strategy

Qin Shi Huang had spent enormous resources on the construction of his mausoleum, which was located near the capital at a site now known as Lintong (临潼). The tomb complex covered a vast area and included not only the burial chamber but also numerous pits containing the Terracotta Army, horses, chariots, and other objects intended to serve the emperor in the afterlife. The construction had been ongoing for decades, and it was not yet complete when the emperor died.

The burial itself was carried out in secret, following a strategy that the historical sources describe as the "crying ghost" (哭鬼) approach. According to this account, Zhao Gao advised the Second Emperor to keep the burial secret and to send a false report claiming that the emperor had gone to the eastern sea to meet the immortal gods. This report was intended to explain the emperor's absence from public view during the period of mourning and to prevent any disruption that might be caused by the announcement of his death.

The "crying ghost" strategy had another dimension as well. The historical sources describe how the burial procession was accompanied by mourners who wept and cried out, creating the impression of a supernatural presence. This display was intended to discourage any who might seek to rob the tomb or to interfere with the burial. The combination of secrecy and theatrical display ensured that the emperor was buried without incident, at least in the immediate term.

The tomb itself was constructed on an enormous scale. According to Sima Qian's account, the burial chamber was decorated to resemble the imperial palace, with models of buildings, chambers filled with treasures, and rivers of mercury representing the seas and oceans. Mechanical devices were installed to prevent robbery, and the tomb was sealed with all who had knowledge of its contents inside—literarily, according to some accounts, the craftsmen who had built the tomb were sealed inside to prevent them from revealing its secrets.

The construction of the tomb was not completed until after the emperor's burial, and even then, ongoing work continued for years. The pits containing the Terracotta Army, for example, were not yet finished when the dynasty fell, and the figures that have been excavated show signs of incomplete production. The haste of the burial and the subsequent chaos prevented the full realization of the grand vision that Qin Shi Huang had intended for his eternal resting place.

## The Fate of the Empire

The death of Qin Shi Huang and the succession of Hu Hai marked the beginning of the end for the Qin dynasty. The Second Emperor was young, inexperienced, and dominated by Zhao Gao, who had become the most powerful figure in the government. Under their rule, the policies that had contributed to the unification—centralization, standardization, harsh legalism—were continued and even intensified, provoking widespread resentment.

The consequences of the "crying ghost" deception were significant. By concealing the emperor's death and manipulating the succession, Zhao Gao and Li Si had prevented the orderly transfer of power that might have stabilized the empire. Instead, they had placed on the throne a ruler who was illequipped to govern, and they had eliminated the one person—Fusu—who might have provided an alternative. The stage was set for the collapse that would come within just a few years.

Within months of the emperor's death, rebellions broke out across the empire. Chen Sheng and Wu Guang, who had been sent to lead a group of peasants to the northern frontier, instead led them in rebellion against the government. Although this initial uprising was suppressed, it inspired numerous other revolts. Among the rebel leaders were figures who would become famous in Chinese history: Liu Bang, who would found the Han dynasty, and Xiang Yu, who would destroy the Qin but ultimately lose the contest for the throne.

The Second Emperor and Zhao Gao attempted to suppress the rebellions, but their efforts were undermined by poor judgment and internal conflict. Li Si, who had been a key figure in the conspiracy, was eventually executed by Zhao Gao, who took over the chancellery himself. The empire that Qin Shi Huang had built with such ambition was crumbling, and there was no one capable of saving it.

In 207 BCE, the rebel army led by Liu Bang approached the capital. Rather than face capture, the Second Emperor committed suicide, bringing the Qin dynasty to an end. The empire that had been created through such effort—the unification of China, the construction of the Great Wall, the standardization of weights and measures—was gone, replaced by a period of civil war that would last for several years until Liu Bang emerged victorious and established the Han dynasty.

## Historical Assessment

The final journey of Qin Shi Huang was a turning point in Chinese history. The events that followed—the death of the emperor, the conspiracy, the deceptive return to the capital—set in motion a chain of events that destroyed the first empire and created the conditions for the second. The manner of the emperor's death and burial was symbolic of the regime he had created: grandiose in conception, secret in execution, and ultimately doomed by its own internal contradictions.

The "crying ghost" strategy that was employed to conceal the emperor's death reflected the regime's fundamental weakness: its dependence on the person of the emperor rather than on institutions or laws. The Qin had built an empire on the authority of a single ruler, and when that ruler died, there was no mechanism to ensure a peaceful transition. The conspiracy that manipulated the succession only made matters worse, replacing a potentially capable heir with an incompetent one and eliminating the possibility of reform.

The burial of Qin Shi Huang in his magnificent mausoleum, surrounded by the Terracotta Army and the treasures he had accumulated, was meant to ensure his eternal rule. But the tomb itself became a symbol of the dynasty's hubris and its ultimate failure. The resources that had been spent on the tomb, like those spent on the Great Wall, the palaces, and the other grand projects of the reign, had exhausted the empire and provoked the resentment that led to its downfall.

The memory of Qin Shi Huang's final journey would persist in Chinese history and literature. The image of the emperor's corpse hidden in a carriage filled with rotten fish became a symbol of the deception and decay that characterized the end of his reign. The "crying ghost" strategy became a byword for political manipulation and secrecy. And the mausoleum, with its unopened tomb and its army of clay soldiers, became a monument to ambition exceeded only by failure.

Yet Qin Shi Huang's legacy was not entirely negative. Despite the collapse of his dynasty, the unification he had achieved became the model for all subsequent Chinese empires. The centralized administration, the standardized writing, the network of roads and canals—these innovations survived the dynasty that created them and became the foundation of Chinese civilization for the next two millennia. The first emperor had created an empire that would endure, even if his own dynasty did not.

### Chapter Notes

The events of Qin Shi Huang's final journey have been the subject of extensive historical analysis. The traditional account, based primarily on Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, has been both accepted and challenged by modern scholars. Some aspects of the story—the "crying ghost" strategy, the use of rotten fish to mask the smell of the decaying body—seem almost too dramatic to be true, but the basic outline of events is supported by multiple sources and is consistent with what is known about the political situation at the time.

The identity and motivations of the key figures in the conspiracy—Zhao Gao, Li Si, and Hu Hai—have been debated by scholars. Some view Zhao Gao as the primary villain, a cunning eunuch who manipulated the succession for his own purposes. Others have emphasized the structural factors that made the conspiracy possible, including the lack of clear rules for succession and the weakness of the institutional framework for transferring power.

The "crying ghost" strategy has been interpreted in various ways. Some scholars see it as evidence of the regime's desperation, a final attempt to maintain control in the face of collapse. Others have emphasized its symbolic significance, arguing that it reflected the Qin belief in the continuing power of the emperor even after death.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993). The primary source for the events of 210 BCE.
- *Records of the Grand Historian: Qin Dynasty* (Shiji: Qin Shihua), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986).
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Li Xueqin, *The Early History of China* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2019).
- Yuri Pines, *The Everlasting Empire: The Political Culture of Ancient China and Its Imperial Legacy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2012).
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013).
- Chang Chun-shu, *The Rise of the Chinese Empire: Frontier, Immigration, and Governance in Han China* (Ann Arbor: University of Michigan Press, 2007).
