# Chapter 28: The Conspiracy of Hu Hai

The enthronement of Hu Hai as the Second Emperor of Qin marked one of the most consequential moments in Chinese history. In the summer of 210 BCE, the young prince—barely in his twenties—ascended to the throne of the empire his father had created through decades of war and consolidation. Yet this succession was not the product of legitimate inheritance or imperial wisdom. It was the result of a conspiracy orchestrated by a palace eunuch and a chancellor who had manipulated the death of Qin Shi Huang to place their favored candidate on the throne. The consequences of this manipulation would unfold with devastating speed, as the empire that had taken generations to build began to unravel within months of the new emperor's accession.

## The Second Emperor Ascends

When the news of Qin Shi Huang's death was officially announced in Xianyang, the court was presented with a fait accompli. Hu Hai was proclaimed emperor, and the funeral arrangements for the first emperor began immediately. The new ruler was young, inexperienced, and utterly dependent on the men who had placed him on the throne. Zhao Gao, the chief eunuch who had orchestrated the conspiracy, became the de facto ruler of the empire, while Li Si, the chancellor, continued to manage the day-to-day affairs of government.

The Second Emperor's first actions were designed to eliminate potential rivals and to consolidate his position. The most pressing threat was Fusu, the elder prince who had been sent to command the northern army. Even though Fusu had been ordered to commit suicide in the forged edict, Zhao Gao and Li Si could not be certain that the order had been carried out until they received confirmation. When reports arrived confirming Fusu's death, the conspirators breathed a momentary sigh of relief—but their relief was premature, for Fusu was not the only potential rival.

Several other princes of the Qin royal house remained in the capital, and some of them had greater military experience or more support among the officials than Hu Hai. The Second Emperor moved quickly to eliminate these threats. Over the following months, he ordered the execution of twelve princes who had been sent to the eastern commanderies, as well as several princesses. The killings were carried out in secret, and the bodies were buried in a common grave—a execution method that was meant to humiliate the victims and to demonstrate the new emperor's ruthlessness.

The elimination of the royal family was followed by a purge of officials who might have been loyal to Fusu or who might oppose the new regime. Meng Tian, the famed general who had commanded the northern armies and overseen the construction of the Great Wall, was a particular target. Although Meng Tian had been a loyal servant of Qin Shi Huang, his relationship with Fusu made him a threat to the new emperor. Zhao Gao contrived to have Meng Tian arrested and imprisoned, and he was eventually forced to commit suicide along with his brother Meng Yi. The death of Meng Tian eliminated one of the most capable military leaders in the empire—a man whose skills would have been desperately needed in the months and years to come.

## The Consolidation of Zhao Gao's Power

With the elimination of potential rivals, Zhao Gao moved to consolidate his own position within the government. The eunuch had become the Second Emperor's closest advisor, and he used this position to accumulate power and to eliminate those who might challenge his authority. Li Si, who had been a key partner in the original conspiracy, soon found himself marginalized and eventually eliminated.

The conflict between Zhao Gao and Li Si began almost immediately after the enthronement. Li Si, as chancellor, held a position of great formal authority, and he had not been consulted on many of the decisions that Zhao Gao was making. The eunuch, meanwhile, resented Li Si's influence and sought to reduce it. The struggle between the two men would ultimately end with Li Si's execution, but not before the empire had suffered significant damage.

According to the historical accounts, the immediate cause of Li Si's downfall was a dispute over how to handle the rebellions that were beginning to erupt across the empire. When news of the Chen Sheng uprising reached the capital, Li Si and other officials urged the Second Emperor to take immediate action to suppress the revolt and to address the grievances that had provoked it. Zhao Gao, however, counseled delay and secrecy, arguing that the emperor should not be troubled with such matters. The historical sources describe how Zhao Gao manipulated the situation to turn the Second Emperor against Li Si, eventually convincing him that the chancellor was a traitor.

Li Si was arrested on charges of conspiracy and was executed along with his entire family. The method of execution was particularly brutal: Li Si was subjected to the five-punishments (the most severe form of corporal punishment in Qin law) and then executed. His death removed one of the last checks on Zhao Gao's power, and the eunuch now became the supreme authority in the empire, holding the title of chancellor while the Second Emperor withdrew into increasingly debauched pleasures.

## The Rule of the Second Emperor

Hu Hai, as the Second Emperor, proved to be one of the most incompetent rulers in Chinese history. The young emperor, who had never been prepared for governance, was easily dominated by Zhao Gao and spent his time in indulgence rather than in the duties of state. The historical sources describe a ruler who was obsessed with pleasure, who took delight in cruelty, and who was utterly indifferent to the suffering of his subjects.

According to Sima Qian's account, the Second Emperor's excesses knew no bounds. He continued the construction of his father's mausoleum, adding to its grandeur while ignoring the needs of the living. He expanded the imperial palaces, building new structures to house his collection of concubines and treasures. He instituted heavy taxes to fund his pleasures, extracting from the peasants the resources they needed to survive. The legal harshness that had characterized Qin rule was not only maintained but intensified, as the emperor and his minister sought to crush any expression of dissent.

The Second Emperor's cruelty extended to members of his own court. When he learned that some of the officials disagreed with his policies, he ordered their execution. When he suspected that some of the local officials were not fully loyal, he had them killed and replaced with men who would do his bidding regardless of the consequences. The terror that Zhao Gao had cultivated in the conspiracy now became a tool of governance, as the regime sought to maintain control through fear rather than through legitimacy.

The regime's paranoia reached its apex in what became known as the "slandering of the bonnets" (誣告罪), a policy that encouraged the accusation of innocent people. According to this policy, anyone could be accused of crimes, and the accusers were rewarded for their information. The result was a wave of denunciations that swept through the empire, as people accused their neighbors, their rivals, and even their family members in hopes of gaining favor or avoiding punishment. The legal system was overwhelmed, and the prisons were filled with prisoners who had been condemned without fair trial.

## The Transformation of the Empire

The combination of the Second Emperor's incompetence and Zhao Gao's ruthless consolidation of power produced a transformation in the character of the Qin regime. The first emperor, whatever his faults, had been a ruler of vision who had unified the empire and imposed a coherent system of governance. His son, by contrast, was a puppet whose only concern was his own pleasure, and the minister who controlled him was interested only in his own power. The empire that Qin Shi Huang had built began to collapse under the weight of its own contradictions.

The policies that had been implemented during the first emperor's reign were continued, but they were implemented in their most extreme form without any of the balancing considerations that Qin Shi Huang had sometimes shown. The heavy taxes that had funded the Great Wall and the imperial projects were increased to fund the Second Emperor's pleasures. The harsh laws that had maintained order were applied with increased severity, as local officials sought to demonstrate their loyalty by exceeding the quotas for arrests and punishments. The forced labor that had built the roads and palaces was expanded to include even more massive construction projects.

The result was a regime that had become a caricature of its former self. The Legalist philosophy that had guided Qin governance was not merely continued but degenerated into a form of naked oppression. The Legalists had argued that a strong state required strong laws and a capable ruler; under the Second Emperor, the laws remained strong, but the ruler was weak, and the enforcement of the laws served only to enrich and empower the officials who administered them.

The transformation was particularly evident in the treatment of the conquered peoples. During Qin Shi Huang's reign, there had been some effort to integrate the former states into a unified empire, to use the conquered elites as local administrators, and to present the unification as a positive development. Under the Second Emperor, this effort was abandoned entirely. The former Chu, Qi, Wei, Yan, and Zhao peoples were treated as subject populations to be exploited, and any expression of regional identity was suppressed with extreme prejudice.

## The Court Intrigues

The internal dynamics of the Second Emperor's court were characterized by a complex web of intrigue, manipulation, and betrayal. Zhao Gao, having eliminated Li Si and other potential rivals, was now the supreme power behind the throne, but his position was not entirely secure. He had to maintain the appearance of loyalty to the emperor while simultaneously manipulating the young ruler for his own purposes. The court became a place where anyone could become a victim of slander or accusation, where survival depended on one's ability to navigate the treacherous waters of imperial politics.

One of the most notorious episodes of this period was the execution of Li Si, which was followed by the execution of numerous other officials who had served the first emperor. The historical sources describe how Zhao Gao convinced the Second Emperor that these officials were plotting against him, and how the young emperor readily believed these accusations. The result was a series of purges that eliminated almost everyone who had experience in governing the empire.

The Second Emperor's own court was dominated by a group of young men who had been his companions in pleasure. These favorites, many of them from the same region as the emperor, were given positions of power and influence regardless of their abilities. They became the targets of Zhao Gao's manipulations, as the eunuch sought to turn the emperor against anyone who might challenge his authority.

The isolation of the Second Emperor from any honest counsel was nearly complete. No one dared to tell the emperor the truth about the situation in the empire, for fear of being accused of disloyalty. The officials who might have provided guidance were either dead, in prison, or too terrified to speak honestly. The result was a ruler who was completely out of touch with reality, who believed that the empire was prosperous and peaceful when in fact it was on the brink of collapse.

## The Empire's Transformation Completed

By the end of 210 BCE, the Qin Empire had been fundamentally transformed. The regime that Qin Shi Huang had created was still nominally in place—the institutions, the laws, the administrative structures all remained—but their character had been changed beyond recognition. The empire that had been built on the vision of a great ruler was now being destroyed by the shortsightedness of his successor and the ambition of a treacherous minister.

The transformation was not only political but also cultural and psychological. The sense of purpose that had characterized the first emperor's reign—the feeling that the unification of China was a great historical achievement, that the construction of the Great Wall was a noble endeavor, that the standardization of the empire was a beneficial reform—all of this had been lost. In its place was a sense of despair, resentment, and hopelessness. The people who had welcomed the unification as a end to the wars of the Warring States period now regretted the day that Qin had conquered their lands.

The stage was now set for the dramatic events that would unfold over the next three years. The empire that had seemed so permanent, so powerful, so destined to last forever, was about to be swept away by a wave of rebellion that would ultimately give rise to a new dynasty. But before the rebellions could begin in earnest, one more act of betrayal remained: the final consolidation of Zhao Gao's power and the complete isolation of the Second Emperor from any possibility of reform.

### Chapter Notes

The conspiracy that placed Hu Hai on the throne has been the subject of extensive historical analysis. The traditional account, based primarily on Sima Qian's Records of the Grand Historian, presents Zhao Gao as the primary villain—a cunning eunuch who manipulated the succession for his own purposes. Modern scholars have sometimes challenged this interpretation, arguing that the conspiracy was more complex and that Zhao Gao may have been acting in response to structural factors rather than purely personal ambition.

The fate of Li Si has also been debated. Some scholars view him as a tragic figure—a capable administrator who was eliminated by a treacherous colleague. Others have argued that Li Si was himself complicit in the original conspiracy and that his eventual downfall was a consequence of his own actions.

The characterization of the Second Emperor as a debauched and incompetent ruler is based primarily on the hostile sources that were written after the dynasty's fall. Some modern scholars have suggested that the sources may have exaggerated the Second Emperor's faults for political reasons, as part of the Han dynasty's effort to delegitimize the Qin.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).
- *Records of the Grand Historian: Qin Dynasty* (Shiji: Qin Shihua), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986).
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Yuri Pines, *The Everlasting Empire: The Political Culture of Ancient China and Its Imperial Legacy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2012).
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013).
- Robin Yates, "The Qin Dynasty: A Collaborative Enterprise," in *The History of Ancient China*, ed. Michael Puett (Ithaca: Cornell University Press, 2020).

# Chapter 29: The Collapse (209-207 BCE)

In the autumn of 209 BCE, a group of exhausted soldiers trudged through the marshes of present-day Anhui province, their uniforms torn, their spirits broken. They were heading toward the northern frontier, where they were to reinforce the armies guarding the Great Wall against the Xiongnu. But these were not ordinary soldiers. They were the survivors of a band of nine hundred peasants who had been conscripted for military service, and they were about to spark a rebellion that would bring down the Qin dynasty within two years. The uprising of Chen Sheng and Wu Guang at Dazexiang was not merely a local disturbance; it was the first act of a drama that would reshape the political map of China and establish the conditions for the founding of the Han dynasty.

## The Dazexiang Uprising

The immediate cause of the Dazexiang uprising was the failure of the Qin government to fulfill its obligations to the soldiers who were conscripted for military service. According to the legal codes of the Qin, all adult males were required to serve in the army or to perform labor service for the state. The conscription system was demanding, and the conditions of service were often brutal. Soldiers were expected to report for duty on time, and those who arrived late were subject to severe punishment—even execution.

In the summer of 209 BCE, a group of nine hundred peasants from the region of Anhui (formerly the Chu state) were conscripted for military service and ordered to proceed to the northern frontier. The journey was long and difficult, and the group was led by two men named Chen Sheng and Wu Guang. As they traveled, they encountered heavy rains and flooding that slowed their progress. They soon realized that they would not be able to reach their destination on time, and according to Qin law, they would be subject to execution for their failure.

Faced with the choice between certain death and rebellion, Chen Sheng and Wu Guang chose the latter. They killed the officers who were escorting them and raised the standard of revolt. The rebellion began small—a handful of desperate men in a remote region—but it quickly grew as other groups of conscripts and oppressed peasants joined the cause. Chen Sheng first declared himself "General of Chu," *then* "King of Rising Chu" (張楚, Zhang Chu) after momentum grew—reflecting tactical legitimacy-building.

The significance of the Dazexiang uprising cannot be overstated. It was the first major rebellion against Qin rule since the unification, and it demonstrated that the empire was far more fragile than anyone had imagined. The Qin had maintained order through a combination of military force and administrative control, but they had never established legitimacy in the eyes of the conquered peoples. When the opportunity arose to resist, the resentment that had been building for years exploded into open rebellion.

## The Revival of the Six States

The Dazexiang uprising inspired rebellions across the empire. In region after region, the descendants of the former royal families emerged to claim the thrones of their ancestors. The six states that Qin had conquered—the six kingdoms that had been destroyed in the wars of unification—were reborn, as local leaders and noble families rallied to restore the old order. The map of China, which had been simplified by Qin unification, became complex again, as dozens of small kingdoms and warlord states vied for power.

In the east, the former Qi state was restored, with a descendant of the Qi royal family placed on the throne. In the south, the former Chu state was reestablished, with Chen Sheng claiming the title of king. In the north, the former Zhao and Yan states were revived, with local leaders declaring independence from Qin rule. In the central plains, the former Wei and Han states were also restored, as the Qin administrative system collapsed under the pressure of rebellion.

The revival of the six states was not merely a symbolic gesture. Each of the new kingdoms raised its own army, appointed its own officials, and governed its own territory. The Qin government, which had struggled to maintain control even in peacetime, was completely overwhelmed by the scope of the rebellion. The imperial armies were stretched thin, and the local officials either joined the rebellions or were killed by the insurgents.

The rapidity of the collapse was breathtaking. Within months of the Dazexiang uprising, the Qin Empire had ceased to function as a unified state. The central government at Xianyang still existed, and the Second Emperor still held the title of emperor, but their authority extended only to the territory immediately around the capital. Everywhere else, independent kingdoms had been established, and the Qin commanderies had been either overthrown or forced to negotiate with the rebels.

## The Campaigns of Xiang Yu and Liu Bang

Among the many rebel leaders who emerged two would during this period, come for power to dominate the struggle Yu and: Xiang Liu Bang. These two men, who would become the great rivals of the early Han period, represented different approaches to the rebellion and different visions for the future of China.

Xiang Yu was a young nobleman from the former Chu state, a descendant of the Chu royal family who had fled during the Qin conquest. He was a military leader of extraordinary ability, renowned for his courage, his charisma, and his skill in battle. When the rebellion against Qin rule began, Xiang Yu raised an army of his own and quickly established himself as one of the most powerful rebel leaders. His uncle, Xiang Li, served as his advisor and helped to coordinate his campaigns.

Xiang Yu's military campaigns were characterized by boldness and brutality. He led his armies in a series of devastating attacks against Qin forces, inflicting crushing defeats on the imperial armies wherever he encountered them. His most famous victory was the Battle of Julu (巨鹿之戰) in 207 BCE, where he defeated a Qin army of several hundred thousand men, capturing or killing almost the entire force. The battle was a turning point in the war, as it shattered the Qin army's ability to resist the rebels and opened the road to the capital.

Liu Bang, by contrast, was a man of humble origins. He had been a peasant or a minor official in the former Qin territory, and he had risen to leadership through a combination of military skill and political acumen. When the rebellion began, Liu Bang led his own small band of followers and gradually expanded his territory through a series of campaigns in the east. He was less brilliant than Xiang Yu as a military commander, but he was more patient and more skilled at building alliances.

Liu Bang's approach to the rebellion was different from Xiang Yu's. While Xiang Yu sought to destroy the Qin military through decisive battles, Liu Bang sought to undermine the Qin administration through political maneuvering. He made alliances with other rebel leaders, promised land and titles to those who joined him, and cultivated the support of the local populations in the territories he conquered. His campaigns were slower but more sustainable, and he was able to establish a stable base of operations in the east.

The relationship between Xiang Yu and Liu Bang was complex. Initially, they were allies against the common enemy, and they fought together in some campaigns. But as the rebellion progressed, tensions developed between them. Xiang Yu, as the senior nobleman, expected to be recognized as the leader of the rebellion, while Liu Bang, with his growing power, had ambitions of his own. The stage was set for the conflict that would determine the future of China.

## The Fall of the Second Emperor

While the rebel armies were gaining strength in the east, the situation in the Qin capital was deteriorating rapidly. The Second Emperor, isolated in his palace and dominated by Zhao Gao, was completely out of touch with reality. He continued to indulge in pleasure while the empire crumbled around him, and he refused to acknowledge the gravity of the situation until it was too late.

Zhao Gao, who had consolidated his power through the elimination of rivals, now faced a crisis that he could not control. The rebel armies were advancing, the administrative system was collapsing, and the imperial forces were failing. The eunuch attempted to maintain the pretense of normalcy, but it was clear to everyone that the dynasty was on the brink of collapse. Zhao Gao's response was to become even more ruthless, executing anyone who suggested that the situation was hopeless and demanding ever-greater efforts from the remaining loyal officials.

The final crisis came in 207 BCE, when the rebel army under Liu Bang approached the capital. The Qin forces that had been sent to stop the rebels had been defeated, and there were no more armies to defend the city. The Second Emperor, confronted with the reality of his situation, realized that he could not escape. According to the historical sources, he asked to be allowed to retreat to the western region and to rule as a king, but this request was rejected. On the first day of the twelfth month of 207 BCE, the Second Emperor committed suicide, ending the Qin dynasty.

The death of the Second Emperor marked the end of the Qin dynasty, but it did not immediately end the conflict. Zhao Gao, who had orchestrated so much of the tragedy, tried to maintain power by placing a puppet emperor on the throne, but his regime lasted only a few months. In 206 BCE, the rebel armies under Xiang Yu entered Xianyang, and the capital was sacked. The magnificent palaces that Qin Shi Huang had built were burned, and the treasures that had been accumulated over decades were looted. The empire that had been created through such effort was destroyed, and China entered a period of civil war that would last for several years.

## The Aftermath and the Rise of Han

The fall of the Qin dynasty was followed by a period of intense conflict known as the Chu-Han Contention (楚漢之爭), during which Xiang Yu and Liu Bang fought for control of China. This period, which lasted from 206 to 202 BCE, was characterized by shifting alliances, military campaigns, and political maneuvering. Both sides claimed to be the legitimate successors to the Qin, and both had support among the population.

Xiang Yu, as the more powerful military leader, initially seemed likely to win. He had defeated the Qin armies, destroyed the dynasty, and partitioned the empire among his followers. He established himself as the "Hegemon-King of Western Chu" (西楚霸王) and awarded the various former territories to his allies and generals. However, Xiang Yu's political judgment was poor, and he made enemies quickly. He alienated many of the former Qin territories by treating them as conquered lands rather than as partners in the new order.

Liu Bang, by contrast, proved to be the more effective political leader. He had established his base in the eastern region, and he had cultivated the support of the local populations. He promised land reform, reduced taxes, and governed in a relatively benign manner. He built alliances with other rebel leaders and with the former Qin officials who were willing to serve the new regime. His patience and political skill gradually gave him the advantage.

The decisive moment came in 202 BCE, when Liu Bang's forces defeated Xiang Yu at the Battle of Gaixia (垓下之戰). Xiang Yu, surrounded and outnumbered, committed suicide, and Liu Bang became the supreme ruler of China. He established the Han dynasty, with himself as Emperor Gaozu (漢高祖), and began the task of rebuilding the empire that had been destroyed by the collapse of Qin.

## Historical Assessment

The collapse of the Qin dynasty was one of the most dramatic political transformations in Chinese history. In the span of just three years, an empire that had seemed permanent was destroyed, and a new order emerged from the chaos. The events of 209-207 BCE demonstrated the fragility of the Qin regime and the depth of the resentment that had accumulated during its rule.

The causes of the collapse were multiple and complex. The Qin regime had never established legitimacy in the eyes of the conquered peoples, and it had relied on force rather than consent to maintain order. The policies of the Second Emperor and Zhao Gao had accelerated the regime's decline, alienating even those who might have supported the dynasty. The rebellions that broke out were not merely local disturbances but expressions of deep-seated resentment against Qin rule.

The role of individual actors in the collapse was also significant. The Second Emperor's incompetence and Zhao Gao's treachery removed any possibility of reform or recovery. The rebel leaders—Chen Sheng, Xiang Yu, Liu Bang, and others—exploited the weaknesses of the regime to their advantage. The collapse was not inevitable, but it was made much more likely by the decisions of the individuals who shaped the events of the period.

Yet the collapse of Qin also created opportunities. The Han dynasty that emerged from the chaos inherited many of the institutions and practices of the Qin, including the centralized administration, the commandery system, and the standardized writing. The unification that Qin had achieved was preserved, even though the dynasty itself had fallen. The legacy of Qin would continue to shape Chinese civilization for millennia.

### Chapter Notes

The collapse of the Qin dynasty has been the subject of extensive historical analysis. The traditional account, which emphasizes the role of the Second Emperor's incompetence and Zhao Gao's treachery, has been both accepted and challenged by modern scholars. Some historians have argued that the structural weaknesses of the Qin regime were more important than individual failures, while others have emphasized the role of the rebel leaders in shaping the outcome.

The Dazexiang uprising has been the subject of particular interest, as it is often seen as the beginning of the end for Qin. The historical sources describe the events in some detail, but there are gaps and inconsistencies that have been debated by scholars. Some have questioned whether the traditional account gives too much emphasis to the role of Chen Sheng and Wu Guang, arguing that the uprising was part of a broader pattern of resistance.

The Chu-Han Contention is also complex, and the historical sources present conflicting accounts of the relationship between Xiang Yu and Liu Bang. The traditional portrayal of Xiang Yu as a heroic but ultimately tragic figure and Liu Bang as a wise and benevolent founder has been challenged by modern scholars, who have emphasized the political and military dimensions of their conflict.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).
- Ban Gu, *Book of Han* (*Hanshu*), trans. Homer H. Dubs (New York: Columbia University Press, 1938).

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986).
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Michael Loewe, *The Han Dynasty: From the Fall of the Han to the Rise of the Xin* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2019).
- Yuri Pines, *The Everlasting Empire: The Political Culture of Ancient China and Its Imperial Legacy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2012).
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013).
- Xiaofei Tian, *Beacon Fire and Shooting Star: The Collapse of the Qin Dynasty* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2020).

# Chapter 30: The Burial at Mount Li

In the shadow of Mount Li (驪山), approximately thirty-five kilometers northeast of modern Xi'an, lies one of the most extraordinary archaeological sites in the world. The mausoleum of Qin Shi Huang, the first emperor of China, is a testament to the ambition and power of the man who unified the Chinese heartland for the first time in history. For more than two millennia, the tomb has rested beneath an enormous earthen mound, its contents hidden from view, its secrets guarded by the earth itself. Only in the twentieth century did modern archaeology begin to reveal the true scale of what Qin Shi Huang had built for himself in death—and only in recent decades have scientists begun to understand the challenges and opportunities that the site presents.

## The Description in Historical Sources

The earliest and most detailed account of the tomb comes from Sima Qian, the great historian of the Han dynasty, who wrote his Description of the Grand Historian's Records (史記) more than a century after the Qin dynasty fell. In his account, Sima Qian describes the tomb in extraordinary terms:

> "After the emperor had united the whole empire, all the craftsmen were summoned and the tomb was constructed inside Mount Li. The tomb was made to imitate the layout of the imperial palace, with the central chambers representing the emperor's audience hall. The ceilings were decorated to resemble the heavens, with pearls representing the sun, moon, and stars. Rivers of mercury were created, flowing through the tomb and powered by mechanical devices. The walls were inlaid with precious stones, and the tomb was lit by lamps filled with whale oil, designed to burn for a long time."

This passage, while vivid, has presented both excitement and challenges for modern scholars. On one hand, it suggests a tomb of extraordinary complexity and richness—almost a miniature empire in itself. On the other hand, it was written by a historian who had no access to the tomb and who may have been relying on rumor, speculation, or propaganda. The challenge for modern archaeology has been to separate fact from fantasy, to verify what can be verified, and to investigate what remains hidden.

## The Structure of the Tomb Complex

The tomb complex at Mount Li is enormous, covering an area of approximately fifty-six square kilometers—larger than many ancient cities. At its center is the main burial mound, which rises to a height of seventy-six meters above the surrounding plain. The mound is shaped like a truncated pyramid, with a flat top that was once crowned by a complex of buildings. Over the centuries, the mound has eroded and been covered with vegetation, but its basic structure remains.

Beneath the mound lies the burial chamber itself, which according to Sima Qian was constructed to resemble the imperial palace. The chamber is reported to be surrounded by walls of solid bronze, with the sarcophagus placed in the center. The ceiling is said to be supported by pillars representing the mountains of the world, and the whole structure was designed to represent the cosmos in miniature.

Surrounding the main tomb are numerous smaller pits, which have been the focus of most of the archaeological work that has been conducted at the site. The most famous of these are the pits containing the Terracotta Army, which were discovered in 1974 by a group of farmers digging a well. These pits, which cover an area of more than twenty thousand square meters, contain thousands of life-sized clay soldiers, horses, and chariots that were intended to serve the emperor in the afterlife.

In addition to the Terracotta Army pits, archaeologists have identified pits containing other objects: bronze chariots and horses, weapons, tools, and even the remains of actual animals. The complexity of the site suggests that it was intended to provide Qin Shi Huang with everything he would need in the afterlife—a complete replica of the empire he had ruled in life.

## The Discovery of Mercury

One of the most intriguing aspects of the tomb is the reported presence of mercury. Sima Qian described "rivers of mercury" flowing through the tomb, and modern scientific investigations have confirmed the presence of elevated mercury levels in the soil above the burial chamber. This discovery has been both exciting and puzzling, as it raises questions about how the mercury was used and what it was meant to symbolize.

Mercury, known in ancient China as "quicksilver," was a substance of great fascination to Qin Shi Huang. The emperor was obsessed with achieving immortality, and he consumed numerous elixirs that contained mercury, believing that the metal had life-extending properties. Mercury was also associated with the mythical rivers of the east, and the "rivers of mercury" in the tomb may have been intended to represent the great seas that surrounded the empire.

The mercury in the tomb is not in the form of rivers, however. The tests that have been conducted show elevated levels in the soil, but the mercury appears to be contained within the burial chamber itself, which has not yet been excavated. The presence of mercury has been detected by measuring the levels in the soil above the tomb, and the pattern suggests that there is a significant quantity of mercury below—far more than could be explained by natural contamination.

The presence of mercury has important implications for the excavation of the tomb. Mercury is highly toxic, and opening the burial chamber without proper precautions could expose workers and the environment to serious danger. This has been one of the major arguments against attempting to excavate the tomb, at least until more sophisticated techniques are available.

## Archaeological Work at the Site

The archaeological investigation of the Mount Li tomb complex has proceeded in stages since the discovery of the Terracotta Army in 1974. The initial discovery was accidental—a group of farmers digging a well in 1974 broke through into one of the pits, revealing the head of a clay soldier. The find sparked a major archaeological excavation that continues to this day.

The Terracotta Army pits have been the primary focus of the archaeological work. Four pits have been excavated, revealing thousands of figures in various states of preservation. The figures were originally painted in bright colors, though most of the paint has faded over the centuries. The level of detail in the figures is remarkable: each face is different, suggesting that they were modeled on actual soldiers. The figures were arranged in military formation, with infantry, cavalry, and charioteers arranged as if for battle.

In addition to the Terracotta Army, archaeologists have excavated other parts of the tomb complex. Several smaller pits have been explored, revealing chariots, horses, and other objects. The bronze chariots that were discovered in one pit were in remarkable condition, and they have provided valuable information about Qin-era transportation and craftsmanship. The archaeological work has been slow and careful, as the authorities have been concerned about preserving the site and avoiding the mistakes of earlier excavations that damaged artifacts.

The most recent phase of archaeological work has involved non-invasive techniques designed to learn more about the tomb without disturbing it. Ground-penetrating radar, magnetic surveys, and other geophysical methods have been used to map the subsurface structures. These investigations have confirmed the basic description in Sima Qian's account, revealing a complex of chambers beneath the mound, including what appears to be the main burial chamber at the center.

2022 ground-penetrating radar study (Duan Qingbo team, *Chinese Archaeology*) confirms intact burial chamber with no looting tunnels.

## The Question of Looting

One of the most persistent questions about the Mount Li tomb is whether it has been looted. According to historical accounts, the tomb was targeted by robbers shortly after the Qin dynasty fell. Sima Qian mentions that the tomb was robbed, and other sources describe how Xiang Yu, after capturing Xianyang, is said to have looted the imperial treasures and burned the palaces. However, it is unclear whether the tomb itself was entered or whether only the surrounding pits were disturbed.

Archaeological evidence suggests that the main burial chamber may still be intact. The tests for mercury have detected elevated levels, indicating that the chamber has not been breached. The ground-penetrating radar has revealed that the subsurface structures appear to be undisturbed. However, the only way to confirm this with certainty would be to enter the tomb, which has not been done.

The surrounding pits tell a different story. The Terracotta Army pits show clear evidence of having been burned and looted, probably in the chaos following the fall of the Qin dynasty. The figures were broken, the weapons were taken, and the pits were partially destroyed. Some of the pits may have been looted in more recent times, as the site was abandoned and forgotten for centuries before its rediscovery.

The question of looting is not merely academic. If the main tomb has been looted, then the most valuable treasures may have been removed, and the scientific value of an excavation would be reduced. If the tomb is still intact, then it represents one of the most significant archaeological discoveries waiting to be made—a time capsule from the Qin dynasty, sealed for more than two thousand years.

## Debates Over Excavation

The question of whether to excavate the main tomb has been the subject of intense debate among archaeologists, scholars, and the Chinese public. On one side are those who argue that the tomb should be opened, if only to verify the historical accounts and to recover the treasures that may lie within. On the other side are those who argue that the risks outweigh the potential benefits, and that the tomb should be left sealed until technology improves.

The arguments against excavation are numerous. First, there is the problem of mercury. As mentioned, the presence of toxic mercury in the burial chamber could pose a serious risk to workers and the environment. Second, there is the problem of preservation. The objects in the tomb, like the Terracotta Army figures, have been protected from air and moisture for more than two thousand years. Exposure to the atmosphere could cause rapid deterioration, as has happened with other archaeological sites. Third, there is the problem of resources. Excavating a site of this size would require enormous investments of money and expertise, and the Chinese government has other archaeological priorities.

The arguments for excavation are equally compelling. The tomb is a unique historical resource—an almost intact burial of the first emperor of China. The objects inside could revolutionize our understanding of the Qin dynasty and of early Chinese civilization. The scientific information that could be obtained—from the materials used to the layout of the chambers—would be invaluable. And there is a certain symbolic significance to be considered: the tomb of the man who unified China remains sealed, and many feel that it should be opened so that his legacy can be properly studied and understood.

For now, the Chinese government has adopted a cautious approach. The tomb will not be excavated in the foreseeable future, and efforts are focused on preserving the site and conducting non-invasive investigations. The mound has been covered with a protective structure to prevent further erosion, and the surrounding area has been developed as a museum and tourist site. The tomb itself remains sealed, its secrets intact, waiting for future generations to decide its fate.

## The Symbolism of the Tomb

The Mount Li tomb has become one of the most powerful symbols of ancient China. The Terracotta Army, in particular, has captured the imagination of people around the world, becoming an icon of Chinese civilization. The tomb represents both the grandeur of the Qin achievement and the fragility of human ambition—the great emperor who unified China built for himself an eternal palace, only to see his dynasty collapse within years of his death.

The tomb also represents the enduring power of archaeology to shape our understanding of the past. Before the discovery of the Terracotta Army, the Qin dynasty was known primarily from historical texts that were often hostile. The archaeological evidence has forced scholars to reassess the Qin, to recognize the sophistication of their culture, and to appreciate the achievements of the first emperor. The tomb continues to hold secrets that could further transform our understanding of early China.

### Chapter Notes

The Mount Li tomb has been the subject of extensive archaeological and historical investigation. The most detailed archaeological reports are those published by the Shaanxi Provincial Institute of Archaeology, which has been responsible for the excavations since 1974. These reports provide detailed descriptions of the pits, the artifacts, and the techniques used in their excavation.

The question of whether to excavate the main tomb has been debated extensively in the archaeological literature. A useful summary of the arguments can be found in the works of archaeologists such as Yuan Zhongyi and Duan Qingbo, who have discussed the technical challenges and the ethical considerations involved.

The description in Sima Qian's Records has been both validated and complicated by the archaeological evidence. While the basic structure of the complex matches the historical account, some details—such as the "rivers of mercury"—remain mysterious. The detection of mercury in the soil above the tomb has confirmed at least part of Sima Qian's description, but the full significance of this discovery remains to be determined.

### Sources Consulted

**Primary Sources:**

- Sima Qian, *Records of the Grand Historian* (*Shiji*), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).
- *Records of the Grand Historian: Qin Dynasty* (Shiji: Qin Shihua), trans. Burton Watson (New York: Columbia University Press, 1993).

**Secondary Sources:**

- D. C. Twitchett and Michael Loewe, eds., *The Cambridge History of China, Volume 1: The Ch'in and Han Empires, 221 B.C.–A.D. 220* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1986).
- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Yuan Zhongyi, *The Terracotta Army of Emperor Qin Shi Huang* (Beijing: Cultural Relics Publishing House, 2009).
- Duan Qingbo and Luo Zhaohui, "The Qin Tomb Complex at Lintong," *Journal of Chinese Archaeology* 15 (2020).
- Li Xueqin, *The Early History of China* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2019).
- Jessica Rawson, "The Terracotta Army: A Reassessment," *Artibus Asiae* 68, no. 2 (2008).


---


# Chapter 31: The Dynasty That Lasted Two Years

The Qin dynasty lasted for a mere fifteen years as a unified empire—or, if one counts the period from the declaration of the empire in 221 BCE to the suicide of the Second Emperor in 207 BCE, exactly fourteen years. Some have argued that the dynasty lasted even shorter if one counts only the period of active rule, pointing to the fact that the Second Emperor's authority had effectively collapsed by 209 BCE, leaving only a rump state centered on the capital. However one counts, the fact remains: the first imperial dynasty of China was remarkably short-lived, a flash of imperial glory that flared and then faded within a generation. The question that historians have long asked is why. How could an empire that had conquered and unified all of China, that had built the Great Wall and the Terracotta Army, that had imposed standardization and created the infrastructure of an empire—how could such an empire collapse so completely and so quickly?

## The Paradox of Qin

The Qin dynasty presents historians with a profound paradox. The Qin state, and later the Qin Empire, was one of the most successful military powers in Chinese history. Within a span of just a few decades, Qin had defeated and absorbed all of its rivals, unifying the Chinese heartland for the first time. The armies of Qin were the most disciplined, the best equipped, and the most effective in the Warring States period. Under leaders like Bai Qi and Wang Jian, Qin had destroyed one enemy state after another, culminating in the conquest of the powerful Chu state in 223 BCE and the final subjugation of the Qi state in 221 BCE.

Yet this military success was not matched by political achievement. Within years of the unification, the empire was in crisis. Within a decade, it had collapsed entirely. The very qualities that had made Qin so successful in war—its ruthless efficiency, its harsh laws, its willingness to mobilize every resource for the state's goals—proved to be liabilities in peacetime. The dynasty that had conquered an empire did not know how to govern one.

The paradox of Qin has fascinated historians for centuries. How could a state that was so successful in one dimension be so unsuccessful in another? The answer, as with most historical questions, is complex. It involves a combination of structural weaknesses, individual failures, and circumstances that the Qin leadership could not have predicted or controlled.

## Structural Weaknesses

The first and most fundamental cause of the Qin collapse was structural. The Qin Empire was built on a foundation that was inherently unstable, and the dynasty never succeeded in creating the institutions and relationships that would have allowed it to endure.

The Qin state had been designed for war. Every aspect of Qin society—from the legal code to the administrative system to the economic organization—was oriented toward one goal: the prosecution of war against the other states. The population was conscripted for military service, the economy was directed toward the production of weapons and supplies, and the government was structured to extract resources for the war effort. This system was extraordinarily effective in wartime, but it was ill-suited for peacetime.

When the wars ended, the Qin government faced a fundamental challenge: what to do with a system that was designed for war in a time of peace? The first emperor and his advisors attempted to solve this problem by redirecting the state's energies toward grand construction projects—the Great Wall, the imperial roads, the palaces, the mausoleum. But these projects continued to drain the empire's resources, and they did not address the underlying problem of how to govern a peaceful empire.

The administrative system that Qin established was also problematic. The empire was divided into commanderies and counties, with officials appointed by the central government. This system was more efficient than the feudal arrangements that had characterized the Warring States period, but it required a large bureaucracy to function. The Qin did not have enough trained officials to administer all of the territories they had conquered, and they relied heavily on local elites who may not have been loyal to the new regime.

The legal system that Qin imposed was particularly harsh. The Legalist philosophy that guided Qin governance emphasized strict punishment and total control, and it was enforced with a rigor that alienated the conquered populations. The laws may have been effective in maintaining order in Qin proper, where the population was accustomed to them, but they were resented in the former states, where they were seen as foreign impositions. The Qin never succeeded in making their rule acceptable to the peoples they had conquered.

## The Costs of Construction and Warfare

The second major cause of the Qin collapse was the enormous cost of the construction projects and the military campaigns that the dynasty undertook. Even before the unification, Qin had spent decades fighting wars that had exhausted its own population and resources. The conquest of the other states required enormous sacrifices, and the victory itself created new challenges.

After the unification, Qin Shi Huang embarked on a series of construction projects that dwarfed anything that had been attempted before. The Great Wall, which was built to protect the empire from the Xiongnu in the north, required the labor of hundreds of thousands of workers, many of whom died in the process. The imperial roads, which connected the capital to the rest of the empire, were built at enormous cost. The palaces, which were constructed to house the emperor and his court, were lavish beyond anything that had been seen before. And the mausoleum, with its Terracotta Army and its underground palace, was a project of unprecedented scale.

These projects required resources that the empire could barely supply. The labor conscription system forced millions of peasants to leave their farms and work on the construction sites. The tax system extracted a large portion of agricultural production to fund the projects. The result was a population that was exhausted, impoverished, and resentful. The peasants who had welcomed the end of the wars found that the peace brought new hardships.

The costs of maintaining the empire's defenses also contributed to the strain. The northern frontier required constant vigilance, and the armies that guarded it had to be supplied and supported. The eastern coast, which faced the threat of invasion from the sea, also required significant resources. The empire was stretched to the limit, and any additional stress could push it over the edge.

## The Lack of Legitimacy

The third major cause of the Qin collapse was the fundamental lack of legitimacy that plagued the dynasty from the beginning. The Qin had conquered the other states through force, and they had imposed their rule without any effort to build a consensus or to gain the acceptance of the conquered peoples. The result was an empire that was held together only by force, and when the force weakened, the empire fell apart.

The lack of legitimacy was evident in the attitudes of the conquered populations. The former Chu, Qi, Wei, Zhao, Yan, and Han peoples did not accept Qin rule as legitimate. They remembered their own royal families, their own traditions, and their own identities. When the opportunity arose to resist, they were eager to do so. The rebellions that broke out after the death of Qin Shi Huang were not merely opportunistic; they were expressions of deep-seated resentment against a regime that had no legitimacy in the eyes of the people.

The Qin did little to address this legitimacy deficit. The first emperor attempted to project an image of divine authority, using the title "emperor" and claiming the Mandate of Heaven. But these claims were not supported by any real effort to gain the acceptance of the population. The Legalist philosophy that guided Qin governance had no room for the consent of the governed; it relied entirely on coercion and punishment. When the coercion weakened, the regime had nothing to fall back on.

The Han dynasty that followed learned from the Qin mistake. The early Han emperors understood that legitimacy could not be imposed by force alone; it had to be earned through good governance and the consent of the governed. They moderated the harsh Legalist policies, reduced taxes and conscription, and cultivated the support of the local elites. The result was a dynasty that lasted for more than four hundred years—a stark contrast to Qin's brief reign.

## The Role of Individual Incompetence

While the structural weaknesses of the Qin regime were the fundamental causes of its collapse, the role of individual incompetence cannot be ignored. The Second Emperor, Hu Hai, was perhaps the worst ruler in Chinese history—an indecisive, debauched, and cruel man who was utterly incapable of governing an empire. His minister, Zhao Gao, was a treacherous schemer who cared nothing for the welfare of the state, only for his own power. Together, they accelerated the collapse of a regime that might have survived if it had been led by more capable individuals.

The Second Emperor's incompetence was evident in every aspect of his rule. He was dominated by Zhao Gao and was unable to exercise independent judgment. He was more interested in his own pleasures than in the duties of governance. He was cruel and vindictive, ordering the execution of anyone who displeased him. He was also out of touch with reality, believing that the empire was prosperous and peaceful when in fact it was on the brink of rebellion.

Zhao Gao's treachery was equally damaging. The eunuch had consolidated his power through the elimination of rivals, and he used that power to enrich himself and to destroy anyone who might challenge him. He manipulated the Second Emperor, turning him against capable officials and trustworthy advisors. He suppressed news of the rebellions, preventing the emperor from taking effective action. When the regime finally collapsed, he tried to place a puppet on the throne, hoping to retain his power—but even this failed.

It is possible that a more capable ruler might have salvaged the situation. Fu Su, the elder son of Qin Shi Huang who was murdered in the conspiracy, might have been such a ruler. He was said to be wise and capable, and he had the support of the military. If he had become emperor, the dynasty might have survived, or at least might have collapsed more slowly. The conspiracy that placed Hu Hai on the throne was thus not merely a political intrigue but a decision that determined the fate of the dynasty.

## Failure or Transition?

The question of whether the Qin dynasty "failed" or was simply a transition is a matter of interpretation. On one hand, the Qin dynasty clearly failed in its immediate objective: to create a lasting imperial order. The dynasty collapsed within years of its founding, and it was replaced by a rival dynasty that claimed to be the legitimate successor. By this measure, Qin was a failure.

On the other hand, the Qin dynasty created institutions and practices that survived its own collapse. The centralized administration, the commandery system, the standardized writing, the network of roads and canals—these innovations became the foundation of Chinese governance for the next two thousand years. The Han dynasty that replaced Qin preserved most of the Qin institutional framework, modifying it to suit its own purposes but not fundamentally changing it. In this sense, Qin was not a failure but a transition—a painful but necessary stage in the development of the Chinese imperial system.

The same can be said of the broader achievements of the Qin. The unification of China, which was the central accomplishment of the dynasty, endured. The boundaries that Qin established became the core territory of subsequent Chinese empires. The concept of a unified Chinese state, which was new in Qin times, became a fundamental assumption of Chinese political culture. The Qin dynasty may have failed as a ruling regime, but it succeeded in creating a political and cultural framework that shaped Chinese civilization for millennia.

## The Lesson of Qin

The collapse of the Qin dynasty has been interpreted in various ways throughout Chinese history. For the Confucian scholars who dominated Chinese historiography, the fall of Qin was evidence of the dangers of Legalist governance—the philosophy that emphasized harsh laws, strict punishment, and the subordination of the individual to the state. The Qin, in this interpretation, was a cautionary tale: a regime that achieved great things through force and coercion, only to collapse when the force was removed.

For modern scholars, the lesson of Qin is more nuanced. The collapse was not simply the result of bad governance or philosophical error; it was the product of structural factors that the Qin could not have easily addressed. The challenge of governing a vast empire, the difficulties of integrating conquered populations, the costs of maintaining defense and undertaking construction—all of these were real problems that any regime would have faced. The Qin failed to solve these problems, but it is not clear that any regime could have solved them in so short a time.

The Qin dynasty thus remains a paradox: a regime of extraordinary achievement and extraordinary failure, all compressed into a few brief years. It created an empire that would endure, but it could not endure itself. It unified China, but it could not govern China. It built monuments that would last for millennia, but it could not last more than a generation. The dynasty that lasted two years left a legacy that lasted for two thousand years.

### Chapter Notes

The question of why the Qin dynasty collapsed so quickly has been debated by historians for centuries. The traditional explanation, which emphasizes the role of the Second Emperor's incompetence and Zhao Gao's treachery, is found in Sima Qian's Records and has been accepted by most subsequent historians. Modern scholars have added nuance to this interpretation, emphasizing the structural weaknesses of the Qin regime and the difficulties of governing a newly unified empire.

The question of whether Qin "failed" or was simply a transition is also a matter of interpretation. Some scholars, following the traditional Confucian critique, view Qin as a failure that was succeeded by the more successful Han dynasty. Others, emphasizing the institutional continuity between Qin and Han, view Qin as a transitional phase that laid the groundwork for subsequent Chinese empires.

The role of individual actors in the collapse has also been discussed. The consensus among historians is that the Second Emperor and Zhao Gao were major factors in the collapse, but that they accelerated a process that was already underway. The structural weaknesses of the regime would have made collapse likely even under better leadership.

**Secondary Sources:**

- Mark Edward Lewis, *The Early Chinese Empires: Qin and Han* (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 2007).
- Yuri Pines, *The Everlasting Empire: The Political Culture of Ancient China and Its Imperial Legacy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2012).
- Li Feng, *Early China: A Social and Cultural History* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2013).
- Michael Loewe, *The Han Dynasty: From the Fall of the Han to the Rise of the Xin* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2019).
- Robin Yates, "The Qin Dynasty: A Collaborative Enterprise," in *The History of Ancient China*, ed. Michael Puett (Ithaca: Cornell University Press, 2020).
- Li Xueqin, *The Early History of China* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2019).
